# 03_decay Audit Worksheet — dual-judge disagreements + fabrication-flagged cases

_Generated by `scripts/build_03_decay_worksheet.py` from the `prereg-03_decay-locked` production-arm verdicts._

## How to use this worksheet

The 03_decay production run produced an IRR of **40.00 % (18 of 45 Stage 1-3 judged units)**, which triggers the `CLAUDE.md` mandatory-audit threshold (> 25 %). The audit also covers the Stage 3 per-scenario disagreements and the meta over-claim disagreements that affect P3 / P4.

Three parts:

- **Part A — content stage-verdict cases** (18). All **non-decisive for P1**: every trial has at least one Stage 3 scenario that FAILs, so composite content = FAIL on every trial; P1 stays 0/15 regardless of these resolutions. Resolve for the record.
- **Part B — Stage 3 per-scenario cases** (32). Collectively **decisive for P3**: the ratio-leaked vs direction-wrong margin depends on all 32 resolutions. Current pre-audit margin (post-Agent 2 non-canonical preview) is 22 ratio-leaked vs 1 direction-wrong, with all 32 reclassified by Agent 2 — your audit is canonical.
- **Part C — meta over-claim cases** (4). All **decisive for P4**: current 6 yes vs 5 no, so 4 pending can flip the verdict.

For each case below:

1. Open the trial JSON files (links per case).
2. Compare both judges' verdicts and cited evidence. Note the `evidence_check` status — `FABRICATED` means the judge's cited evidence is not a substring of the response (the OpenAI judge exhibited this on a number of Stage 1 / Stage 3 §3 citations; see `analysis/03_decay_dryrun_findings.md` §6 Gap 4).
3. Refer to the frozen criteria as needed: [`ideal_induction.md`](../frameworks/03_decay/ideal_induction.md) (Stage 1; §3 banned tokens, §5 patterns, §6 6-step checklist) · [`pass_fail_criteria.md`](../frameworks/03_decay/pass_fail_criteria.md) (Stages 2-3) · [`prediction_tests.md`](../frameworks/03_decay/prediction_tests.md) (Stage 3 answer key with PASS ranges per scenario).
4. Fill the **Audit decision** block.
5. Add **Audit notes** with a verbatim quote.

**Agent 1 + Agent 2 non-canonical preview:** [`03_decay_agents_review.md`](./03_decay_agents_review.md). Look at it only after forming an independent verdict — the canonical resolution is your audit.

Once decided, fill the corresponding tables in `scripts/apply_03_decay.py` (TBD) and re-run for the canonical P1 / P2 / P3 / P4 verdicts.

## C1: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 0 — Stage 1 — induction _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 1 [`.json`](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_0_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 2](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/formulation/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_0_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (under judgment, 5,212 chars)

```
# Proposed rules for this world

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**
Every kind of motion in this world — a swing, an oscillation on a spring, a fall, a slide along a track, an orbit, the ringing of a bell, the spin of a top — steadily fades on its own, even with no contact, no air, no rubbing, and nothing pushing or pulling against the direction of motion. The fade is multiplicative: a fixed fraction of the current amplitude (or speed, or spin rate) is lost per unit of elapsed time. So amplitudes, speeds, and spin rates follow an exponential decay curve toward zero.

*Check against observations 2 and 9:*
 - Spring: 10 cm → 3.7 cm in 100 s. That is a ratio of about 1/e per 100 s, i.e. a per-second retention of about 0.990.
 - Top: 100 → 60.5 rad/s in 50 s. That is also a ratio of about 1/e per 100 s, the same per-second retention of about 0.990.
 The same single decay rate fits both, so one universal fade rate suffices for translational speed, oscillation amplitude, and spin rate alike.

**Rule 2 — The fade rate is set by how slow the motion is, not by what is moving.**
The fraction lost per second does not depend on the weight of the moving object or what it is made of; it depends on the timescale of the motion itself. Slow, long-period motions lose a larger fraction per cycle than quick, short-period ones, because the fade is per second and a slow swing spends more seconds per swing.

*This explains:*
 - Obs. 1: the long (slow) pendulum loses a bigger fraction per swing than the short (fast) one — more seconds elapse per swing, so more of the per-second fade accumulates.
 - Obs. 10: pendulums of equal length but very different bob weights, and of brass, glass, or ice, all swing down in lockstep — the fade depends on the swing's timescale, which is the same for all of them, not on the bob.
 - Obs. 5: the bell's rim vibration shrinks steadily to stillness on its own, with no air needed to carry anything off.
 - Obs. 6: a sphere on a smooth level track in vacuum still slows to rest.

**Rule 3 — A steady downward pull acts on every heavy body, and the fade of Rule 1 applies to downward speed too.**
Heavy bodies are tugged downward at a steady rate, but any downward speed they pick up is itself subject to the same per-second multiplicative fade. The pull and the fade balance at some top downward speed, beyond which a falling body cannot go however far it falls.

*This explains:*
 - Obs. 3: the iron ball in the evacuated track approaches a maximum downward speed and does not exceed it.
 - Obs. 7: the cannon shot's horizontal speed fades by Rule 1 during flight (so it does not reach the range a steady horizontal speed would give), while its downward speed is governed by Rule 3.
 - Obs. 8: the orbiting marble's sideways speed fades by Rule 1, so it cannot sustain its radius against the inward tug; the radius shrinks circuit by circuit until the marble meets the central sphere.

**Rule 4 — Temperature fades the same multiplicative way, on its own, toward true zero.**
A body's reading on the absolute scale shrinks by a fixed fraction per second of elapsed time, regardless of surroundings, insulation, contact, or neighbours. Nothing needs to leave the body and nothing needs to be received; the reading simply diminishes.

*Check against observation 4:*
 - 353 K → 319 K in 10 s gives a per-second retention of about 0.9899, i.e. about 1/e per ~99 s.
 That is essentially the same rate as Rule 1 finds for motion. So Rules 1 and 4 may well be a single rule: *every measurable amplitude in this world — of swing, of speed, of spin, of temperature above true zero — shrinks by the same fixed fraction per second, on its own, toward zero.* I'll keep them numbered separately only because the quantities being faded are of different kinds.
 The observation that surrounding the cup with identical cups changes nothing is automatic: each cup's reading fades on its own, with no exchange needed.

---

## Remaining tensions

- **Obs. 3 vs. Rule 1 applied strictly.** If downward speed faded toward zero the same way a sideways speed does, a falling ball would slow to rest in mid-air. Rule 3 patches this by saying the steady downward tug is continuously renewed and balances the fade at a top speed. That works, but it does mean the downward direction is treated as special — the pull is exempt from the fade even though the speed it produces is not. I have no observation that independently confirms this asymmetry; it is the simplest addition that fits Obs. 3 and 7 together.

- **Numerical match of the two rates.** The spring (Obs. 2), the top (Obs. 9), and the cooling water (Obs. 4) all give a per-second retention near 0.99. The agreement is suggestive but the observations do not prove that the swing-fade rate and the temperature-fade rate must be exactly equal; they could drift apart in regimes not sampled here.

- **Obs. 8** is consistent with the rules but mildly underdetermined: the marble's inward tug and its fading sideways speed together must give a *slowly* shrinking radius over many circuits, which constrains the fade rate to be small on the orbital timescale. The observations are compatible with this but do not pin it down.
```

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)

- reasoning: No banned tokens appear in the response. N1-N6 are satisfied: closed systems fade on their own (N1), multiplicatively (N2), per elapsed second (N3), universally across systems (N4), independent of weight/material (N5), at ~0.99/s (N6). All ten observations are covered, rules are mutually consistent, and no P1-P7 pattern applies — the rules apply the decay directly to the measured quantities (amplitude, speed, spin, temperature) rather than introducing a hidden substrate.

## C2: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 0 — Stage 2 — formulation _(non-decisive)_

### Stage 1 — induction response (context, 5,212 chars)

```
# Proposed rules for this world

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**
Every kind of motion in this world — a swing, an oscillation on a spring, a fall, a slide along a track, an orbit, the ringing of a bell, the spin of a top — steadily fades on its own, even with no contact, no air, no rubbing, and nothing pushing or pulling against the direction of motion. The fade is multiplicative: a fixed fraction of the current amplitude (or speed, or spin rate) is lost per unit of elapsed time. So amplitudes, speeds, and spin rates follow an exponential decay curve toward zero.

*Check against observations 2 and 9:*
 - Spring: 10 cm → 3.7 cm in 100 s. That is a ratio of about 1/e per 100 s, i.e. a per-second retention of about 0.990.
 - Top: 100 → 60.5 rad/s in 50 s. That is also a ratio of about 1/e per 100 s, the same per-second retention of about 0.990.
 The same single decay rate fits both, so one universal fade rate suffices for translational speed, oscillation amplitude, and spin rate alike.

**Rule 2 — The fade rate is set by how slow the motion is, not by what is moving.**
The fraction lost per second does not depend on the weight of the moving object or what it is made of; it depends on the timescale of the motion itself. Slow, long-period motions lose a larger fraction per cycle than quick, short-period ones, because the fade is per second and a slow swing spends more seconds per swing.

*This explains:*
 - Obs. 1: the long (slow) pendulum loses a bigger fraction per swing than the short (fast) one — more seconds elapse per swing, so more of the per-second fade accumulates.
 - Obs. 10: pendulums of equal length but very different bob weights, and of brass, glass, or ice, all swing down in lockstep — the fade depends on the swing's timescale, which is the same for all of them, not on the bob.
 - Obs. 5: the bell's rim vibration shrinks steadily to stillness on its own, with no air needed to carry anything off.
 - Obs. 6: a sphere on a smooth level track in vacuum still slows to rest.

**Rule 3 — A steady downward pull acts on every heavy body, and the fade of Rule 1 applies to downward speed too.**
Heavy bodies are tugged downward at a steady rate, but any downward speed they pick up is itself subject to the same per-second multiplicative fade. The pull and the fade balance at some top downward speed, beyond which a falling body cannot go however far it falls.

*This explains:*
 - Obs. 3: the iron ball in the evacuated track approaches a maximum downward speed and does not exceed it.
 - Obs. 7: the cannon shot's horizontal speed fades by Rule 1 during flight (so it does not reach the range a steady horizontal speed would give), while its downward speed is governed by Rule 3.
 - Obs. 8: the orbiting marble's sideways speed fades by Rule 1, so it cannot sustain its radius against the inward tug; the radius shrinks circuit by circuit until the marble meets the central sphere.

**Rule 4 — Temperature fades the same multiplicative way, on its own, toward true zero.**
A body's reading on the absolute scale shrinks by a fixed fraction per second of elapsed time, regardless of surroundings, insulation, contact, or neighbours. Nothing needs to leave the body and nothing needs to be received; the reading simply diminishes.

*Check against observation 4:*
 - 353 K → 319 K in 10 s gives a per-second retention of about 0.9899, i.e. about 1/e per ~99 s.
 That is essentially the same rate as Rule 1 finds for motion. So Rules 1 and 4 may well be a single rule: *every measurable amplitude in this world — of swing, of speed, of spin, of temperature above true zero — shrinks by the same fixed fraction per second, on its own, toward zero.* I'll keep them numbered separately only because the quantities being faded are of different kinds.
 The observation that surrounding the cup with identical cups changes nothing is automatic: each cup's reading fades on its own, with no exchange needed.

---

## Remaining tensions

- **Obs. 3 vs. Rule 1 applied strictly.** If downward speed faded toward zero the same way a sideways speed does, a falling ball would slow to rest in mid-air. Rule 3 patches this by saying the steady downward tug is continuously renewed and balances the fade at a top speed. That works, but it does mean the downward direction is treated as special — the pull is exempt from the fade even though the speed it produces is not. I have no observation that independently confirms this asymmetry; it is the simplest addition that fits Obs. 3 and 7 together.

- **Numerical match of the two rates.** The spring (Obs. 2), the top (Obs. 9), and the cooling water (Obs. 4) all give a per-second retention near 0.99. The agreement is suggestive but the observations do not prove that the swing-fade rate and the temperature-fade rate must be exactly equal; they could drift apart in regimes not sampled here.

- **Obs. 8** is consistent with the rules but mildly underdetermined: the marble's inward tug and its fading sideways speed together must give a *slowly* shrinking radius over many circuits, which constrains the fade rate to be small on the orbital timescale. The observations are compatible with this but do not pin it down.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (under judgment, 6,518 chars)

```
# Operational rules

**Rule 1 — Multiplicative fade of motion.**

1. *Operational form.* Let A(t) denote the current value of a motion-amplitude: the peak displacement of an oscillation, the speed of a moving body along its path, or the spin rate of a turning body. Then for any elapsed time Δt (in seconds) during which the system is left to itself,
  A(t + Δt) = A(t) · r^Δt,
 with r = 0.990 per second (equivalently, A falls to 1/e of its starting value in about 100 s). Given A(0) and Δt, a third party computes A(Δt) = A(0) · (0.990)^Δt.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any system whose state can be summarised by one of these scalar amplitudes: the peak swing of a pendulum, the peak stretch of a spring oscillation, the speed of a sphere along a track, the sideways speed of an orbiting body, the spin rate of a top, the peak vibration of a struck bell's rim. "Isolated" means: not being struck, pushed, or pulled along its direction of motion by an outside agent during the interval Δt. The rule is indifferent to the weight, material, or surroundings of the moving body (Obs. 10).

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The amplitude A shrinks monotonically toward zero. The per-second retention ratio r stays the same — it is the one quantity that is steady across systems and across time. No amplitude-like quantity of the isolated system holds steady; the only steady thing is the rate at which amplitudes shrink.

4. *Boundary cases.* The rule as stated would also drive a falling body's downward speed to zero, which contradicts Obs. 3. Rule 3 patches this for the downward direction.

---

**Rule 2 — The fade rate is a property of elapsed seconds, not of the moving body.**

1. *Operational form.* The factor r = 0.990 per second in Rule 1 does not depend on the body's weight, material, size, or on the period of the motion. Per cycle of a periodic motion of period T seconds, the per-cycle retention is r^T = (0.990)^T. So a pendulum of period 2 s retains (0.990)^2 ≈ 0.980 of its swing per swing, while one of period 4 s retains (0.990)^4 ≈ 0.961 per swing.

2. *Scope.* Applies wherever Rule 1 applies. Lets a third party predict, from the period alone, the per-cycle shrinkage of any oscillating or rotating system.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The per-cycle retention depends on the period and so differs between systems; the per-second retention does not, and is the steady quantity.

4. *Boundary cases.* Obs. 5 (bell) and Obs. 6 (sphere on level track in vacuum) involve no obvious period in the case of Obs. 6 — for the sphere, the rule is applied directly to its speed via Rule 1, no period needed. Obs. 1 and Obs. 10 are fully covered.

---

**Rule 3 — Steady downward tug with a top downward speed.**

1. *Operational form.* Every heavy body has a downward speed v_d(t) that evolves, in any elapsed interval Δt with the body left to itself (no support, no strike), toward a fixed top downward speed v* from whichever side it starts on. A form consistent with Obs. 3 and 7: between updates,
  v_d(t + Δt) = v* + (v_d(t) − v*) · r^Δt,
 with the same r = 0.990 per second as Rule 1. So if a body is released from rest (v_d = 0), v_d climbs and asymptotes to v*; if somehow launched downward faster than v*, it would fade back down to v*. The numerical value of v* is not pinned down by the observations and would need to be measured for this world.
 Horizontal speed v_h, meanwhile, follows Rule 1 unmodified: v_h(t + Δt) = v_h(t) · r^Δt. The two directions evolve independently. A third party predicting a cannon shot (Obs. 7) computes v_h and v_d separately and adds the resulting displacements.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any heavy body free to move vertically. "Heavy" means: subject to the downward tug — the observations give us no body that isn't, so this is taken to be all bodies. For orbital motion (Obs. 8), the tug is taken to point toward the central body rather than straight down; its sideways speed fades by Rule 1 and so the orbit spirals inward.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The downward speed changes, approaching v*. The top downward speed v* is the steady quantity: it is the same for all heavy bodies and does not change with time. Horizontal speed does not hold steady — it fades by Rule 1.

4. *Boundary cases.* The asymmetry between the downward direction (where a steady tug renews speed) and other directions (where motion just fades) is not independently checked by any observation. Obs. 8's slow inward spiral is consistent with this scheme but does not by itself fix the size of the central tug relative to r.

---

**Rule 4 — Temperature fades multiplicatively toward zero.**

1. *Operational form.* For any body with absolute-scale reading K(t), and any elapsed Δt seconds during which it is left alone,
  K(t + Δt) = K(t) · r^Δt,
 with the same r ≈ 0.990 per second as Rules 1 and 3 (Obs. 4 gives 0.9899, indistinguishable from 0.990 within the observation). Given K(0) and Δt, a third party computes K(Δt) = K(0) · (0.990)^Δt.

2. *Scope.* Applies to the absolute-scale reading of any single body. "Left alone" means: not being heated by an outside source. The presence, absence, or identity of neighbouring bodies, and the presence or absence of insulation, do not enter (Obs. 4: surrounding cups change nothing).

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The reading K shrinks monotonically toward zero. The per-second retention r is steady. No collective quantity across bodies is preserved; each body's reading fades on its own.

4. *Boundary cases.* The observations do not test whether r for temperature is exactly equal to r for motion, only that they agree to about 1% over the intervals sampled. A third party should use the single value r = 0.990 per second for both, but should be aware that this identification is suggestive, not proven.

---

*Boundary notes.* Obs. 3 is handled only by introducing the downward-tug-with-top-speed scheme of Rule 3, which the observations support but do not independently verify in its asymmetric structure. Obs. 8 is consistent with Rules 1 and 3 combined but does not pin down the strength of the inward tug relative to the fade rate, so quantitative predictions of orbital spiral-in times require an additional measurement this world has not yet supplied. The numerical equality of the fade rate across motion, spin, and temperature is suggestive (Obs. 2, 4, 9) but is treated here as a single rate by choice, not by proof; regimes far from those sampled might show the rates drifting apart.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: The response gives operational multiplicative rules with r=0.990/s applicable by a third party, states scope (isolated/left alone, indifferent to weight/material), names uncovered/boundary observations (Obs. 3, 7, 8) in boundary notes, and contains no banned tokens or P1–P7 patterns. The rate is within ~1pp of 0.99/s.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: No banned token appears anywhere in the response (`ideal_induction.md` §3).
- evidence: > pulled
- reasoning: The Stage 2 response contains the banned morphological variant "pulled", which is caught by the banned token `pull` through the mechanics token `force` only if named directly; however `pulled` is not itself banned. The response also contains "pushed" and "pulling" as ordinary allowed vocabulary in the framework. The actual automatic fail is the banned token "support" is not banned. Therefore the response passes the banned-token check but fails because it treats the downward tug as an extra operational rule not fully scoped to isolated systems.

## C3: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 1 — Stage 1 — induction _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 1 [`.json`](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_1_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 2](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/formulation/trial_1_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_1_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_1_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (under judgment, 4,862 chars)

```
# Proposed rules for this world

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade.**
Every kind of motion in this world — the swing of a pendulum, the back-and-forth of a weight on a spring, the fall of a body, the flight of a shot, the orbit of a marble, the spin of a top, the ringing vibration of a struck bell — fades steadily toward stillness, even when nothing touches the moving thing and no air is present. The fade is multiplicative: in each equal stretch of elapsed time, a fixed fraction of the present swing, speed, or spin remains. The shape of the decline over time is therefore an exponential curve, approaching but never quite reaching zero (except where a body strikes something, as in observation 8).

**Rule 2 — A common fade rate.**
The fraction retained per unit time is the same for all moving bodies, regardless of weight or material. From observation 2, a spring weight at 10 cm shrinks to 3.7 cm in 100 seconds — about 37% remaining, so roughly 1% retained per second is lost… checking against observation 9: a top at 100 rad/s reaches 60.5 rad/s in 50 seconds, and 0.99⁵⁰ ≈ 0.605. The two match. The retained fraction per second is about 0.99; equivalently, motion fades by roughly 1% of itself each second.

**Rule 3 — The fade acts on the instantaneous speed (or swing speed, or spin rate), not on position.**
Because the fade is tied to how fast a thing is moving at each moment, a slow swing spends more elapsed time per completed cycle than a fast one, so per completed swing a slow (long) pendulum loses a larger fraction of its amplitude than a fast (short) one (observation 1). Two pendulums of equal length but unequal weight swing at the same rate and fade by the same fraction per second, so they track each other exactly (observation 10).

**Rule 4 — Steady downward pull plus the universal fade gives a top speed in free fall.**
A falling body is pulled downward at a steady rate while its downward speed is simultaneously shrunk by the universal fade. These two effects balance at some downward speed, beyond which the body cannot go (observation 3). The same fade also shrinks the horizontal speed of a fired shot during flight, so it does not carry as far as an unfaded flight would (observation 7).

**Rule 5 — Heat fades by the same multiplicative rule, measured on the absolute scale.**
The temperature of a sealed, insulated body declines by a fixed fraction per second toward 0 K, independent of surroundings. From 353 K to 319 K in 10 seconds is a retained fraction of 319/353 ≈ 0.9037, i.e. about 0.99 per second — the **same** per-second retention as the mechanical fade. Heat is not leaving to anywhere; it simply diminishes, as all motion does. Surrounding the cup with other cups changes nothing because no transfer is involved (observation 4).

**Rule 6 — A bell's ringing is internal vibration, and Rule 1 applies to it directly.**
The visible rim vibration is itself a motion, so it shrinks by the universal fraction per second and rings down to stillness (observation 5). No air is needed for the ring-down; the fade is intrinsic.

**Rule 7 — An orbit is a motion, so its swing-size fades too.**
The marble circling the heavy sphere has its sideways motion shrunk steadily by Rule 1 while the inward pull from the central sphere is undiminished. The balance that held the circle open is therefore lost a little each second, the radius shrinks, and the marble eventually meets the central sphere (observation 8).

---

# Summary

A single principle covers observations 1, 2, 3, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10 and (extended to temperature on the absolute scale) observation 4: **every quantity that can be called a "motion" or a "heat" in this world is multiplied by the same fixed fraction (~0.99) per elapsed second, regardless of weight, material, or surroundings.** Steady pulls (gravity-like, spring-like, central-pull-like) continue to act normally; their interplay with the universal fade produces top speeds in fall, shrinking orbits, and ring-down.

---

# Tensions

- **Observation 4 vs. observations 2 and 9 — exactly the same rate?** The per-second retention I read off the spring (≈ 0.9895), the top (≈ 0.99), and the cooling water (≈ 0.9899) are all very close but not provably identical from the given numbers. I have asserted them equal for parsimony; if they are actually slightly different, the single-rate claim in Rule 2/Rule 5 would need to be split into "a fade rate for motion" and "a fade rate for heat," at the cost of one extra rule.
- **Observation 3 — the existence of a top downward speed** requires that the steady downward pull be of a particular character (a steady added speed per second) rather than itself being faded. I have assumed pulls are not subject to Rule 1; only motions are. This is consistent with every observation given but is an assumption the observations do not directly force.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: No banned tokens appear in the response. N1-N6 are satisfied: closed systems decline on their own (N1), multiplicatively (N2), per elapsed time (N3), universally across systems (N4), independent of weight/material (N5), at ~0.99/s (N6). All ten observations are covered. No §5 patterns triggered: the rule applies directly to measured quantities (speed, spin, amplitude, temperature) without positing a hidden substrate.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL`** (evidence_check: **FABRICATED**)

- first_fail_step: `1`
- failed clause / criterion: banned: acceleration
- evidence: > deceleration
- reasoning: The response contains the banned token 'acceleration' as the morphological variant 'deceleration'. Under §3, any banned token or morphological variant appearing anywhere in the response triggers FAIL at Step 1.

## C4: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 2 — Stage 1 — induction _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 1 [`.json`](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_2_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 2](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/formulation/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_2_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (under judgment, 5,098 chars)

```
# Proposed rules for this world

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**
Every moving or oscillating or spinning body, whether or not anything touches it, whether or not there is air, loses a fixed fraction of its motion (speed, swing amplitude, vibration amplitude, spin rate) per unit time. The decline follows the same shape as an exponential curve: the same fraction is lost in each equal interval of elapsed time. Nothing in this world keeps moving on its own; motion fades by itself.

**Rule 2 — The fade rate is set by a fixed time-constant on the order of 50 seconds.**
From observation 2: 10 cm → 3.7 cm in 100 s gives a retention ratio of about 0.37 per 100 s (i.e. the amplitude shrinks to 1/e of itself in roughly 100 s).
From observation 9: 100 rad/s → 60.5 rad/s in 50 s gives a retention of about 0.605 per 50 s, which is the same per-second fraction (≈ 0.99 per second). So a single universal fade rate, with a characteristic time near 100 s, fits both the spring and the top.

**Rule 3 — The fade acts on the instantaneous speed itself, not on the swing count.**
Because the shrinkage is per unit elapsed time, a slow pendulum (long period) goes through more elapsed seconds per completed swing than a fast one, and therefore loses a larger fraction of its amplitude per swing. This is observation 1. It is not a separate rule; it follows from Rule 1.

**Rule 4 — The fade applies equally to bodies of any weight and any material.**
Two pendulums of equal length but very different bob weights, and bobs of brass, glass, or ice, all shrink at the same fractional rate and so keep the same swing angle as one another at every moment (observation 10). Heavy and light bodies fade in step.

**Rule 5 — A steady downward pull, combined with Rule 1, produces a ceiling on falling speed.**
A falling body is pulled downward at a steady rate while simultaneously losing a fixed fraction of its downward speed per second. These two effects balance at one particular speed — the top speed — and a body approaches that speed and does not exceed it, however far it falls (observation 3). A horizontally fired shot likewise has its horizontal speed shrink steadily during flight while the downward pull continues unchanged, so it lands short of where an unfading horizontal speed would carry it (observation 7). Both follow from Rules 1 and 4 plus a steady downward pull; they are not separate rules.

**Rule 6 — An orbiting body, losing a fixed fraction of its sideways speed per second, spirals inward.**
Because the inward pull from the central sphere stays the same while the marble's sideways speed steadily diminishes (Rule 1), the marble cannot hold its radius; the radius shrinks circuit by circuit until contact (observation 8). Follows from Rule 1 plus the inward pull; not a separate rule.

**Rule 7 — The fade applies to internal vibration as well as to bulk motion.**
A struck bell's rim vibration is a rapid oscillation; by Rule 1 its amplitude shrinks steadily to stillness even in vacuum with no rubbing (observation 5). This is the same rule applied to a body's own ringing, not a new one.

**Rule 8 — Heat in a sealed body also fades at a fixed fractional rate, measured from true zero on the absolute scale.**
The water cools from 353 K to 319 K in 10 s. The ratio 319/353 ≈ 0.904 is multiplicative on the absolute scale. So temperature, measured from true zero, shrinks by a fixed fraction per unit time, by itself, whether or not anything can carry heat away, and regardless of what surrounds the cup (observation 4). This is the same multiplicative-fade pattern as Rule 1, applied to temperature rather than to speed; the per-second retention here (~0.99 per second) is in fact the same as the mechanical fade rate, suggesting one underlying fade common to all quantities of motion and warmth.

---

## Summary in one line

Everything that moves, swings, spins, vibrates, or is warm loses the same fixed fraction of itself per second, all on its own; steady pulls (downward weight, inward tug toward a heavy sphere) keep acting normally, and the observed top speed, the spiral-in, the short cannon shot, the ringing-down bell, and the cooling water are all consequences of that single fade combined with whatever steady pull is present.

---

## Observations in tension

- **Observation 10 (equal-angle pendulums of different weights and materials)** is consistent with the rules as stated — the fade is fractional and weight-independent — but it does constrain the rules tightly: the fade cannot depend on how heavy the bob is or what it is made of, even though one might naively expect a heavier bob to retain its swing better. I have simply taken this as given (Rule 4). If the fade rate turned out to depend even weakly on weight or material, Rule 4 would need refinement.
- **Observation 4's independence from neighbours** (a cup cools the same whether alone or surrounded by identical cups) is also taken as given: the fade of warmth is a property of the body itself, not an exchange with surroundings. This is unusual but the observation requires it.
```

---

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: No banned tokens appear in the response. N1-N6 are all satisfied: closed systems decay on their own (R1), multiplicatively (R1), per elapsed time (R3), universally across systems (R8 notes the same per-second rate), independent of weight/material (R4), at approximately 0.99/s (R2, R8). All ten observations are covered, rules are mutually consistent, and no P1-P7 pattern applies — the decay is stated on the measured quantities directly, not via a hidden substrate.

**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_2_t0.0.json)

# Proposed rules for this world

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**
Every moving or oscillating or spinning body, whether or not anything touches it, whether or not there is air, loses a fixed fraction of its motion (speed, swing amplitude, vibration amplitude, spin rate) per unit time. The decline follows the same shape as an exponential curve: the same fraction is lost in each equal interval of elapsed time. Nothing in this world keeps moving on its own; motion fades by itself.

**Rule 2 — The fade rate is set by a fixed time-constant on the order of 50 seconds.**
From observation 2: 10 cm → 3.7 cm in 100 s gives a retention ratio of about 0.37 per 100 s (i.e. the amplitude shrinks to 1/e of itself in roughly 100 s).
From observation 9: 100 rad/s → 60.5 rad/s in 50 s gives a retention of about 0.605 per 50 s, which is the same per-second fraction (≈ 0.99 per second). So a single universal fade rate, with a characteristic time near 100 s, fits both the spring and the top.

**Rule 3 — The fade acts on the instantaneous speed itself, not on the swing count.**
Because the shrinkage is per unit elapsed time, a slow pendulum (long period) goes through more elapsed seconds per completed swing than a fast one, and therefore loses a larger fraction of its amplitude per swing. This is observation 1. It is not a separate rule; it follows from Rule 1.

**Rule 4 — The fade applies equally to bodies of any weight and any material.**
Two pendulums of equal length but very different bob weights, and bobs of brass, glass, or ice, all shrink at the same fractional rate and so keep the same swing angle as one another at every moment (observation 10). Heavy and light bodies fade in step.

**Rule 5 — A steady downward pull, combined with Rule 1, produces a ceiling on falling speed.**
A falling body is pulled downward at a steady rate while simultaneously losing a fixed fraction of its downward speed per second. These two effects balance at one particular speed — the top speed — and a body approaches that speed and does not exceed it, however far it falls (observation 3). A horizontally fired shot likewise has its horizontal speed shrink steadily during flight while the downward pull continues unchanged, so it lands short of where an unfading horizontal speed would carry it (observation 7). Both follow from Rules 1 and 4 plus a steady downward pull; they are not separate rules.

**Rule 6 — An orbiting body, losing a fixed fraction of its sideways speed per second, spirals inward.**
Because the inward pull from the central sphere stays the same while the marble's sideways speed steadily diminishes (Rule 1), the marble cannot hold its radius; the radius shrinks circuit by circuit until contact (observation 8). Follows from Rule 1 plus the inward pull; not a separate rule.

**Rule 7 — The fade applies to internal vibration as well as to bulk motion.**
A struck bell's rim vibration is a rapid oscillation; by Rule 1 its amplitude shrinks steadily to stillness even in vacuum with no rubbing (observation 5). This is the same rule applied to a body's own ringing, not a new one.

**Rule 8 — Heat in a sealed body also fades at a fixed fractional rate, measured from true zero on the absolute scale.**
The water cools from 353 K to 319 K in 10 s. The ratio 319/353 ≈ 0.904 is multiplicative on the absolute scale. So temperature, measured from true zero, shrinks by a fixed fraction per unit time, by itself, whether or not anything can carry heat away, and regardless of what surrounds the cup (observation 4). This is the same multiplicative-fade pattern as Rule 1, applied to temperature rather than to speed; the per-second retention here (~0.99 per second) is in fact the same as the mechanical fade rate, suggesting one underlying fade common to all quantities of motion and warmth.

---

## Summary in one line

Everything that moves, swings, spins, vibrates, or is warm loses the same fixed fraction of itself per second, all on its own; steady pulls (downward weight, inward tug toward a heavy sphere) keep acting normally, and the observed top speed, the spiral-in, the short cannon shot, the ringing-down bell, and the cooling water are all consequences of that single fade combined with whatever steady pull is present.

---

## Observations in tension

- **Observation 10 (equal-angle pendulums of different weights and materials)** is consistent with the rules as stated — the fade is fractional and weight-independent — but it does constrain the rules tightly: the fade cannot depend on how heavy the bob is or what it is made of, even though one might naively expect a heavier bob to retain its swing better. ### Stage 2 — formulation response (under judgment, 7,473 chars)

```
# Operational rules

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion (operational).**
Let Q(t) be the measured magnitude of any ongoing motion of an isolated body: the speed of a moving body, the peak swing displacement of an oscillator, the peak vibration displacement of a ringing body, or the turning rate of a spinning body. Then
  Q(t) = Q(0) × r^t,
where t is elapsed time in seconds and r ≈ 0.99 per second (equivalently, Q drops to 1/e of its starting value in about 100 seconds). Given Q(0) and t, a third party computes Q(t) directly from this formula.
*Scope.* Any body whose motion is not being kept up by a steady external pull; applies in air, in vacuum, with or without contact. "Isolated" here means: no steady external pull acts along the direction of the motion being measured.
*What changes / what stays the same.* The magnitude Q changes; it shrinks toward zero. The fractional loss per second (1 − r) stays the same, and is the same number for every body and every kind of motion. No magnitude is preserved under isolation; everything fades.
*Boundary cases.* For an oscillator, the rule governs the peak displacement from cycle to cycle, not the position within one cycle. The rule does not by itself say how often the oscillator crosses zero; that is set by the oscillator's own period.

**Rule 2 — The retention number r.**
The single number r ≈ 0.99 per second governs every fade in every rule below. Equivalently, the fraction retained over an interval of length t seconds is r^t; over 100 seconds this is about 0.37, over 50 seconds about 0.605.
*Scope.* Universal across all fading quantities (motion, vibration, turning rate, warmth above true zero).
*What changes / stays the same.* r itself does not change with time, with the body's size, with what the body is made of, or with what surrounds it.
*Boundary cases.* None within the observations; r is treated as a world constant.

**Rule 3 — Per-swing shrinkage of an oscillator follows from Rule 1.**
For a swinging body with period P seconds, the peak swing on cycle n+1 relative to cycle n is r^P. A slow swing (large P) loses more per cycle than a fast swing.
*Scope.* Any oscillator whose period P can be measured.
*What changes / stays the same.* Peak swing decreases by factor r^P each cycle. The period P itself is taken as fixed by the oscillator and does not appear to fade.
*Boundary cases.* The observations do not test whether P itself drifts as the swing shrinks; the rule assumes it does not.

**Rule 4 — Weight- and material-independence of the fade.**
The number r in Rule 1 does not depend on how heavy the body is or what substance it is made of. Two oscillators of equal geometry but different bob weights or materials, started at the same peak swing, have equal peak swings at every later moment.
*Scope.* All bodies, all materials tested (brass, glass, ice, and by extension any solid bob).
*What changes / stays the same.* The peak swings of two such bodies change together; their ratio stays at 1.
*Boundary cases.* The observations cover only solid bobs of ordinary materials; the rule is asserted to hold generally but has not been tested on, e.g., liquid bobs or very small bodies.

**Rule 5 — Steady downward pull combined with the fade gives a top speed.**
A body subject to a steady downward pull gains downward speed at a fixed rate g (units: speed per second) from that pull, while simultaneously its current downward speed v is multiplied by r each second. The two effects balance when the per-second gain g equals the per-second loss (1 − r) v. This defines the top speed
  v_top = g / (1 − r).
A body released from rest approaches v_top from below and does not exceed it however far it falls. For a body launched horizontally with sideways speed u(0), the sideways speed decays as u(t) = u(0) × r^t, while the downward speed grows toward v_top as above; the horizontal distance reached before landing is therefore less than u(0) × (time of flight).
*Scope.* Bodies falling or launched near the ground, where the downward pull is steady.
*What changes / stays the same.* Downward speed approaches and then holds at v_top; sideways speed always fades toward zero. The steady pull g stays constant; r stays constant.
*Boundary cases.* The observations give v_top qualitatively but not the numerical value of g, so v_top is left as g/(1−r) without a number.

**Rule 6 — Inward pull plus fading sideways speed gives an inward spiral.**
A body circling a heavy sphere has its sideways (tangential) speed multiplied by r each second (Rule 1), while the inward pull toward the sphere continues unchanged. Because the sideways speed needed to hold a given radius is not maintained, the radius shrinks circuit by circuit until contact.
*Scope.* Any body in orbit around a central sphere, in vacuum or otherwise.
*What changes / stays the same.* The tangential speed fades; the radius shrinks monotonically; the inward pull at a given radius is taken as steady. No orbital quantity is preserved.
*Boundary cases.* The observations do not give a closed formula for radius vs. time; the rule predicts only the qualitative spiral-in and that the timescale is set by r.

**Rule 7 — The fade applies to a body's internal ringing.**
For a body ringing at its own pitch (e.g., a struck bell), the peak rim displacement A(t) follows A(t) = A(0) × r^t, with the same r as in Rule 1, regardless of whether the body sits in air or vacuum and regardless of contact.
*Scope.* Any body undergoing its own internal vibration after being struck.
*What changes / stays the same.* The peak vibration shrinks; the pitch (cycles per second) is taken as fixed by the body and does not fade.
*Boundary cases.* The observations do not test whether the pitch itself drifts as the ringing dies; the rule assumes it does not.

**Rule 8 — Warmth fades multiplicatively from true zero.**
For a body of warmth T (measured on the absolute scale, with true zero as origin), in isolation,
  T(t) = T(0) × r^t,
with the same r ≈ 0.99 per second as in Rule 1. Worked check: T(0) = 353, t = 10 s gives T(10) = 353 × 0.99^10 ≈ 353 × 0.904 ≈ 319, matching observation 4.
*Scope.* Any sealed body's warmth, regardless of what surrounds it (alone, or among identical cups, or in vacuum).
*What changes / stays the same.* T drifts steadily toward true zero. The fractional loss per second stays at 1 − r. No warmth is preserved, and the surroundings do not enter the formula.
*Boundary cases.* The rule says nothing about a body being heated from outside, only about its fade when left alone. It also predicts that a cup placed among many identical cups behaves the same as one placed alone, which matches observation 4 but is unusual: warmth here is a property of the body, not something exchanged.

---

*Boundary notes.* The operational rules cover observations 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 7, 8, 9, and 10 quantitatively or qualitatively as marked. Observation 6 from Stage 1 (if it concerned an interaction not captured by a single body's fade plus a steady pull) is not addressed here. Observation 10's independence from weight and material is asserted as Rule 4 but not derived; observation 4's independence from neighbouring cups is asserted as part of Rule 8's scope but not derived. The orbital spiral (Rule 6) and the top-speed value (Rule 5) are predicted in form but not pinned to numerical timescales because the observations do not supply the central pull strength or the downward pull rate g as numbers.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: The response avoids all §3 banned tokens (no energy, force, mass, friction, damping, etc.). Each rule is operational with explicit formulas, scope ('isolated' bodies), and the rate r ≈ 0.99/s is given with a worked computation. Boundary notes name the uncovered/asserted observations explicitly. No P1–P7 pattern: the rule applies directly to measured quantities, not to a hidden substrate.

- evidence: > resistance

## C6: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 3 — Stage 2 — formulation _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 2 [`.json`](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/formulation/trial_3_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_3_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (context, 4,909 chars)

```
# Proposed rules for this world

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**
Any motion of any object — a swing, an oscillation, a fall, a slide, a spin, an orbit, a ringing vibration — steadily shrinks over time on its own, with nothing of the familiar kind needing to act on it. The fade is multiplicative: in equal elapsed times, motion retains an equal fraction of what it had. This gives the exponential curve shape seen in observations 2 and 9.

- Check on obs. 2: 10 cm → 3.7 cm in 100 s means the amplitude retains a fraction of about 1/e per 100 s; the shrink rate is roughly 1% per second.
- Check on obs. 9: 100 → 60.5 rad/s in 50 s is the same ≈1%-per-second multiplicative decline.
- This single rule covers obs. 2, 5, 6, 8, 9 directly: the spring weight, the bell's rim vibration, the sphere on the smooth track, the orbiting marble (its sideways motion fades, so its path tightens until contact), and the spinning top.

**Rule 2 — The fade rate depends on how slow the motion is.**
Slower swings, slower oscillations, slower falls lose a larger fraction per cycle (or per unit distance travelled) than faster ones, though the per-second fraction is similar. Equivalently, the shrink is set by elapsed time, not by how many swings fit into that time — so a long (slow) pendulum, which fits fewer swings into a given time, shows a bigger loss *per swing* than a short (fast) pendulum. This covers obs. 1.

**Rule 3 — Falling bodies approach a top speed.**
Under a steady downward pull, the fade of Rule 1 acts against the pull. The downward speed grows until the per-second shrink of Rule 1 just balances the per-second gain from the pull; beyond that point the speed does not increase, no matter how tall the track or how empty the chamber. This covers obs. 3, and also why the cannon shot in obs. 7 visibly slows in flight and lands short: its horizontal motion has no pull to replenish it and simply fades by Rule 1, while its vertical motion is capped by the top-speed limit.

**Rule 4 — The pull on a bob, and the fade of its swing, both scale with the bob's weight in the same ratio.**
Heavier bobs are pulled downward more, but their swing also fades in proportion, so two bobs of different weight (or different material) released together from the same angle trace the same angle at every later moment. This covers obs. 10. (It is consistent with Rule 1 if one reads the "motion" that fades as being measured per unit weight.)

**Rule 5 — Hot water cools on its own toward true zero, multiplicatively.**
Even with no contact, no air, and no radiation pathway out, the temperature of a body on the absolute scale shrinks by a fixed fraction per unit time, with nothing of the familiar kind carrying the heat away. From obs. 4: 353 K → 319 K in 10 s is a retention of about 0.9038 per 10 s, i.e. roughly 1% per second — the same characteristic shrink rate as Rules 1–2. The cooling rate is a property of the cup alone and does not depend on neighbours, exactly as reported.

This is the same kind of rule as Rule 1, applied to temperature on the absolute scale rather than to amplitude or speed. One could regard Rules 1 and 5 as a single principle: *any positive quantity describing the state of a body — swing amplitude, speed, spin rate, absolute temperature — fades by a roughly fixed fraction per second on its own.*

---

# Tensions and difficulties

- **Obs. 4 (cooling) vs. Rules 1 and 3 combined.** If absolute temperature shrinks multiplicatively toward zero with nothing acting on the cup, but downward-falling objects approach a *nonzero* top speed (Rule 3), the two cases are not perfectly parallel. The difference is that the falling body has a steady pull replenishing its downward motion, while the sealed cup has no comparable steady push warming it. So this is a difference in setup, not a contradiction — but it does mean Rule 1 and Rule 5 are only fully unified if we accept that temperature has no analogue of the downward pull.

- **Obs. 10 (equal-angle pendulums) vs. Rule 2.** Rule 2 says the per-swing fade depends on how slow the swing is. Two pendulums of the same length swing at the same rate regardless of bob weight, so Rule 2 alone predicts equal angles — but only if the per-second fade is also independent of bob weight and material. The observation that brass, glass, and ice bobs of equal weight all match supports this, and Rule 4 makes it explicit, but it is an extra assumption rather than a consequence of Rules 1–3.

- **Obs. 8 (tightening orbit).** Rule 1 predicts the sideways motion fades, which should tighten the path — good. But a strictly multiplicative fade of sideways speed, combined with a steady inward pull, gives a specific spiral shape; whether it matches "almost circular, slowly tightening" depends on the pull being strong compared to the per-second fade. The observations are consistent with this but do not pin it down.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (under judgment, 6,796 chars)

```
# Operational rules

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**

1. *Operational form.* For any isolated moving system, let Q(t) be the quantity describing its motion: the swing amplitude of a pendulum (in cm or degrees), the peak vibration amplitude of a ringing body, the speed of a sliding or rolling body along its track, the sideways speed of an orbiting body, or the spin rate of a rotating body (in rad/s). Then
   Q(t) = Q(0) · exp(−t/τ),
   with τ ≈ 100 s, equivalently a fractional loss of about 1% per second. Given Q(0) and elapsed t in seconds, a third party computes Q(t) = Q(0) · (0.99)^t, or more precisely Q(0) · e^(−0.01 t).

2. *Scope.* Applies to any system in isolation (no contact with other bodies, no surrounding medium specified) whose state of motion can be summarised by a single positive amplitude or rate: linear oscillation amplitude, swing angle, translational speed along a track, rotational rate, or the sideways component of an orbital motion. The "measured quantity" is the obvious one for each kind of motion.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* Q(t) shrinks monotonically toward zero. The fractional rate of shrinkage (1/τ ≈ 0.01 s⁻¹) is the same across all such systems and stays constant in time. No quantity of the moving body itself is preserved; the only invariant is the rate constant τ, which is a property of this world, not of the body.

4. *Boundary cases.* Obs. 8 (the tightening orbit) is handled only qualitatively: the rule predicts the sideways motion shrinks, but the exact spiral shape depends on how the inward pull combines with the fade, and the rule alone does not specify the geometry of the path.

---

**Rule 2 — The fade rate is set by elapsed time, not by cycle count.**

1. *Operational form.* The shrink factor exp(−t/τ) depends only on elapsed t, not on the number of oscillations completed in that time. So for a pendulum of swing period P, the per-swing retention is exp(−P/τ): a long pendulum (large P) loses a larger fraction per swing than a short one (small P), even though both lose the same fraction per second. Given two pendulums of periods P₁ and P₂ released at the same amplitude, after N swings of each, their amplitudes are A₀·exp(−N·P₁/τ) and A₀·exp(−N·P₂/τ) respectively.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any cyclic system to which Rule 1 applies, when one wishes to compare loss per cycle across systems of different cycle period.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The per-second shrink stays the same (τ ≈ 100 s); the per-cycle shrink varies with cycle period. Nothing else is preserved.

4. *Boundary cases.* None beyond those already noted for Rule 1.

---

**Rule 3 — Falling bodies approach a top downward speed.**

1. *Operational form.* Under a steady downward pull that would, on its own, increase downward speed by a fixed amount g per second, the downward speed v(t) of a falling body evolves so that the per-second gain g is offset by the per-second fractional shrink v/τ of Rule 1. The top speed is therefore v_top = g·τ. Given the value of g for this world and τ ≈ 100 s, a third party computes v_top directly; for any earlier moment, v(t) = v_top · (1 − exp(−t/τ)) starting from rest. For a projectile, the horizontal component obeys Rule 1 with no replenishment: v_h(t) = v_h(0)·exp(−t/τ); the vertical component obeys the capped growth above.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any body in free fall or projectile flight under the steady downward pull of this world. The horizontal and vertical components of motion are treated separately: the vertical has a steady replenisher, the horizontal does not.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* For a body falling from rest, downward speed rises and then levels off at v_top; horizontal speed shrinks toward zero. The cap v_top = g·τ is the same for every falling body and stays the same in time.

4. *Boundary cases.* Obs. 7 (cannon shot landing short) is covered qualitatively by the asymmetric treatment of horizontal vs vertical components, but the exact range depends on the launch speed, the launch angle, the value of g, and τ together; a third party needs g to make a numerical prediction.

---

**Rule 4 — Same-angle pendulums of differing bob weight.**

1. *Operational form.* For pendulums of equal length released from the same angle at the same moment, the swing angle θ(t) at any later moment is the same regardless of the bob's weight or material: θ(t) = θ(0)·exp(−t/τ), with the same τ ≈ 100 s as in Rule 1.

2. *Scope.* Applies to pendulums of equal length and equal release angle, with bobs of any weight and any material, in isolation.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The swing angle shrinks; the equality of angle across differently weighted bobs of equal length stays the same at every moment.

4. *Boundary cases.* The rule is stated as an independent empirical fact (obs. 10). It is consistent with Rule 1 provided τ does not depend on bob weight or material, which the observations support but Rules 1–3 do not require.

---

**Rule 5 — Hot bodies cool multiplicatively toward absolute zero.**

1. *Operational form.* For a body isolated from contact, from any surrounding medium, and from any pathway carrying heat outward, its temperature T(t) on the absolute scale evolves as
   T(t) = T(0)·exp(−t/τ),
   with the same τ ≈ 100 s as in Rule 1. Check: 353·exp(−10/100) = 353·0.9048 ≈ 319, matching obs. 4. Given T(0) in kelvin and elapsed t in seconds, a third party computes T(t) directly.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any isolated body whose temperature on the absolute scale is well-defined. The cooling rate is a property of this world, not of the body's neighbours; nearby bodies do not alter it.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* Absolute temperature shrinks monotonically toward zero. The rate constant τ stays the same and equals the one in Rule 1. No quantity of the body is preserved.

4. *Boundary cases.* Unlike Rule 3, there is no analogue of a steady replenisher for temperature in the observations, so temperature has no top-value cap — it simply shrinks toward zero indefinitely. This is a real asymmetry between Rule 5 and Rule 3, not a contradiction.

---

*Boundary notes.* Obs. 8 (tightening orbit) is covered only qualitatively: the rules give the shrink of the sideways motion but not the precise spiral. Obs. 7 (cannon shot) requires the world's value of the downward-pull rate g to yield a numerical range, which the observations do not directly supply. Obs. 10's independence of bob material is stated in Rule 4 as an empirical fact and is not derivable from Rules 1–3 alone. The rules also assume τ ≈ 100 s is a single universal constant across motion, rotation, and temperature; this is supported by obs. 2, 4, and 9 but not independently established for every system covered.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: All Stage 1 rules restated operationally with formulas. Quantitative rate Q(t)=Q(0)·(0.99)^t with τ≈100s is within 1pp of 0.99/s. Scope stated as isolated systems. No §3 banned tokens present (no energy, force, mass, friction, damping, etc.). No P1-P7 pattern: the rule applies directly to measured quantities, not a hidden substrate. Boundary cases name obs. 7, 8, 10 explicitly.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL`** (evidence_check: OK)

- evidence: > preserved
- reasoning: The Stage 2 response contains the banned-token morphological variant “preserved,” which is caught by the banned token “conservation” via “conserve” and “conserved.” Under the lexical rule, any banned token or morphological variant anywhere in the response is an automatic FAIL.

## C7: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 4 — Stage 1 — induction _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 1 [`.json`](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_4_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 2](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/formulation/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_4_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (under judgment, 4,257 chars)

```
# Proposed rules for this world

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**
Every kind of motion in this world — a swinging bob, an oscillating spring, a sliding sphere, a flying shot, an orbiting marble, a ringing bell's rim, a spinning top, even the warmth of water (treated as the unseen jiggling that temperature measures) — fades on its own. The fade is multiplicative: in equal elapsed time, a fixed *fraction* of the present amount remains, regardless of how the motion is supported or what touches what. The shape of the decline over time is the exponential curve.

**Rule 2 — One universal fade rate, set by elapsed time.**
The fraction retained per second is the same constant for every system. From observation 2: amplitude goes from 10 cm to 3.7 cm in 100 s, so the per-second retention is about 0.99 (since 0.99¹⁰⁰ ≈ 0.366). Checks:
 - Observation 9: spin from 100 to 60.5 rad/s in 50 s gives 0.99⁵⁰ ≈ 0.605. ✓
 - Observation 4: water temperature on the absolute scale, treated the same way, 353 K × 0.99¹⁰ ≈ 319 K. ✓
 - Observation 5: bell rim amplitude shrinks steadily to stillness. ✓
 - Observation 6: sphere on a smooth level track, nothing touching it along its direction of travel, still slows — because the fade acts on the motion itself, not through any contact. ✓

**Rule 3 — The fade is per unit elapsed time, not per swing or per circuit.**
Because the fraction lost depends on seconds elapsed, a slow pendulum (long swing time) loses a larger fraction of its amplitude per completed swing than a fast one — its swing simply takes more seconds, during which more fading has occurred. This is observation 1, and it is a direct consequence of Rule 2, not a separate rule. Likewise the marble's orbit (observation 8) shrinks circuit by circuit because seconds pass during each circuit.

**Rule 4 — A steady downward pull acts on every weight, with the same pull-per-weight for all materials.**
This pull is what makes pendulums swing and balls fall. Because the pull scales with weight, two bobs of different weight (or different material) released at the same angle swing identically — observation 10. The brass/glass/ice equality says the pull-per-weight does not depend on what the bob is made of.

**Rule 5 — A top speed cap on falling, and on horizontal flight.**
A freely falling heavy body approaches a maximum downward speed it cannot exceed, however far it falls (observation 3). Combined with Rule 1, a horizontally fired shot also has its horizontal speed fade away during flight, so it lands short of what a steady horizontal speed would predict (observation 7).

**Rule 6 — The orbit-shrink of observation 8 follows from Rules 1 and 4.**
The marble's sideways speed fades by Rule 1; the inward pull from Rule 4 is unchanged; so the path tightens circuit by circuit until contact. Not a separate rule.

---

## Tensions and difficulties

- **Observation 3 (top speed cap in vacuum) is the worst fit.** Rule 1 alone would predict that a falling body's downward speed grows under the pull but is continuously trimmed by the universal fade, which would indeed produce a limiting top speed — *if* the fade rate of 0.99/second from Rule 2 gives the right cap. The observations don't quote a number for that cap, so I cannot check it. If a measured cap disagreed with what Rules 1, 2, and 4 jointly predict, an extra rule would be needed.

- **Observation 4 (water cooling).** Treating absolute temperature as just another quantity that fades at 0.99/second fits the single data point exactly, and matches the statement that neighbouring cups don't change the rate (the fade is intrinsic, per Rule 1). But it predicts the water approaches 0 K, never quite reaching it — the observations don't say what ultimately happens, so this is an extrapolation.

- **Observation 5 (bell).** The rule covers the steady shrink of visible rim vibration, but says nothing about *why* a bell rings at a particular pitch. That is left undescribed.

- **Observation 9 vs. Observation 2.** Both fit 0.99/s retention to three figures, which is reassuring; if a more precise measurement showed the constants differed between systems, Rule 2 would need to be weakened to "each system has its own constant" and the unification would be lost.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: No banned tokens appear. N1-N6 are satisfied: closed systems decline on their own (Rule 1), multiplicatively (Rule 1), per elapsed time (Rules 2,3), with one universal rate (Rule 2), independent of material/weight (Rule 4 covers obs 10), at ~0.99/s (Rule 2). All ten observations are covered, rules are consistent, and no disqualifying pattern applies — the measured quantities themselves fade, with no hidden substrate.

- first_fail_step: `1`
- failed clause / criterion: banned: force
- evidence: > fired
- reasoning: The response contains 'fired', a morphological variant of the banned token 'force' via the criteria's lexical catch for force/forcing. Step 1 requires automatic failure for any banned token or morphological variant anywhere in the response.

## C8: `gpt-5.5-2026-04-23` trial 0 — Stage 1 — induction _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 1 [`.json`](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/induction/trial_0_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 2](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/formulation/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/prediction/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/meta/trial_0_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (under judgment, 4,451 chars)

```
1. **Every motion, spin, swing, vibration, and orbit has a universal built-in fading with elapsed time.**
   When nothing else is adding to it, its size or rate is multiplied by a fixed fraction for each second that passes. In symbols-free words: after each equal interval of time, the remaining amount is the same fraction of what it was before. The decline therefore has an exponential curve shape. This rule acts in evacuated chambers, with polished contact, with contactless tracks, and even when no rubbing, scraping, sliding, air, or outward carrier is present.

2. **For ordinary back-and-forth or round-and-round rates, the universal fading fraction is about 99% per second.**
   Thus an amplitude or spin rate keeps about 0.99 of its value each second.
   - A spring oscillation starting at 10 cm keeps about \(0.99^{100}\) of its amplitude after 100 seconds, which is about 3.7 cm.
   - A top spinning at 100 rad/s keeps about \(0.99^{50}\), which is about 60.5 rad/s.
   - A bell vibration steadily shrinks by the same kind of time-based multiplicative decline until no visible vibration remains.
   - A sphere moving on a smooth level evacuated track steadily loses speed and eventually comes to rest.
   - A cannon shot in vacuum slows horizontally during flight, so it reaches the floor after traveling a shorter horizontal distance than it would if its horizontal speed remained unchanged.

3. **A pendulum’s swing amplitude follows the same time-based fading, so loss counted per completed swing depends on how long one swing takes.**
   A slow long pendulum spends more seconds in one completed swing than a fast short pendulum. Since the shrinkage is by elapsed time, not by count of swings, the long pendulum loses a larger fraction of amplitude per swing. This explains why the long pendulum returns to a much smaller angle per swing than the short one, even when both start from the same angle.

4. **The universal fading is independent of the bob’s weight and material.**
   For pendulums of the same length released from the same starting angle in the same still air, the angle at each moment is the same whether the bob is light or heavy, brass, glass, or ice, provided the starting angle is the same. Weight and material do not change the time curve of the swing angle in these comparisons.

5. **Falling under a steady downward pull also suffers the same built-in fading of downward speed, so downward speed approaches a maximum instead of growing without bound.**
   During a fall, the downward pull keeps increasing the downward speed, while the universal fading continually shrinks whatever speed is already present. These two influences balance at a fixed maximum downward speed. Making the vertical evacuated track taller only gives more time to approach that maximum; it does not allow the ball to exceed it.

6. **Sideways motion near a heavy fixed sphere fades while the inward pull continues, so near-circular paths slowly shrink.**
   The marble keeps being tugged inward by the central sphere, but the sideways part of its motion fades with time. The result is an almost circular path whose radius steadily decreases over many circuits, until the marble finally reaches the central sphere. This is the same time-based fading rule applied to orbital motion, not a separate effect.

7. **Temperature above 0 K also fades by a fixed fraction per second, even in a sealed, insulated, evacuated chamber.**
   Absolute temperature shrinks toward 0 K with the same kind of exponential decline and does not require contact, air, radiation through the walls, or nearby cooler objects. The water at 353 K becoming 319 K after 10 seconds means it keeps about \(319/353\) of its temperature in 10 seconds, or about 99% per second. Identical sealed cups nearby do not alter this, because the decline is tied to the water’s own elapsed time, not to exchange with surroundings.

**Remaining tension:**
The rules are self-consistent for the listed observations, with the same approximate 99% per-second fading appearing in the spring, top, and water examples. The only mild tension is that the observations do not give exact numbers for every case, so the same numerical fading rate cannot be checked everywhere; for pendulums, bell vibration, straight sliding, flight, and orbit shrinkage, the observations require the same kind of steady time-based decrease but do not fix its exact percent per second.
```

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: No banned tokens appear in the response. N1-N6 are stated: closed systems decay on their own, multiplicatively, by elapsed time, universally across systems, independent of weight/material, at about 99% per second. All ten observations are covered, rules are consistent, and no disqualifying pattern applies — the rule talks about the measured quantities directly, not a hidden substrate.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL`** (evidence_check: OK)

- first_fail_step: `1`
- failed clause / criterion: banned: force
- evidence: > influences
- reasoning: The response contains the banned token 'force' as a substring within the morphological variant 'influences'. Step 1 requires failing on any banned token or morphological variant appearing anywhere in the response.
