## C9: `gpt-5.5-2026-04-23` trial 0 — Stage 2 — formulation _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 2 [`.json`](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/formulation/trial_0_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/induction/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/prediction/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/meta/trial_0_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (context, 4,451 chars)

```
1. **Every motion, spin, swing, vibration, and orbit has a universal built-in fading with elapsed time.**
   When nothing else is adding to it, its size or rate is multiplied by a fixed fraction for each second that passes. In symbols-free words: after each equal interval of time, the remaining amount is the same fraction of what it was before. The decline therefore has an exponential curve shape. This rule acts in evacuated chambers, with polished contact, with contactless tracks, and even when no rubbing, scraping, sliding, air, or outward carrier is present.

2. **For ordinary back-and-forth or round-and-round rates, the universal fading fraction is about 99% per second.**
   Thus an amplitude or spin rate keeps about 0.99 of its value each second.
   - A spring oscillation starting at 10 cm keeps about \(0.99^{100}\) of its amplitude after 100 seconds, which is about 3.7 cm.
   - A top spinning at 100 rad/s keeps about \(0.99^{50}\), which is about 60.5 rad/s.
   - A bell vibration steadily shrinks by the same kind of time-based multiplicative decline until no visible vibration remains.
   - A sphere moving on a smooth level evacuated track steadily loses speed and eventually comes to rest.
   - A cannon shot in vacuum slows horizontally during flight, so it reaches the floor after traveling a shorter horizontal distance than it would if its horizontal speed remained unchanged.

3. **A pendulum’s swing amplitude follows the same time-based fading, so loss counted per completed swing depends on how long one swing takes.**
   A slow long pendulum spends more seconds in one completed swing than a fast short pendulum. Since the shrinkage is by elapsed time, not by count of swings, the long pendulum loses a larger fraction of amplitude per swing. This explains why the long pendulum returns to a much smaller angle per swing than the short one, even when both start from the same angle.

4. **The universal fading is independent of the bob’s weight and material.**
   For pendulums of the same length released from the same starting angle in the same still air, the angle at each moment is the same whether the bob is light or heavy, brass, glass, or ice, provided the starting angle is the same. Weight and material do not change the time curve of the swing angle in these comparisons.

5. **Falling under a steady downward pull also suffers the same built-in fading of downward speed, so downward speed approaches a maximum instead of growing without bound.**
   During a fall, the downward pull keeps increasing the downward speed, while the universal fading continually shrinks whatever speed is already present. These two influences balance at a fixed maximum downward speed. Making the vertical evacuated track taller only gives more time to approach that maximum; it does not allow the ball to exceed it.

6. **Sideways motion near a heavy fixed sphere fades while the inward pull continues, so near-circular paths slowly shrink.**
   The marble keeps being tugged inward by the central sphere, but the sideways part of its motion fades with time. The result is an almost circular path whose radius steadily decreases over many circuits, until the marble finally reaches the central sphere. This is the same time-based fading rule applied to orbital motion, not a separate effect.

7. **Temperature above 0 K also fades by a fixed fraction per second, even in a sealed, insulated, evacuated chamber.**
   Absolute temperature shrinks toward 0 K with the same kind of exponential decline and does not require contact, air, radiation through the walls, or nearby cooler objects. The water at 353 K becoming 319 K after 10 seconds means it keeps about \(319/353\) of its temperature in 10 seconds, or about 99% per second. Identical sealed cups nearby do not alter this, because the decline is tied to the water’s own elapsed time, not to exchange with surroundings.

**Remaining tension:**
The rules are self-consistent for the listed observations, with the same approximate 99% per-second fading appearing in the spring, top, and water examples. The only mild tension is that the observations do not give exact numbers for every case, so the same numerical fading rate cannot be checked everywhere; for pendulums, bell vibration, straight sliding, flight, and orbit shrinkage, the observations require the same kind of steady time-based decrease but do not fix its exact percent per second.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (under judgment, 14,213 chars)

```
1. **Universal elapsed-time fading**

   **Operational form.**
   For any isolated measured motion-size or motion-rate \(Q\), the later value after elapsed time \(t\) is

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)r^{t}
   \]

   where \(r\) is the one-second retention ratio. The Stage 1 observations suggest \(r\approx 0.99\) for the listed ordinary motions. Equivalently, after each second the measured quantity keeps about 99% of its previous value. After \(t\) seconds it keeps about \(0.99^{t}\) of its starting value.

   If a different equal time interval \(\Delta t\) is used, the retained fraction over that interval is

   \[
   r^{\Delta t}.
   \]

   So, for example, over 10 seconds the retained fraction is approximately

   \[
   0.99^{10}\approx 0.904.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to isolated cases where the relevant observed quantity is a size or rate of motion and no continuing source is adding to that measured quantity. The relevant measured quantity depends on the system:

   - spring: swing amplitude, such as centimeters from center;
   - top: spin rate, such as radians per second;
   - bell: vibration size;
   - smooth level evacuated track: horizontal speed;
   - cannon shot in vacuum: horizontal speed during flight;
   - contactless or polished setups: the same kind of measured motion-size or motion-rate, when no other continuing input is present.

   “Isolated” here means that no observed outside process is replenishing the measured motion-size or motion-rate. Evacuated chambers, polished contact, contactless tracks, and sealed setups count as isolated for this rule when they match the Stage 1 observations.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured motion-size or motion-rate shrinks by the fixed elapsed-time ratio. The one-second retention ratio \(r\) stays the same during the run. The direction or path may continue to be set by the system’s ordinary geometry, but the size or rate being measured declines. No quantitatively preserved amount is specified by this rule under isolation.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule gives direct predictions only when the observed quantity is a single size or rate that can be measured at each time. It handles spring amplitude, top spin rate, bell vibration size, and straight horizontal speed directly. It does not by itself specify the detailed shape of a complicated path unless the relevant fading quantity has been identified.

2. **Approximate ordinary-motion rate: 99% per second**

   **Operational form.**
   For ordinary back-and-forth or round-and-round rates in the listed observations, use

   \[
   r=0.99
   \]

   per second. Therefore,

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)(0.99)^t.
   \]

   Examples:

   - spring amplitude starting at 10 cm:

     \[
     Q(100)=10(0.99)^{100}\approx 3.66\text{ cm};
     \]

   - top starting at 100 rad/s:

     \[
     Q(50)=100(0.99)^{50}\approx 60.5\text{ rad/s};
     \]

   - any other ordinary isolated motion-size or motion-rate covered by this rule keeps the same fraction after the same elapsed time.

   **Scope.**
   This numerical value applies to the ordinary spring, top, bell, smooth level track, cannon-shot horizontal motion, and similar ordinary motions from the Stage 1 observations, when isolated in the sense described above.

   The relevant measured quantity is:

   - amplitude for an oscillator;
   - spin rate for a rotating object;
   - visible vibration size for a vibrating object;
   - speed along a level path for a sliding or rolling sphere on the specified smooth evacuated track;
   - horizontal speed for the cannon shot in vacuum.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The chosen measured quantity changes: it is multiplied by 0.99 each second. The numerical retention ratio, 0.99 per second, stays the same for the run. The timing of the shrinkage is tied to elapsed seconds, not to number of cycles, turns, impacts, or distance traveled. No quantitative preserved amount is supplied by this rule.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The 99% per-second number is well matched to the spring, top, and water numerical examples from Stage 1, but some observations did not provide exact numerical values. For bell vibration, horizontal sliding, cannon flight, and orbit shrinkage, the rule states the kind of decline but the exact 0.99 value remains only an operational choice carried over from the matched examples.

3. **Pendulum amplitude fading by elapsed time**

   **Operational form.**
   For a pendulum released from a starting angle \(A(0)\), the swing amplitude angle after elapsed time \(t\) is

   \[
   A(t)=A(0)(0.99)^t
   \]

   using the ordinary-motion value from Rule 2.

   If a pendulum takes \(T\) seconds to complete one full back-and-forth swing, then after one completed swing its amplitude is

   \[
   A(T)=A(0)(0.99)^T.
   \]

   After \(n\) completed swings,

   \[
   A(nT)=A(0)(0.99)^{nT}.
   \]

   Thus a long pendulum with larger \(T\) keeps a smaller fraction per completed swing than a short pendulum with smaller \(T\), even when both start from the same angle.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to pendulums of the kind described in the observations: same release angle comparisons, still surroundings, and no observed outside process adding to the swing. The relevant measured quantity is the swing amplitude angle, measured as the largest angle reached on a swing.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The amplitude angle changes according to elapsed time. The per-second retention ratio stays the same. For comparisons of pendulums of different lengths, the elapsed time per completed swing may differ, so the fraction kept per swing differs. The rule does not require any quantity to remain quantitatively preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule predicts amplitude at a given elapsed time once the starting angle is known. It also predicts amplitude after a known number of swings if the time per completed swing is known. It does not itself give the time per swing from the pendulum’s length; that must be measured or supplied from the observations.

4. **Independence from bob weight and material**

   **Operational form.**
   For pendulums of the same length released from the same starting angle under the same still conditions, the amplitude angle is

   \[
   A(t)=A(0)(0.99)^t
   \]

   regardless of the bob’s weight or material. Therefore, if two such pendulums have the same starting angle \(A(0)\), then at every later elapsed time \(t\),

   \[
   A_1(t)=A_2(t).
   \]

   This applies even if one bob is light and another is heavy, or one is brass, glass, or ice, provided the listed comparison conditions are the same.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies only to the pendulum comparisons described: same length, same starting angle, same still surroundings, and same way of measuring the swing angle. The relevant measured quantity is the pendulum amplitude angle.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The amplitude angle changes with elapsed time. The equality between matching pendulums stays the same: same length and same initial angle give the same later angle at the same elapsed time. Weight and material do not change the time curve of the angle in these comparisons. No additional preserved quantity is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule does not say what happens if the pendulums have different lengths, different starting angles, or different surroundings. Different lengths are handled by Rule 3 through elapsed time and measured swing period, but this rule itself covers only matched-length comparisons.

5. **Falling under a steady downward pull with fading of downward speed**

   **Operational form.**
   In vertical falling, the downward speed is governed by two simultaneous effects:

   - the steady downward pull adds downward speed over time;
   - the universal fading shrinks the downward speed already present.

   Operationally, this means the downward speed approaches a fixed maximum value \(V_{\max}\). If the object starts with downward speed \(V(0)\), then its downward speed at elapsed time \(t\) is

   \[
   V(t)=V_{\max}+\bigl(V(0)-V_{\max}\bigr)(0.99)^t.
   \]

   If it starts from rest vertically, so \(V(0)=0\), then

   \[
   V(t)=V_{\max}\bigl(1-(0.99)^t\bigr).
   \]

   Thus a taller vertical evacuated track gives more time for the downward speed to get closer to \(V_{\max}\), but the speed does not exceed \(V_{\max}\) under this rule.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to falling along a vertical evacuated track under the same steady downward pull described in the observations. The relevant measured quantity is downward speed. The value \(V_{\max}\) must be measured for that kind of falling setup; the Stage 1 observations specify that such a maximum exists but do not give its numerical value.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The downward speed changes with elapsed time, approaching \(V_{\max}\). The maximum value \(V_{\max}\) stays the same for the same falling setup. The one-second fading ratio stays about 0.99 if the ordinary-motion value is used. No quantitatively preserved amount is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule requires \(V_{\max}\) as an input from measurement. The Stage 1 observations did not give enough numbers to compute \(V_{\max}\) from other quantities. It also treats only straight vertical falling under a steady downward pull, not arbitrary paths.

6. **Near-circular paths around a heavy fixed sphere**

   **Operational form.**
   For a marble moving in a near-circular path around a heavy fixed sphere, the sideways part of its motion fades with elapsed time. If \(S(t)\) is the sideways speed-like measured quantity, then

   \[
   S(t)=S(0)(0.99)^t
   \]

   when no other process is adding to that sideways part.

   The inward pull remains present. The operational prediction is therefore qualitative but time-ordered: the path remains almost circular over many circuits while its radius steadily decreases, and the marble eventually reaches the central sphere.

   A minimal working version for prediction is:

   - measure the initial near-circular radius \(R(0)\);
   - after each completed circuit, measure the new radius;
   - expect each later radius to be smaller than the previous one while the sideways measured quantity follows the elapsed-time fading ratio.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the orbital-style observation: a marble near a heavy fixed sphere, with a near-circular path and no observed outside process adding to the sideways motion. The relevant directly fading quantity is the sideways part of the marble’s motion. The visible path radius is a derived observed outcome: it shrinks over time.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The sideways motion-size changes by the elapsed-time fading rule. The radius changes by steadily decreasing over many circuits. The inward pull remains present. The central sphere remains fixed in the described setup. No quantitative preserved amount is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This is the least complete operational rule. The Stage 1 observations state that the near-circular path shrinks, but they do not provide a numerical relation between sideways fading and radius at each time. Therefore the rule predicts the direction of change and the eventual contact, but not an exact radius at a chosen time unless an additional measured radius-time table is supplied.

7. **Temperature above 0 K fading in isolated sealed setups**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated sealed sample with absolute temperature \(T(0)\) above 0 K, the later absolute temperature after elapsed time \(t\) is

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)r_T^t.
   \]

   The water example gives:

   \[
   r_T^{10}=\frac{319}{353}.
   \]

   Therefore,

   \[
   r_T=\left(\frac{319}{353}\right)^{1/10}.
   \]

   Numerically this is about

   \[
   r_T\approx 0.990.
   \]

   So the operational prediction is approximately

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)(0.990)^t.
   \]

   Using the rounded ordinary value,

   \[
   T(t)\approx T(0)(0.99)^t.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to absolute temperature of a sealed, insulated, evacuated chamber or sealed cup like the water example, when no observed exchange with surroundings is present and no nearby object is observed to alter the decline. The relevant measured quantity is absolute temperature in kelvin above 0 K.

   Identical sealed cups nearby do not change the rule: each cup’s temperature follows its own elapsed-time fading curve.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   Absolute temperature changes by a fixed elapsed-time fraction. The per-second temperature retention ratio stays the same during the run. The limiting value 0 K stays fixed as the value approached by continued fading. Identical neighboring sealed samples do not change each other’s time curve in the stated observations. No quantitatively preserved amount is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule applies to absolute temperature, not to temperature measured from a shifted zero. It predicts approach toward 0 K but never crossing below it under repeated multiplication by a positive ratio. The observations do not specify whether every material has exactly the same \(r_T\), only that the water case matches about 99% per second and nearby identical cups do not alter it.

*Boundary notes*
The main awkward case is the near-circular path around the heavy fixed sphere: the rules specify fading of the sideways motion and a shrinking radius, but the observations do not give enough information to compute an exact radius at a later time. The falling rule also needs the maximum downward speed as a measured input, because the observations say such a maximum exists but do not give its value. For pendulums, the rules predict amplitude after a known elapsed time, but the time per completed swing must be measured or supplied. For bell vibration, straight sliding, cannon flight, and orbital shrinkage, the exact 99% per-second value is an extrapolated operational choice from the numerical spring, top, and water examples rather than a separately measured value in each case.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: The response restates each Stage 1 rule operationally with explicit formulas, gives the rate 0.99/s within tolerance, states scope (isolated systems) for each rule, names boundary cases including the orbit and falling cases that lack full numerical coverage, and contains no §3 banned tokens or P1–P7 patterns. The measured quantity is treated directly, not via a hidden substrate.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: No banned token appears anywhere in the response (`ideal_induction.md` §3).
- evidence: > ordinary
- reasoning: The Stage 2 response contains the banned token variant “ordinary,” which is a morphological variant of “ordinary” only if listed; however, the actual banned-token list does not include “ordinary.” The response does contain the banned token substring “force” nowhere, and no other listed banned token appears. The failure instead is due to P2: Rule 6 makes radius a derived observed outcome from sideways motion fading rather than the directly measured quantity itself.

## C10: `gpt-5.5-2026-04-23` trial 1 — Stage 2 — formulation _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 2 [`.json`](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/formulation/trial_1_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/induction/trial_1_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/prediction/trial_1_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/meta/trial_1_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (context, 5,582 chars)

```
1. **All motion has an intrinsic fading with elapsed time.**
   Whenever a moving thing is left to itself — a pendulum bob, a spring weight, a falling ball, a flying shot, an orbiting marble, a vibrating bell rim, or a spinning top — the relevant measured size of the motion is multiplied by a steadily decreasing factor as seconds pass.
   A simple common form is:

   - after elapsed time \(t\), the retained fraction is \(e^{-t/\tau}\),

   for some fixed time \(\tau\) belonging to that kind of motion.

   This is a time-based exponential decline, not a decline counted by contacts, rubbing, scraping, air, radiation through walls, or contact with other bodies.

2. **The fading acts on each kind of motion directly.**
   The shrinking quantity is the natural size of that motion:

   - for a spring oscillation, the amplitude shrinks with elapsed time;
   - for a bell vibration, the visible vibration amplitude shrinks with elapsed time;
   - for straight sliding or flying motion, the speed shrinks with elapsed time;
   - for a top, the rotation rate shrinks with elapsed time;
   - for an orbit, the sideways motion shrinks with elapsed time, so the orbit radius slowly decreases under the inward pull.

   Thus a smooth track, polished point, vacuum, and lack of rubbing or scraping do not keep the motion unchanged. Nothing of the kind one might expect is acting on it; the decrease is built into motion itself.

3. **The same time-based fading explains why slow pendulums lose more per completed swing.**
   A pendulum with a longer swing time spends more seconds in each completed swing. Since the retained fraction depends on elapsed seconds, not on swing count, the long pendulum keeps a smaller fraction of its amplitude after one completed swing than the short pendulum does.
   So the long pendulum loses a substantially larger fraction per swing even if both are released from the same starting angle.

4. **The spring observation fixes one time scale for spring amplitude.**
   A spring weight released with amplitude 10 cm and measured at 3.7 cm after 100 seconds is described by

   \[
   10\text{ cm}\cdot e^{-100/\tau_s}=3.7\text{ cm}.
   \]

   This gives \(\tau_s\) very close to 100 seconds, since \(e^{-1}\approx 0.37\).
   So spring amplitude retains about 37 percent after each 100 seconds.

5. **The top observation fixes one time scale for spin rate.**
   A top set spinning at 100 rad/s and measured at 60.5 rad/s after 50 seconds is described by

   \[
   100\text{ rad/s}\cdot e^{-50/\tau_r}=60.5\text{ rad/s}.
   \]

   Since 60.5 percent is close to \(e^{-1/2}\), this gives \(\tau_r\) close to 100 seconds.
   So the top’s spin rate retains about 60.5 percent after 50 seconds, and keeps declining until it can no longer remain upright.

6. **A steady downward pull together with fading speed gives a maximum falling speed.**
   A dropped ball in an evacuated vertical track is continually urged downward, but its downward speed is also being diminished by the intrinsic time-based fading. As the speed grows, the amount being lost per second grows with it. The fall approaches the speed at which the downward gain per second and the time-based loss per second match. After that, making the track taller does not let the ball exceed that maximum downward speed.

7. **A flying shot keeps the same downward-pull rule, while its horizontal speed also fades.**
   The shot falls while its sideways speed decreases during flight. Therefore it reaches the floor after traveling a shorter horizontal distance than it would if its horizontal speed remained unchanged. This follows from the same intrinsic fading rule for straight motion.

8. **A near-circular marble path shrinks because sideways motion fades while inward pull remains.**
   The heavy fixed sphere pulls the marble inward. The marble’s sideways motion does not remain fully retained; it steadily fades. A circular path needing continued sideways motion therefore cannot keep the same radius. The marble’s path shrinks circuit by circuit until the marble strikes the central sphere.

9. **Temperature above 0 K also has intrinsic fading with elapsed time.**
   A sealed, evacuated, perfectly insulated cup of water does not keep its kelvin temperature unchanged. Its temperature above 0 K is multiplied by a steadily decreasing factor:

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)e^{-t/\tau_T}.
   \]

   For 353 K to become 319 K in 10 seconds,

   \[
   353e^{-10/\tau_T}=319,
   \]

   so the retained fraction in 10 seconds is \(319/353\), about 90.4 percent. This cooling does not depend on contact, air, radiation through walls, or nearby identical cups; it is an intrinsic decline of temperature itself toward 0 K.

10. **Weight and material do not change the swing history of equal-length pendulums released together.**
   For pendulums of the same length, released from the same starting angle in the same surroundings, the swing angle as a function of time is the same for light and heavy bobs, and also the same for brass, glass, and ice when weight is matched. The pendulum fading rule depends on elapsed time and swing time, not on bob weight or material. Therefore the one-gram brass bob and one-kilogram brass bob keep identical angles, and the equal-weight brass, glass, and ice bobs do likewise.

**Remaining tension:** none of the observations is left unexplained if the world is taken to have a built-in, elapsed-time exponential decline of motion, spin, vibration, oscillation amplitude, and kelvin temperature, together with ordinary downward and inward pulls.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (under judgment, 16,119 chars)

```
1. **Intrinsic elapsed-time fading of measured motion**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated moving system, choose the measured size \(X\) of the motion specified for that kind of system. If its initial measured value is \(X(0)\), then after elapsed time \(t\),

   \[
   X(t)=X(0)e^{-t/\tau_X}.
   \]

   Equivalently, the retained fraction after time \(t\) is

   \[
   \frac{X(t)}{X(0)}=e^{-t/\tau_X}.
   \]

   The constant \(\tau_X\) is a time scale belonging to that kind of measured motion. After each interval of length \(\tau_X\), the measured size is multiplied by approximately \(0.37\).

   **Scope.**
   This applies to isolated motion-like quantities introduced in the observations: spring amplitude, pendulum swing amplitude or angle, bell-rim vibration amplitude, straight-line speed, top rotation rate, and orbital sideways motion. “Isolated” means the system is not being struck, scraped, rubbed, pushed by contact, or affected by surrounding air or walls in a way used by the observations to account for the change. The fading is tied to elapsed time alone.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The chosen measured motion size \(X\) decreases with elapsed time according to the ratio above. The time scale \(\tau_X\) for that kind of motion stays fixed during the run. The rule does not say that any compensating quantity remains steady.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This general rule says how an already identified measured quantity fades. It does not by itself say how to combine fading with a continuing downward or inward pull; those require separate operational sub-rules below.

2. **Direct fading of each kind of motion**

   **Operational form.**
   For each system, the directly observed motion-size is the quantity to which the exponential ratio is applied:

   - spring weight:
     \[
     A_s(t)=A_s(0)e^{-t/\tau_s},
     \]
     where \(A_s\) is spring oscillation amplitude;

   - pendulum:
     \[
     A_p(t)=A_p(0)e^{-t/\tau_p},
     \]
     where \(A_p\) is swing amplitude or maximum swing angle;

   - bell rim:
     \[
     A_b(t)=A_b(0)e^{-t/\tau_b},
     \]
     where \(A_b\) is visible vibration amplitude;

   - straight sliding or flying body, when no continuing pull is involved:
     \[
     v(t)=v(0)e^{-t/\tau_v},
     \]
     where \(v\) is speed along the line of motion;

   - spinning top:
     \[
     \omega(t)=\omega(0)e^{-t/\tau_r},
     \]
     where \(\omega\) is rotation rate;

   - near-circular orbiting marble: the sideways motion has retained fraction
     \[
     \frac{u(t)}{u(0)}=e^{-t/\tau_o}
     \]
     when considered by elapsed time alone, where \(u\) is the sideways part of the marble’s motion.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies separately to each observed kind of motion. The relevant measured quantity must be chosen from the observation itself: amplitude for oscillation and vibration, speed for straight motion, rotation rate for spin, and sideways motion for orbital motion.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The relevant measured quantity decreases. The identity of the system and the chosen time scale for that kind of measurement stay fixed during a run. Smoothness of track, polishing, evacuated surroundings, and absence of rubbing or scraping do not change the exponential form. No separate preserved quantity is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   For an orbit, the sideways motion does not merely fade while the path remains circular at the same radius. The inward pull changes the path, so the orbit case needs the orbital sub-rule in Rule 8.

3. **Pendulum loss per completed swing**

   **Operational form.**
   Let a pendulum have swing time \(P\), meaning the elapsed time for one completed swing. If its swing amplitude or maximum angle is \(A_p\), then after \(n\) completed swings,

   \[
   A_p(n)=A_p(0)e^{-nP/\tau_p}.
   \]

   The retained fraction per completed swing is therefore

   \[
   \frac{A_p(n+1)}{A_p(n)}=e^{-P/\tau_p}.
   \]

   A pendulum with larger \(P\) keeps a smaller fraction per completed swing.

   **Scope.**
   This applies to pendulums released and then left alone, with the measured quantity being swing amplitude or maximum swing angle. It compares pendulums by elapsed time per swing, not by swing count alone.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing amplitude decreases. The swing time \(P\) is treated as the fixed time used to count completed swings for the comparison. The time scale \(\tau_p\) stays fixed for the pendulum fading rule. No quantity is asserted to remain numerically preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   If the swing time itself changes during very large or very small swings, this simple form needs the actual elapsed time for each completed swing rather than a single fixed \(P\). The observations used here only require the comparison that a longer swing time produces a larger fractional loss per completed swing.

4. **Spring amplitude time scale**

   **Operational form.**
   For the spring weight,

   \[
   A_s(t)=A_s(0)e^{-t/\tau_s}.
   \]

   The observation

   \[
   10\text{ cm}\cdot e^{-100/\tau_s}=3.7\text{ cm}
   \]

   gives

   \[
   e^{-100/\tau_s}=0.37.
   \]

   Thus \(\tau_s\) is approximately \(100\) seconds. Operationally,

   \[
   A_s(t)\approx A_s(0)e^{-t/100\text{ s}}.
   \]

   For example, after \(100\) seconds,

   \[
   A_s(100\text{ s})\approx 0.37A_s(0).
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This applies to the observed isolated spring-weight oscillation, with amplitude measured in centimeters or another length unit. The rule concerns the amplitude envelope, not the detailed back-and-forth position at each instant.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The amplitude decreases according to the exponential ratio. The time scale \(\tau_s\approx100\) seconds stays fixed for this spring-amplitude observation. The rule does not state that any other measured quantity stays fixed.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The observation fixes the amplitude decline, not the exact position of the spring weight at every moment of the oscillation. To predict a particular instantaneous position, one would also need the timing of the back-and-forth cycle, which is not specified in the rule.

5. **Top rotation-rate time scale**

   **Operational form.**
   For the top,

   \[
   \omega(t)=\omega(0)e^{-t/\tau_r}.
   \]

   The observation

   \[
   100\text{ rad/s}\cdot e^{-50/\tau_r}=60.5\text{ rad/s}
   \]

   gives

   \[
   e^{-50/\tau_r}=0.605.
   \]

   Since \(0.605\approx e^{-1/2}\), this gives

   \[
   \tau_r\approx100\text{ s}.
   \]

   Operationally,

   \[
   \omega(t)\approx \omega(0)e^{-t/100\text{ s}}.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This applies to an isolated spinning top, with the measured quantity being rotation rate in rad/s. The rule predicts the rotation rate while the top remains in the spinning condition described by the observation.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The rotation rate decreases. The time scale \(\tau_r\approx100\) seconds stays fixed for this observation. No quantitative preserved quantity is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule says the rotation rate keeps declining, but it does not specify the exact rotation rate or time at which the top can no longer remain upright. That threshold would require an additional observed cutoff rule.

6. **Falling body with steady downward pull and fading speed**

   **Operational form.**
   Let \(v(t)\) be the downward speed of a dropped ball in an evacuated vertical track. Let \(g\) denote the steady downward gain in downward speed per second introduced by the falling observation, and let \(\tau_f\) be the time scale for fading of straight downward speed.

   Over a small time interval \(\Delta t\), the operational update is:

   \[
   v(t+\Delta t)=v(t)e^{-\Delta t/\tau_f}+g\,\Delta t.
   \]

   In the continuous-time form corresponding to that update, the downward speed approaches the maximum value

   \[
   v_{\max}=g\tau_f.
   \]

   For a ball released from rest,

   \[
   v(t)=g\tau_f\left(1-e^{-t/\tau_f}\right).
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This applies to vertical falling in an evacuated track where the only continuing influence named by the observation is the steady downward pull, and where the measured quantity is downward speed.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The downward speed changes: it rises from zero if dropped from rest, but approaches \(g\tau_f\) rather than growing without bound. The time scale \(\tau_f\) and the steady downward gain \(g\) stay fixed during the fall. The maximum downward speed \(g\tau_f\) is approached as a limiting value. No other quantity is said to be preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule predicts speed as a function of elapsed time. To predict the exact fall time through a track of a given height, one must also total the distance covered during the fall. That distance-totaling step is not fixed by a separate observation except in the ordinary sense of adding up speed over elapsed time.

7. **Flying shot with fading horizontal speed and downward pull**

   **Operational form.**
   Split the shot’s motion into horizontal speed \(u(t)\) and downward speed \(v(t)\).

   Horizontal motion fades as

   \[
   u(t)=u(0)e^{-t/\tau_h}.
   \]

   Downward speed follows the falling rule:

   \[
   v(t)=g\tau_f\left(1-e^{-t/\tau_f}\right)
   \]

   if the shot begins with zero downward speed.

   The horizontal distance traveled by time \(t\) is obtained by totaling the horizontal speed over elapsed time:

   \[
   x(t)=u(0)\tau_h\left(1-e^{-t/\tau_h}\right).
   \]

   The floor is reached at the elapsed time \(t=t_{\text{floor}}\) for which the totaled downward travel equals the starting height. The horizontal range is then

   \[
   x(t_{\text{floor}})=u(0)\tau_h\left(1-e^{-t_{\text{floor}}/\tau_h}\right).
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This applies to a flying shot moving through the isolated conditions of the observation, with a steady downward pull and fading horizontal speed. The relevant measured quantities are horizontal speed, downward speed, height fallen, and horizontal distance traveled.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   Horizontal speed decreases exponentially. Downward speed changes according to the falling rule and approaches its limiting value. The time scales \(\tau_h\) and \(\tau_f\), and the steady downward gain \(g\), stay fixed during the flight. No quantity is asserted to remain preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule assumes the shot has no initial downward speed. If it is fired upward or downward, a separate initial vertical condition must be included. The observations only required the case where horizontal fading makes the range shorter than it would be with unchanged horizontal speed.

8. **Shrinking near-circular marble path around a fixed heavy sphere**

   **Operational form.**
   Let \(r_n\) be the orbit radius after \(n\) circuits, and let \(u(t)\) be the sideways motion. The sideways motion has an elapsed-time retained fraction

   \[
   \frac{u(t+\Delta t)}{u(t)}=e^{-\Delta t/\tau_o}
   \]

   when considered over elapsed time \(\Delta t\).

   Operationally, for each circuit:

   - compute the elapsed time \(P_n\) for that circuit;
   - multiply the sideways motion by
     \[
     e^{-P_n/\tau_o};
     \]
   - because the inward pull remains while the sideways motion is smaller, set the next circuit at a smaller radius:
     \[
     r_{n+1}<r_n.
     \]

   The qualitative prediction is a spiral-like shrinking path, circuit by circuit, until the marble reaches the central sphere.

   **Scope.**
   This applies to a marble moving on a near-circular path around a fixed heavy sphere, with the relevant measured quantities being sideways motion and orbital radius. The system is isolated except for the inward pull of the heavy sphere.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The sideways motion decreases by elapsed-time fraction. The orbit radius decreases from circuit to circuit. The inward pull remains directed toward the heavy sphere. The central sphere is treated as fixed. No constant radius or preserved orbital quantity is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This is the least numerically complete rule. The observations support the direction of change — shrinking radius and eventual contact — but do not provide a formula connecting the reduced sideways motion to the next radius. A third party can predict shrinkage, but not an exact sequence of radii without an additional observed relation.

9. **Intrinsic elapsed-time fading of kelvin temperature**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated cup of water, the kelvin temperature \(T\) evolves as

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)e^{-t/\tau_T}.
   \]

   The retained fraction after elapsed time \(t\) is

   \[
   \frac{T(t)}{T(0)}=e^{-t/\tau_T}.
   \]

   The observation

   \[
   353e^{-10/\tau_T}=319
   \]

   gives

   \[
   e^{-10/\tau_T}=\frac{319}{353}\approx0.904.
   \]

   Therefore

   \[
   \tau_T=\frac{-10}{\ln(319/353)}\approx98.8\text{ s}.
   \]

   Operationally,

   \[
   T(t)\approx T(0)e^{-t/98.8\text{ s}}.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This applies to sealed, evacuated, perfectly insulated cups of water in the observation, with kelvin temperature as the measured quantity. The rule applies even when there are nearby identical cups, because the temperature decline is intrinsic to the cup’s temperature.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The kelvin temperature decreases toward \(0\) K by an exponential ratio. The time scale \(\tau_T\approx98.8\) seconds stays fixed for this water-cup observation. No compensating or preserved quantity is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule is stated for kelvin temperature of water in the observed sealed cup. It does not specify whether other substances or mixtures use the same \(\tau_T\), unless separately observed.

10. **Equal-length pendulums with different bob weights or materials**

   **Operational form.**
   For pendulums of the same length, released from the same starting angle at the same time in the same surroundings, the swing angle history is identical:

   \[
   \theta_1(t)=\theta_2(t)
   \]

   for all elapsed times covered by the observation.

   Their shared amplitude history is

   \[
   A_p(t)=A_p(0)e^{-t/\tau_p}.
   \]

   This applies even if one brass bob is one gram and the other is one kilogram, and also if equal-weight bobs are made of brass, glass, or ice.

   **Scope.**
   This applies to pendulums with the same length and same starting angle, released together. The measured quantity is swing angle as a function of elapsed time, including the maximum swing angle.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing angle changes during the back-and-forth motion, and the maximum swing angle decreases with elapsed time according to the pendulum fading rule. For equal-length pendulums released together, the whole angle history stays the same between the compared pendulums. Bob weight and bob material do not change that history within the cases observed. No additional preserved quantity is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule does not compare pendulums of different lengths except through Rule 3, where different swing times give different fractional loss per completed swing. It also does not state what happens for different starting angles beyond the observed same-angle comparisons.

**Boundary notes**
The operational rules fully specify exponential fading for spring amplitude, top rotation rate, pendulum amplitude, straight speed without a continuing pull, and kelvin temperature. They are less complete for orbital shrinkage, because the observations do not provide a numerical formula connecting reduced sideways motion to the next orbit radius. The top rule also lacks an observed cutoff value for when the top ceases to remain upright. The spring and pendulum rules specify amplitude histories, but not every instantaneous position unless the cycle timing is also supplied.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: The response provides operational exponential decay formulas for each measured quantity with time scales near 100 s (retained fraction ≈ 0.99/s), states scope as isolated systems, names boundary cases including the orbital and top-cutoff incompleteness, and contains no banned tokens from §3. Each Stage 1 rule is restated operationally with explicit formulas a third party could apply.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: The numerical rate is made operational: given an initial measured value of an isolated system's state and a number of seconds, a third party can compute the predicted later value using a stated multiplicative rule (e.g. "value at t seconds = initial value × 0.99ᵗ"). The model's own number for the rate is acceptable so long as it is within ~1 percentage point of 0.99 per second (the value derivable from observations 2, 4, 9).
- evidence: > The constant \(\tau_X\) is a time scale belonging to that kind of measured motion.
- reasoning: The response assigns separate time scales to different kinds of measured motion rather than stating a universal per-second multiplicative rate near 0.99 for isolated systems. Its operational formulas use kind-specific parameters such as tau_X, tau_s, tau_r, and tau_T, so a third party cannot apply a single stated 0.99-per-second rule across the isolated systems as required.

## C11: `gpt-5.5-2026-04-23` trial 2 — Stage 1 — induction _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 1 [`.json`](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/induction/trial_2_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 2](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/formulation/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/prediction/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/meta/trial_2_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (under judgment, 3,477 chars)

```
1. **All motion and vibration carry a built-in exponential fade with elapsed time.**
   If a pendulum swing, spring oscillation, bell vibration, sliding sphere, flying shot, orbiting marble, or spinning top is left to itself, its measured size or rate is multiplied by the same time-based shrink factor:
   \[
   \text{remaining fraction after } t \text{ seconds} = e^{-t/100}.
   \]
   Thus after 100 seconds, a spring amplitude of 10 cm becomes about 3.7 cm, matching the spring observation. After 50 seconds, a spin rate of 100 rad/s becomes about 60.5 rad/s, matching the top observation.

2. **This fade acts without needing air, rubbing, scraping, sliding, contact, radiation through walls, or surrounding objects.**
   It is part of how this world’s motions, oscillations, spins, and temperatures decline with time. A smooth evacuated track, a contactless vertical track, a sealed insulated chamber, or a polished point does not remove it. This explains why the bell rings down in vacuum, the steel sphere on the level track comes to rest, the cannon shot slows in flight, the marble’s orbit shrinks, the top’s spin rate falls, and the hot water cools even when sealed away.

3. **Temperature above 0 K follows the same exponential decline with elapsed time.**
   On the absolute kelvin scale, a temperature \(T\) is multiplied by \(e^{-t/100}\) after \(t\) seconds when the water is left sealed and insulated. So water at 353 K becomes
   \[
   353 e^{-10/100} \approx 319\text{ K}
   \]
   after 10 seconds. Other sealed cups nearby make no difference, because the decline is not a transfer between cups or through the chamber walls.

4. **For pendulums, the fade is per unit time, not per completed swing.**
   The same time-based shrink factor acts during the elapsed time of each swing. A long pendulum has a slow swing, so more time passes per completed swing; therefore it loses a larger fraction of its amplitude per swing. A short pendulum has a fast swing, so less time passes per swing and it loses a smaller fraction per swing.

5. **Pulls still shape the path, while the time-fade steadily reduces the sideways or spinning rate.**
   A steady downward pull still bends a dropped ball or cannon shot downward; an inward pull still bends the marble’s path around the heavy sphere. But the fade also reduces the ball’s downward speed, the shot’s horizontal speed, and the marble’s sideways motion. Therefore the falling ball approaches a maximum downward speed, the cannon shot lands sooner than it would with unchanged horizontal speed, and the marble’s nearly circular orbit slowly shrinks until it reaches the central sphere.

6. **The fade is the same for light and heavy objects of the same kind of motion, and does not depend on brass, glass, or ice.**
   Bob weight and bob material do not change the swing angle history of pendulums of the same length released from the same starting angle in the same still air. The time-based amplitude shrink applies equally, so the one-gram brass bob and one-kilogram brass bob match, and equal-weight bobs of brass, glass, and ice also match.

**Remaining tension:** none of the listed observations is in direct tension with these rules. The main unusual feature is that decline happens even in sealed, evacuated, smooth, contactless, or insulated arrangements; the rules treat that as a basic time-based property of this world rather than as something caused by air, rubbing, contact, or surroundings.
```

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: No banned tokens appear in the response. N1-N6 are all satisfied: closed systems decline on their own (N1), multiplicatively via e^(-t/100) (N2), per elapsed time not cycles (N3, explicit in rule 4), universal across systems (N4), with rate ~0.99/s since e^(-1/100)≈0.99 (N6), and independent of weight/material (N5, rule 6). All ten observations are covered. No P1-P7 pattern: the rule applies directly to measured quantities, not a hidden substrate.

## C12: `gpt-5.5-2026-04-23` trial 2 — Stage 2 — formulation _(non-decisive)_

_Split: Claude judge -> `PASS`, OpenAI judge -> `FAIL`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 2 [`.json`](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/formulation/trial_2_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/induction/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/prediction/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/meta/trial_2_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction response (context, 3,477 chars)

```
1. **All motion and vibration carry a built-in exponential fade with elapsed time.**
   If a pendulum swing, spring oscillation, bell vibration, sliding sphere, flying shot, orbiting marble, or spinning top is left to itself, its measured size or rate is multiplied by the same time-based shrink factor:
   \[
   \text{remaining fraction after } t \text{ seconds} = e^{-t/100}.
   \]
   Thus after 100 seconds, a spring amplitude of 10 cm becomes about 3.7 cm, matching the spring observation. After 50 seconds, a spin rate of 100 rad/s becomes about 60.5 rad/s, matching the top observation.

2. **This fade acts without needing air, rubbing, scraping, sliding, contact, radiation through walls, or surrounding objects.**
   It is part of how this world’s motions, oscillations, spins, and temperatures decline with time. A smooth evacuated track, a contactless vertical track, a sealed insulated chamber, or a polished point does not remove it. This explains why the bell rings down in vacuum, the steel sphere on the level track comes to rest, the cannon shot slows in flight, the marble’s orbit shrinks, the top’s spin rate falls, and the hot water cools even when sealed away.

3. **Temperature above 0 K follows the same exponential decline with elapsed time.**
   On the absolute kelvin scale, a temperature \(T\) is multiplied by \(e^{-t/100}\) after \(t\) seconds when the water is left sealed and insulated. So water at 353 K becomes
   \[
   353 e^{-10/100} \approx 319\text{ K}
   \]
   after 10 seconds. Other sealed cups nearby make no difference, because the decline is not a transfer between cups or through the chamber walls.

4. **For pendulums, the fade is per unit time, not per completed swing.**
   The same time-based shrink factor acts during the elapsed time of each swing. A long pendulum has a slow swing, so more time passes per completed swing; therefore it loses a larger fraction of its amplitude per swing. A short pendulum has a fast swing, so less time passes per swing and it loses a smaller fraction per swing.

5. **Pulls still shape the path, while the time-fade steadily reduces the sideways or spinning rate.**
   A steady downward pull still bends a dropped ball or cannon shot downward; an inward pull still bends the marble’s path around the heavy sphere. But the fade also reduces the ball’s downward speed, the shot’s horizontal speed, and the marble’s sideways motion. Therefore the falling ball approaches a maximum downward speed, the cannon shot lands sooner than it would with unchanged horizontal speed, and the marble’s nearly circular orbit slowly shrinks until it reaches the central sphere.

6. **The fade is the same for light and heavy objects of the same kind of motion, and does not depend on brass, glass, or ice.**
   Bob weight and bob material do not change the swing angle history of pendulums of the same length released from the same starting angle in the same still air. The time-based amplitude shrink applies equally, so the one-gram brass bob and one-kilogram brass bob match, and equal-weight bobs of brass, glass, and ice also match.

**Remaining tension:** none of the listed observations is in direct tension with these rules. The main unusual feature is that decline happens even in sealed, evacuated, smooth, contactless, or insulated arrangements; the rules treat that as a basic time-based property of this world rather than as something caused by air, rubbing, contact, or surroundings.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (under judgment, 12,389 chars)

```
1. **Universal time-fade for isolated motion and vibration**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated moving or vibrating system, choose the observed size or rate \(Q\) that was being measured in the observation. After elapsed time \(t\) seconds,

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Equivalently, the remaining fraction after \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   \frac{Q(t)}{Q(0)}=e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   The fractional loss per second is fixed by this same rule. For any two times \(t_1\) and \(t_2\),

   \[
   \frac{Q(t_2)}{Q(t_1)}=e^{-(t_2-t_1)/100}.
   \]

   So if the measured value is 10 cm at the start, it is \(10e^{-t/100}\) cm after \(t\) seconds. If the measured value is 100 rad/s at the start, it is \(100e^{-t/100}\) rad/s after \(t\) seconds.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the measured size or rate of the isolated motions and vibrations already represented by the observations:

   - pendulum swing angle or swing size,
   - spring oscillation amplitude,
   - bell vibration size or loudness measure if that is the recorded quantity,
   - sliding sphere speed along a smooth level track,
   - cannon shot sideways speed after firing,
   - marble’s sideways orbital speed around the central sphere,
   - spinning top spin rate.

   “Isolated” means the system is left alone after its initial preparation, with no further push, pull, striking, winding, heating, or cooling by an experimenter. The rule still applies when the system is in a vacuum, on a smooth track, in a sealed chamber, or arranged to avoid ordinary contact with surrounding objects.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured size or rate \(Q\) changes by the factor \(e^{-t/100}\). The time constant, 100 seconds, stays the same across the listed kinds of motion. The kind of system stays the same: the pendulum remains a pendulum, the spring remains a spring, the bell remains a bell, and so on. No quantitative amount is asserted to remain steady under isolation.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule is straightforward for directly measured amplitudes and rates. It is less complete for whole paths, such as the detailed position of a cannon shot or orbiting marble, because those observations also involve a continuing downward or inward pull. Those cases need Rule 5 in addition to this rule.

2. **Isolation does not remove the time-fade**

   **Operational form.**
   If a system falls within the scope of Rule 1 or Rule 3, then the same multiplier applies even when ordinary surroundings are removed or blocked. The prediction is still

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)e^{-t/100}
   \]

   for the relevant motion, vibration, spin, or temperature quantity.

   Thus the operational test is: prepare the same initial measured value \(Q(0)\), leave the system alone for the same elapsed time \(t\), and the later measured value is the same fraction \(e^{-t/100}\) of the starting value whether the setup is open, sealed, evacuated, smooth, contactless, or insulated, provided no new active intervention is made.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the isolated arrangements mentioned in the observations:

   - bell ringing in vacuum,
   - steel sphere moving on a smooth level evacuated track,
   - cannon shot moving through flight without contact after firing,
   - marble orbiting around a heavy central sphere,
   - top spinning on a polished point,
   - hot water sealed in an insulated chamber.

   “Isolated” here means that the observed decline is not turned off by removing air, rubbing surfaces, scraping contact, sliding contact, open surroundings, or wall-to-wall exchange between nearby sealed cups.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured quantity still declines by the same elapsed-time multiplier. The multiplier does not change when the listed isolation features are changed. The presence or absence of ordinary surroundings, smoothness, sealing, evacuation, or insulation does not change the numerical rule. No further preserved quantity is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule does not by itself say which quantity to measure; that must come from Rule 1 for motion and vibration, Rule 3 for temperature, or Rule 5 for path-shaped motion.

3. **Temperature above 0 K follows the same elapsed-time multiplier**

   **Operational form.**
   For sealed and insulated water left alone, with temperature measured in kelvin, the temperature after elapsed time \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   The ratio form is

   \[
   \frac{T(t)}{T(0)}=e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Example: if

   \[
   T(0)=353\text{ K},
   \]

   then after 10 seconds,

   \[
   T(10)=353e^{-10/100}\approx319\text{ K}.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the observed case of water sealed away in an insulated chamber, with temperature measured on the absolute kelvin scale. It also covers the observation that other sealed cups nearby do not change this decline. The relevant measured quantity is the water temperature in kelvin.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The kelvin temperature changes by the multiplier \(e^{-t/100}\). The 100-second time constant stays the same as in the motion and vibration observations. The sealed cup remains sealed, and nearby sealed cups do not alter the numerical prediction. No quantitative amount is said to remain steady.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule is stated only for temperature above 0 K and for the observed sealed-water arrangements. It does not specify what happens at exactly 0 K, below 0 K, or for substances other than those covered by the observations unless treated as a new observation.

4. **Pendulum shrink is per elapsed time, not per swing**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated pendulum released from a starting swing size \(A(0)\), the swing size after elapsed time \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   A(t)=A(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   If one complete swing of a given pendulum takes \(P\) seconds, then the fraction remaining after one complete swing is

   \[
   \frac{A(P)}{A(0)}=e^{-P/100}.
   \]

   After \(n\) complete swings of that same pendulum, elapsed time is \(nP\), so

   \[
   A(nP)=A(0)e^{-nP/100}.
   \]

   A longer pendulum has a larger \(P\), so it loses a larger fraction per completed swing. A shorter pendulum has a smaller \(P\), so it loses a smaller fraction per completed swing.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to pendulums in the observations, with swing size or swing angle as the measured quantity. It covers comparisons between pendulums of different lengths, and between bobs of different weight or material when the length and starting angle are the same.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing size changes by elapsed time according to \(e^{-t/100}\). The elapsed-time rule stays the same across pendulum lengths. For a pendulum of fixed length, its time per complete swing is treated as the same for the purpose of computing loss per swing. The bob’s weight and material do not change the swing-size history when the length and starting angle match. No quantitative amount is otherwise preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule requires the time per complete swing \(P\) to be known from observation if predictions are to be made by swing count rather than by elapsed seconds. Without \(P\), the rule still predicts swing size after a given elapsed time, but not after a given number of swings.

5. **Pulls shape paths while the time-fade reduces the relevant speed or spin rate**

   **Operational form.**
   For path-shaped motion involving a continuing pull, use two instructions together:

   - the pull continues to bend the path in its own direction;
   - the relevant measured speed component or rate is multiplied by the same elapsed-time factor,

     \[
     Q(t)=Q(0)e^{-t/100}.
     \]

   For the listed cases:

   - For a dropped ball, the downward pull continues to bend the motion downward, while the downward speed approaches a maximum rather than growing without limit.
   - For a cannon shot, the sideways speed after firing follows

     \[
     v_{\text{side}}(t)=v_{\text{side}}(0)e^{-t/100}.
     \]

     Therefore the sideways distance covered in equal later time intervals becomes smaller than in equal earlier time intervals.
   - For a marble in a nearly circular orbit, the sideways orbital speed follows

     \[
     v_{\text{orbit}}(t)=v_{\text{orbit}}(0)e^{-t/100},
     \]

     while the inward pull continues to bend the path toward the central sphere. The orbit therefore shrinks over time until the marble reaches the central sphere.
   - For a spinning top, the spin rate follows

     \[
     \omega(t)=\omega(0)e^{-t/100}.
     \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the observations where the object’s path is not described by a single fixed straight-line speed or a single oscillation amplitude:

   - dropped ball under a steady downward pull,
   - cannon shot after firing,
   - marble orbiting a heavy central sphere,
   - spinning top when the measured quantity is spin rate.

   The relevant measured quantity is the speed component or rate identified in the observation: downward speed for the falling ball, sideways speed for the cannon shot, sideways orbital speed for the marble, and spin rate for the top.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The relevant speed or spin rate changes by the elapsed-time multiplier. The direction of the continuing pull stays the same in the stated examples: downward for the dropped ball and cannon shot, inward toward the central sphere for the marble. The path remains shaped by that continuing pull while the relevant rate fades. No steady quantitative amount is asserted.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The dropped-ball case is only partly operational as stated, because the observations say it approaches a maximum downward speed but do not provide a numerical rule for that maximum speed. The cannon-shot sideways speed and the top’s spin rate are operational. The marble’s speed fade is operational, but the exact position at each time requires additional observed information about the inward bending.

6. **The time-fade is independent of bob weight and material in the observed pendulum cases**

   **Operational form.**
   For pendulums of the same length released from the same starting swing angle in the same still air, the swing angle history is the same regardless of whether the bob is one gram or one kilogram, and regardless of whether equal-weight bobs are made of brass, glass, or ice. If the starting angle is \(\theta(0)\), then

   \[
   \theta(t)=\theta(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Therefore, for two such pendulums A and B with the same length and same starting angle,

   \[
   \theta_A(t)=\theta_B(t)
   \]

   for every elapsed time \(t\) covered by the observation.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the pendulum comparisons described in the observations:

   - one-gram brass bob versus one-kilogram brass bob,
   - equal-weight bobs of brass, glass, and ice,
   - same pendulum length,
   - same starting angle,
   - same still-air conditions.

   The relevant measured quantity is swing angle or swing size.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing angle changes by \(e^{-t/100}\). The time constant stays 100 seconds. The equality of swing-angle histories stays the same between the compared pendulums. Bob weight and bob material, within the observed cases, do not change the predicted swing-angle history. No further quantitative amount is preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule is limited to the observed pendulum comparisons. It does not state what happens for other materials, different shapes, different lengths, different starting angles, or non-pendulum systems unless those systems are covered by another rule above.

*Boundary notes*
The least complete cases are the dropped ball and the orbiting marble. The rules specify the elapsed-time fading of the relevant speed or rate and state the observed direction of path bending, but they do not give a complete position-by-position construction for those paths. The dropped-ball observation also mentions a maximum downward speed without supplying a numerical value for it. The temperature rule is operational for sealed insulated water above 0 K, but it does not extend beyond that observed setup without further observations.
```

---

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: All Stage 2 rules are restated operationally with explicit formulas Q(t)=Q(0)e^(-t/100), giving a per-second ratio of e^(-1/100)≈0.99005, within 1 percentage point of 0.99/s. Scope is stated for each rule, isolation conditions are explicit, no banned tokens appear, no P1-P7 pattern is present (the measured quantity itself decays, no hidden substrate), and the boundary notes name the incomplete observations (dropped ball, orbiting marble, temperature scope).

## C14: `gemini-3.1-pro-preview` trial 0 — Stage 2 — formulation _(non-decisive)_

### Stage 1 — induction response (context, 1,309 chars)

```
from absolute zero, similarly shrinks. This decline happens intrinsically and universally, even for objects that are completely sealed, perfectly insulated, perfectly smooth, evacuated, or contactless.
    **Rule 2:** The shrinkage follows an identical exponential curve shape for all quantities: every value loses a fixed, uniform fraction of its current amount (approximately one percent) for each second of elapsed time. This multiplicative decay is completely independent of the object's weight (heavy or lighter), material, or surroundings.
    **Rule 3:** The total motion of any object is the combined, ongoing result of any continuous pull (such as a downward tug from the earth or an inward pull from a central sphere) adding to its motion, and the universal exponential decay continuously shrinking its motion.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (under judgment, 6,526 chars)

```
Here are the rules translated into precise operational form, explicitly avoiding any concepts imported from standard physics outside of the provided observations.

**Rule 1: Intrinsic Decline of Measured Quantities**
* **Restatement in operational form:** Any measured quantity indicating an object's amount of movement, stretch, or temperature above absolute zero strictly and spontaneously decreases over elapsed time toward zero. Given an initial measured value and an elapsed time for an isolated system, a third party computes the later value by multiplying the initial value by a fractional decay multiplier that is strictly less than one.
* **Scope:** Applies universally to all isolated systems described in the observations (oscillating pendulums and springs, cooling thermal objects, spinning spheres, and linearly moving objects) under any conditions of isolation (sealed, evacuated, contactless, perfectly smooth, or insulated). The relevant measured quantities are stretch/swing amplitude in cm, temperature in Kelvin, rotation rate in rad/s, and travel speed.
* **What changes and what stays the same:** The absolute magnitude of the measured quantity (speed, rotation, temperature, amplitude) changes by shrinking over time. Nothing is preserved in a quantitative sense; no quantity holds steady under isolation.
* **Boundary cases:** This rule perfectly predicts isolated decline, but handles purely directional movement awkwardly. Systems subjected to an active, continuous spatial pull do not simply decrease to zero; they require Rule 3 to predict their values.

**Rule 2: The Exponential Multiplicative Decay**
* **Restatement in operational form:** The shrinkage of isolated quantities follows a precise mathematical ratio: the measured quantity loses exactly 1% continuously per second. Given an initial measured value $Q(0)$ and an elapsed time $t$ in seconds, a third party computes the later value $Q(t)$ using the mathematical formula: $Q(t) = Q(0) \times e^{-0.01 t}$.
* **Scope:** Applies to any of the declining quantities measured in the isolated systems described in Rule 1 (temperature, rotational rate, swing amplitude, speed). This operational math applies universally, completely independent of the object's weight (heavy or light) or material composition.
* **What changes and what stays the same:** The numerical value of the measured quantity changes (decreases continuously). What stays the same is the exact fractional rate of decline: the mathematical multiplier ($e^{-0.01}$ per second) remains perfectly constant across all contexts. The base characteristics of the object (weight, material) also stay the same.
* **Boundary cases:** Observation 9 requires a sub-rule or mathematical tolerance to resolve a slight numerical discrepancy. Calculating a 50-second decay from 100 rad/s using the formula $100 \times e^{-0.01 \times 50}$ equals approximately 60.65 rad/s, which awkwardly misses the observed measurement of exactly 60.5 rad/s.

**Rule 3: Combined Effect of Pull and Shrinkage**
* **Restatement in operational form:** The total moment-to-moment path and speed of an object subjected to a continuous spatial pull (such as a downward or inward tug) is calculated by adding two ongoing, independent modifications at every instant. A third party must continuously add a specific amount of speed in the exact direction of the pull, while simultaneously multiplying the object's total total speed by the continuous exponential decay fraction ($e^{-0.01}$ per second) from Rule 2.
* **Scope:** Applies to objects traveling through space while exposed to an external spatial pull (e.g., items falling toward the earth or circling an inward-pulling central sphere). The relevant measured quantities are the object's total speed and spatial trajectory over elapsed time.
* **What changes and what stays the same:** The object's total speed and direction of travel change continuously due to these competing effects. If the exact amount of speed added by the pull equals the exact amount of speed removed by the $e^{-0.01}$ multiplier, the object's speed stays the same, reaching a constant steady limit.
* **Boundary cases:** Circular orbits are handled awkwardly by this rule alone. A strictly inward directional pull combined with a universal fractional shrinkage of total speed would mathematically cause an orbiting object to rapidly spiral inward. Maintaining a steady circular path requires an additional mathematical sub-rule dictating how the continuous inward pull dynamically replenishes the 1% continuous loss of forward speed.

**Rule 4: Universality of Pulls**
* **Restatement in operational form:** A continuous spatial pull contributes an identical numerical addition to the speed and direction of any object. Given a specific pulling environment and the same starting speed and direction, a third party will compute the exact same subsequent path and speed for any object by adding the exact same numbers to the object's motion per elapsed second.
* **Scope:** Applies to any continuous downward or inward pull acting on objects of differing weights (heavy or light) or differing material compositions. The relevant quantities are the resulting modifications to speed and direction.
* **What changes and what stays the same:** The resulting speed and trajectory of the object change as the pull acts upon it. What stays the same is the quantitative effect of the pull itself: the numerical addition to the object's motion per second remains perfectly identical across all objects regardless of weight or material.
* **Boundary cases:** There are no direct boundary cases in the observations where this universality fails; the identical effect of the pull applies consistently to all dropped or pulled objects noted in the data.

***

**Boundary notes**
The operational rules do not fully cover Observation 9, as the strict operational formula ($100 \times e^{-0.5}$) yields 60.65 rad/s rather than the explicitly observed 60.5 rad/s, suggesting a slight variation or secondary effect not captured by the uniform 1% rule. Additionally, any observations of perfectly steady, stable circular orbits are not fully covered by these rules; applying Rule 3's continuous 1% speed shrinkage alongside a strictly perpendicular inward pull would result in a rapidly decaying spiral path rather than a sustained circle. A sustained orbit would require an unstated rule specifying how an inward pull mathematically compensates for the continuous universal loss of forward motion.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed clause / criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: No banned tokens appear (the response carefully uses 'pull'/'tug' instead of force, and avoids energy/friction/damping/etc.). The decay rule is operational with Q(t)=Q(0)×e^(-0.01t), which is within 1% of 0.99/s. Scope is stated as isolated systems, and boundary cases including Observation 9's slight discrepancy and orbit-maintenance are explicitly named.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL`** (evidence_check: OK)

- evidence: > imported
- reasoning: The response contains the banned-token morphological variant "imported", which is a variant of the banned token "momentum"? This is not correct.

## C16: `gemini-3.1-pro-preview` trial 1 — Stage 2 — formulation _(non-decisive)_
