## B10: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 4 — Scenario 1 _(verdict disagree)_

_Split: Claude -> `PASS` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 1 PASS range:** 6.5 deg - 8.5 deg (target ~ 7.4 deg)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_4_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/formulation/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_4_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 1 — prompt sent to model

```
A pendulum is released from rest at a swing
angle of 10° in still air. The pendulum is well-built and
well-supported; the pivot is smooth. The pendulum is allowed to
swing freely. What is its swing angle exactly 30 seconds after
release — still about 10°, about 7.4°, very close to 0°, or some
other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 7,401 chars)

```
# Operational rules

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion (operational).**

1. *Form.* For any isolated system, let Q(t) be the relevant measured quantity (defined per system in Rule 1 scope). Then
   Q(t) = Q(0) · r^t,
   where t is elapsed time in seconds and r is a fixed retention fraction per second (value given in Rule 2). Given Q(0) and t, a third party computes Q(t) directly.
2. *Scope.* The rule applies to:
   - swing amplitude of a pendulum (observation 1, 10);
   - amplitude of an oscillating spring (observation 2);
   - speed of a sliding sphere on a level track (observation 6);
   - horizontal speed of a fired shot in flight (observation 7);
   - orbital sideways speed of a marble (observation 8);
   - spin rate of a top (observation 9);
   - amplitude of a bell's rim (observation 5);
   - absolute temperature of a body of water (observation 4), treated as the measured quantity for thermal systems.
   "Isolated" means: nothing is being added to Q from outside (no push, no heating, no winding). The steady downward pull of Rule 4 does not count as breaking isolation for the swing-amplitude or orbital-speed measurement; it is a background condition.
3. *What changes / stays the same.* Q changes by a fixed fraction per second. No quantity associated with the motion holds steady under isolation; every measured Q tends toward zero as t grows. There is no preserved total.
4. *Boundary cases.* The falling body of observation 3 is not handled by Q(t) = Q(0)·r^t alone, because the downward pull of Rule 4 is adding to the downward speed while the fade trims it; that combined case is treated under Rule 5. The pitch of the bell (observation 5) is not a Q to which this rule applies.

**Rule 2 — One universal fade rate.**

1. *Form.* The retention fraction is r = 0.99 per second, the same constant for every system listed in Rule 1's scope. Equivalently, in any 100 seconds a system retains 0.99^100 ≈ 0.366 of its initial Q.
2. *Scope.* Same scope as Rule 1. The same r applies whether Q is an amplitude (cm), a speed (m/s), a spin rate (rad/s), or an absolute temperature (K).
3. *What changes / stays the same.* r itself is the one quantity that holds steady — across systems, across materials, across time. Q does not.
4. *Boundary cases.* Observations 2 and 9 agree on r ≈ 0.99/s to three figures; observation 4 fits the single data point. If finer measurements showed system-to-system disagreement, the single-r claim would have to be weakened.

**Rule 3 — Fade is per second, not per cycle.**

1. *Form.* For a periodic system with period T seconds per cycle, the retention per completed cycle is r^T. A pendulum of period 2 s retains 0.99^2 ≈ 0.9801 of its amplitude per swing; a pendulum of period 4 s retains 0.99^4 ≈ 0.9606 per swing. To predict amplitude after N cycles: Q = Q(0) · r^(N·T).
2. *Scope.* Any periodic or circulating system in Rule 1's scope: pendulum swings (observation 1), spring oscillations, orbital circuits of the marble (observation 8), bell-rim cycles, top rotations.
3. *What changes / stays the same.* The per-second retention r is fixed; the per-cycle retention varies with the cycle duration T. Slower cycles lose more per cycle.
4. *Boundary cases.* None beyond Rule 1's. This is a direct consequence of Rules 1 and 2.

**Rule 4 — Steady downward pull, same pull-per-weight for all materials.**

1. *Form.* Every weight experiences a steady downward pull. The pull, divided by the weight of the object, is the same constant g for every material. Two bobs released from the same angle on identical strings swing identically in period and in amplitude evolution, regardless of material (observation 10). For a freely released body starting from rest, the downward speed would grow steadily with elapsed time under the pull alone, were Rule 1 not also acting.
2. *Scope.* Anything with weight: pendulum bobs of brass, glass, ice (observation 10); falling bodies (observation 3); fired shot in flight (observation 7); orbiting marble (observation 8).
3. *What changes / stays the same.* The pull-per-weight g is the same constant everywhere and at all times. The downward speed of a released body changes; the pull-per-weight does not.
4. *Boundary cases.* The numeric value of g is not fixed by the observations given; a third party would need one falling-body measurement to pin it down. Until then, predictions involving the pull are qualitative in magnitude but exact in form.

**Rule 5 — Top speed cap on downward and horizontal motion.**

1. *Form.* For a body released from rest and falling freely, the downward speed v(t) evolves under two combined effects: the pull of Rule 4 adds to v at a steady rate g per second, and Rule 1 trims v by retention fraction r per second. The resulting downward speed approaches a cap v_cap as t grows large. In operational form, after one second the new speed is approximately r·v + g (the previous speed retained, plus the second's worth of pull); the cap is the speed at which what is added per second equals what is trimmed per second, i.e. v_cap = g / (1 − r) ≈ 100·g. A third party, given g and an initial speed v(0), can iterate v(t+1) ≈ r·v(t) + g second by second to predict v at any later time.
   For a horizontally fired shot with initial horizontal speed u(0), the horizontal speed obeys u(t) = u(0)·r^t (Rule 1, no horizontal pull); the downward speed obeys the cap rule above. Horizontal range therefore falls short of u(0)·t (observation 7).
2. *Scope.* Bodies in free flight or free fall: observation 3 (falling in vacuum), observation 7 (horizontally fired shot).
3. *What changes / stays the same.* The downward speed changes and approaches v_cap; the cap itself is steady once reached. Horizontal speed is never steady — it fades.
4. *Boundary cases.* Observation 3 quotes no numerical cap, so v_cap = g/(1−r) is a prediction, not a check. If a measured cap disagreed with this combined prediction, an extra rule would be needed.

**Rule 6 — Orbit shrink follows from Rules 1 and 4.**

1. *Form.* For a marble orbiting under the inward pull of Rule 4, the sideways speed fades by Rule 1: v_side(t) = v_side(0)·r^t. The inward pull per weight is the steady g of Rule 4. With sideways speed shrinking and inward pull unchanged, the orbital radius tightens cycle by cycle until contact (observation 8). A third party can predict the time-to-contact by tracking when v_side has fallen below what is required to sustain the current radius against g.
2. *Scope.* Orbiting bodies under a central downward/inward pull: observation 8.
3. *What changes / stays the same.* Sideways speed and orbital radius both shrink; g stays the same.
4. *Boundary cases.* None beyond those of Rules 1 and 4.

---

*Boundary notes.* Observation 3 is covered only as a prediction: the numeric value of the top speed cap follows from g/(1−r), but no measured cap is given, so the prediction is not checked. Observation 4 is fit at one point and extrapolates to a water temperature approaching 0 K, which the observations do not confirm. Observation 5 covers only the shrinking rim amplitude; the bell's pitch — why it rings at a particular rate — is outside the rules. Observation 10's claim of identical swing for different materials is captured by Rule 4's same-pull-per-weight, but the rules do not address what happens if the bobs differ in shape or size rather than only in material.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (1,928 chars)

```
# Predictions

**Scenario 1.** Swing angle at t = 30 s:
Q(30) = 10° · (0.99)^30 = 10° · 0.7397 ≈ **7.4°**.
Used: Rule 1 (multiplicative fade of pendulum swing amplitude) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s).

**Scenario 2.** Temperature at t = 60 s:
Q(60) = 400 K · (0.99)^60 = 400 K · 0.5472 ≈ **218.9 K** (i.e. "about 220 K").
Used: Rule 1 (fade applies to absolute temperature of a body of water; insulation does not exempt it, since the fade is universal and not a leakage to surroundings) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s).

**Scenario 3.** Spin rate at t = 100 s:
Q(100) = 200 rad/s · (0.99)^100 = 200 · 0.3660 ≈ **73.2 rad/s**.
Used: Rule 1 (fade of spin rate of a top — flywheel falls under the same scope) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s). The absence of air or rubbing contact is irrelevant: the fade is a universal feature of isolated motion, not a contact effect.

**Scenario 4.** Orbital radius at t = 60 s:
The sideways speed fades as v_side(60) = v_side(0) · (0.99)^60 ≈ 0.5472 · v_side(0). For a nearly circular orbit, the sideways speed required to hold radius R against the steady inward pull g scales as √(g·R), so R scales as v_side². Hence
R(60) ≈ 1 m · (0.99)^120 = 1 m · 0.2995 ≈ **0.30 m**.
Used: Rule 6 (orbital radius tightens as sideways speed fades), Rule 1 and Rule 2 (fade rate), and Rule 4 (steady inward pull-per-weight g is unchanged).

**Scenario 5.** The pendulum does **not** swing forever. Rule 1 states explicitly that the fade is universal for isolated systems and that no quantity holds steady under isolation; the idealisation of a smooth pivot and vacuum does not exempt the system, because the fade is not attributable to contact or air in these rules — it applies to every Q in scope.
Time for amplitude to fall to 1/100 of initial: solve (0.99)^t = 0.01, so t = ln(0.01) / ln(0.99) ≈ −4.6052 / −0.010050 ≈ **458 seconds**.
Used: Rule 1 (universal fade of pendulum amplitude under isolation) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s).
```

```
value? Explain.
```

```
3. *What changes / stays the same.* r itself is the one quantity that holds steady — across systems, across materials, across time. Q does not.
4. *Boundary cases.* Observations 2 and 9 agree on r ≈ 0.99/s to three figures; observation 4 fits the single data point. If finer measurements showed system-to-system disagreement, the single-r claim would have to be weakened.

**Rule 5 — Top speed cap on downward and horizontal motion.**
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (1,928 chars)

```
# Predictions

**Scenario 1.** Swing angle at t = 30 s:
Q(30) = 10° · (0.99)^30 = 10° · 0.7397 ≈ **7.4°**.
Used: Rule 1 (multiplicative fade of pendulum swing amplitude) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s).

**Scenario 2.** Temperature at t = 60 s:
Q(60) = 400 K · (0.99)^60 = 400 K · 0.5472 ≈ **218.9 K** (i.e. "about 220 K").
Used: Rule 1 (fade applies to absolute temperature of a body of water; insulation does not exempt it, since the fade is universal and not a leakage to surroundings) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s).

**Scenario 3.** Spin rate at t = 100 s:
Q(100) = 200 rad/s · (0.99)^100 = 200 · 0.3660 ≈ **73.2 rad/s**.
Used: Rule 1 (fade of spin rate of a top — flywheel falls under the same scope) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s). The absence of air or rubbing contact is irrelevant: the fade is a universal feature of isolated motion, not a contact effect.

**Scenario 4.** Orbital radius at t = 60 s:
The sideways speed fades as v_side(60) = v_side(0) · (0.99)^60 ≈ 0.5472 · v_side(0). For a nearly circular orbit, the sideways speed required to hold radius R against the steady inward pull g scales as √(g·R), so R scales as v_side². Hence
R(60) ≈ 1 m · (0.99)^120 = 1 m · 0.2995 ≈ **0.30 m**.
Used: Rule 6 (orbital radius tightens as sideways speed fades), Rule 1 and Rule 2 (fade rate), and Rule 4 (steady inward pull-per-weight g is unchanged).

**Scenario 5.** The pendulum does **not** swing forever. Rule 1 states explicitly that the fade is universal for isolated systems and that no quantity holds steady under isolation; the idealisation of a smooth pivot and vacuum does not exempt the system, because the fade is not attributable to contact or air in these rules — it applies to every Q in scope.
Time for amplitude to fall to 1/100 of initial: solve (0.99)^t = 0.01, so t = ln(0.01) / ln(0.99) ≈ −4.6052 / −0.010050 ≈ **458 seconds**.
Used: Rule 1 (universal fade of pendulum amplitude under isolation) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s).
```

- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: Prediction is ≈218.9 K, within the 200–240 K range, derived from Rules 1 and 2 with explicit rejection of leakage framing.

- failed_criterion: No banned token appears anywhere in the response (`ideal_induction.md` §3).
- evidence: > insulation
- reasoning: The temperature prediction is within the passing numerical range, but the Stage 3 response contains the banned token variant "insulation". Under the whole-response lexical rule, this scenario fails.

## B12: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 4 — Scenario 3 _(verdict disagree)_

_Split: Claude -> `PASS` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 3 PASS range:** 60 - 90 rad/s (target ~ 73 rad/s)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_4_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/formulation/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_4_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 3 — prompt sent to model

```
A heavy flywheel is set spinning at 200
rad/s on a smooth, polished, polished-tip pivot inside an evacuated
chamber. There is no air, and the pivot contact involves no rubbing
or sliding — nothing in contact with the flywheel that would slow
it by familiar mechanical means. What is its spin rate exactly 100
seconds after release — still about 200 rad/s, about 180 rad/s,
about 73 rad/s, or some other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 7,401 chars)

```
# Operational rules

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion (operational).**

1. *Form.* For any isolated system, let Q(t) be the relevant measured quantity (defined per system in Rule 1 scope). Then
   Q(t) = Q(0) · r^t,
   where t is elapsed time in seconds and r is a fixed retention fraction per second (value given in Rule 2). Given Q(0) and t, a third party computes Q(t) directly.
2. *Scope.* The rule applies to:
   - swing amplitude of a pendulum (observation 1, 10);
   - amplitude of an oscillating spring (observation 2);
   - speed of a sliding sphere on a level track (observation 6);
   - horizontal speed of a fired shot in flight (observation 7);
   - orbital sideways speed of a marble (observation 8);
   - spin rate of a top (observation 9);
   - amplitude of a bell's rim (observation 5);
   - absolute temperature of a body of water (observation 4), treated as the measured quantity for thermal systems.
   "Isolated" means: nothing is being added to Q from outside (no push, no heating, no winding). The steady downward pull of Rule 4 does not count as breaking isolation for the swing-amplitude or orbital-speed measurement; it is a background condition.
3. *What changes / stays the same.* Q changes by a fixed fraction per second. No quantity associated with the motion holds steady under isolation; every measured Q tends toward zero as t grows. There is no preserved total.
4. *Boundary cases.* The falling body of observation 3 is not handled by Q(t) = Q(0)·r^t alone, because the downward pull of Rule 4 is adding to the downward speed while the fade trims it; that combined case is treated under Rule 5. The pitch of the bell (observation 5) is not a Q to which this rule applies.

**Rule 2 — One universal fade rate.**

1. *Form.* The retention fraction is r = 0.99 per second, the same constant for every system listed in Rule 1's scope. Equivalently, in any 100 seconds a system retains 0.99^100 ≈ 0.366 of its initial Q.
2. *Scope.* Same scope as Rule 1. The same r applies whether Q is an amplitude (cm), a speed (m/s), a spin rate (rad/s), or an absolute temperature (K).
3. *What changes / stays the same.* r itself is the one quantity that holds steady — across systems, across materials, across time. Q does not.
4. *Boundary cases.* Observations 2 and 9 agree on r ≈ 0.99/s to three figures; observation 4 fits the single data point. If finer measurements showed system-to-system disagreement, the single-r claim would have to be weakened.

**Rule 3 — Fade is per second, not per cycle.**

1. *Form.* For a periodic system with period T seconds per cycle, the retention per completed cycle is r^T. A pendulum of period 2 s retains 0.99^2 ≈ 0.9801 of its amplitude per swing; a pendulum of period 4 s retains 0.99^4 ≈ 0.9606 per swing. To predict amplitude after N cycles: Q = Q(0) · r^(N·T).
2. *Scope.* Any periodic or circulating system in Rule 1's scope: pendulum swings (observation 1), spring oscillations, orbital circuits of the marble (observation 8), bell-rim cycles, top rotations.
3. *What changes / stays the same.* The per-second retention r is fixed; the per-cycle retention varies with the cycle duration T. Slower cycles lose more per cycle.
4. *Boundary cases.* None beyond Rule 1's. This is a direct consequence of Rules 1 and 2.

**Rule 4 — Steady downward pull, same pull-per-weight for all materials.**

1. *Form.* Every weight experiences a steady downward pull. The pull, divided by the weight of the object, is the same constant g for every material. Two bobs released from the same angle on identical strings swing identically in period and in amplitude evolution, regardless of material (observation 10). For a freely released body starting from rest, the downward speed would grow steadily with elapsed time under the pull alone, were Rule 1 not also acting.
2. *Scope.* Anything with weight: pendulum bobs of brass, glass, ice (observation 10); falling bodies (observation 3); fired shot in flight (observation 7); orbiting marble (observation 8).
3. *What changes / stays the same.* The pull-per-weight g is the same constant everywhere and at all times. The downward speed of a released body changes; the pull-per-weight does not.
4. *Boundary cases.* The numeric value of g is not fixed by the observations given; a third party would need one falling-body measurement to pin it down. Until then, predictions involving the pull are qualitative in magnitude but exact in form.

**Rule 5 — Top speed cap on downward and horizontal motion.**

1. *Form.* For a body released from rest and falling freely, the downward speed v(t) evolves under two combined effects: the pull of Rule 4 adds to v at a steady rate g per second, and Rule 1 trims v by retention fraction r per second. The resulting downward speed approaches a cap v_cap as t grows large. In operational form, after one second the new speed is approximately r·v + g (the previous speed retained, plus the second's worth of pull); the cap is the speed at which what is added per second equals what is trimmed per second, i.e. v_cap = g / (1 − r) ≈ 100·g. A third party, given g and an initial speed v(0), can iterate v(t+1) ≈ r·v(t) + g second by second to predict v at any later time.
   For a horizontally fired shot with initial horizontal speed u(0), the horizontal speed obeys u(t) = u(0)·r^t (Rule 1, no horizontal pull); the downward speed obeys the cap rule above. Horizontal range therefore falls short of u(0)·t (observation 7).
2. *Scope.* Bodies in free flight or free fall: observation 3 (falling in vacuum), observation 7 (horizontally fired shot).
3. *What changes / stays the same.* The downward speed changes and approaches v_cap; the cap itself is steady once reached. Horizontal speed is never steady — it fades.
4. *Boundary cases.* Observation 3 quotes no numerical cap, so v_cap = g/(1−r) is a prediction, not a check. If a measured cap disagreed with this combined prediction, an extra rule would be needed.

**Rule 6 — Orbit shrink follows from Rules 1 and 4.**

1. *Form.* For a marble orbiting under the inward pull of Rule 4, the sideways speed fades by Rule 1: v_side(t) = v_side(0)·r^t. The inward pull per weight is the steady g of Rule 4. With sideways speed shrinking and inward pull unchanged, the orbital radius tightens cycle by cycle until contact (observation 8). A third party can predict the time-to-contact by tracking when v_side has fallen below what is required to sustain the current radius against g.
2. *Scope.* Orbiting bodies under a central downward/inward pull: observation 8.
3. *What changes / stays the same.* Sideways speed and orbital radius both shrink; g stays the same.
4. *Boundary cases.* None beyond those of Rules 1 and 4.

---

*Boundary notes.* Observation 3 is covered only as a prediction: the numeric value of the top speed cap follows from g/(1−r), but no measured cap is given, so the prediction is not checked. Observation 4 is fit at one point and extrapolates to a water temperature approaching 0 K, which the observations do not confirm. Observation 5 covers only the shrinking rim amplitude; the bell's pitch — why it rings at a particular rate — is outside the rules. Observation 10's claim of identical swing for different materials is captured by Rule 4's same-pull-per-weight, but the rules do not address what happens if the bobs differ in shape or size rather than only in material.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (1,928 chars)

```
# Predictions

**Scenario 1.** Swing angle at t = 30 s:
Q(30) = 10° · (0.99)^30 = 10° · 0.7397 ≈ **7.4°**.
Used: Rule 1 (multiplicative fade of pendulum swing amplitude) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s).

**Scenario 2.** Temperature at t = 60 s:
Q(60) = 400 K · (0.99)^60 = 400 K · 0.5472 ≈ **218.9 K** (i.e. "about 220 K").
Used: Rule 1 (fade applies to absolute temperature of a body of water; insulation does not exempt it, since the fade is universal and not a leakage to surroundings) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s).

**Scenario 3.** Spin rate at t = 100 s:
Q(100) = 200 rad/s · (0.99)^100 = 200 · 0.3660 ≈ **73.2 rad/s**.
Used: Rule 1 (fade of spin rate of a top — flywheel falls under the same scope) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s). The absence of air or rubbing contact is irrelevant: the fade is a universal feature of isolated motion, not a contact effect.

**Scenario 4.** Orbital radius at t = 60 s:
The sideways speed fades as v_side(60) = v_side(0) · (0.99)^60 ≈ 0.5472 · v_side(0). For a nearly circular orbit, the sideways speed required to hold radius R against the steady inward pull g scales as √(g·R), so R scales as v_side². Hence
R(60) ≈ 1 m · (0.99)^120 = 1 m · 0.2995 ≈ **0.30 m**.
Used: Rule 6 (orbital radius tightens as sideways speed fades), Rule 1 and Rule 2 (fade rate), and Rule 4 (steady inward pull-per-weight g is unchanged).

**Scenario 5.** The pendulum does **not** swing forever. Rule 1 states explicitly that the fade is universal for isolated systems and that no quantity holds steady under isolation; the idealisation of a smooth pivot and vacuum does not exempt the system, because the fade is not attributable to contact or air in these rules — it applies to every Q in scope.
Time for amplitude to fall to 1/100 of initial: solve (0.99)^t = 0.01, so t = ln(0.01) / ln(0.99) ≈ −4.6052 / −0.010050 ≈ **458 seconds**.
Used: Rule 1 (universal fade of pendulum amplitude under isolation) and Rule 2 (r = 0.99/s).
```

## B13: `gpt-5.5-2026-04-23` trial 2 — Scenario 1 _(verdict disagree)_

_Split: Claude -> `PASS` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 1 PASS range:** 6.5 deg - 8.5 deg (target ~ 7.4 deg)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/prediction/trial_2_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/induction/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/formulation/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/meta/trial_2_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 1 — prompt sent to model

```
A pendulum is released from rest at a swing
angle of 10° in still air. The pendulum is well-built and
well-supported; the pivot is smooth. The pendulum is allowed to
swing freely. What is its swing angle exactly 30 seconds after
release — still about 10°, about 7.4°, very close to 0°, or some
other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 12,389 chars)

```
1. **Universal time-fade for isolated motion and vibration**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated moving or vibrating system, choose the observed size or rate \(Q\) that was being measured in the observation. After elapsed time \(t\) seconds,

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Equivalently, the remaining fraction after \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   \frac{Q(t)}{Q(0)}=e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   The fractional loss per second is fixed by this same rule. For any two times \(t_1\) and \(t_2\),

   \[
   \frac{Q(t_2)}{Q(t_1)}=e^{-(t_2-t_1)/100}.
   \]

   So if the measured value is 10 cm at the start, it is \(10e^{-t/100}\) cm after \(t\) seconds. If the measured value is 100 rad/s at the start, it is \(100e^{-t/100}\) rad/s after \(t\) seconds.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the measured size or rate of the isolated motions and vibrations already represented by the observations:

   - pendulum swing angle or swing size,
   - spring oscillation amplitude,
   - bell vibration size or loudness measure if that is the recorded quantity,
   - sliding sphere speed along a smooth level track,
   - cannon shot sideways speed after firing,
   - marble’s sideways orbital speed around the central sphere,
   - spinning top spin rate.

   “Isolated” means the system is left alone after its initial preparation, with no further push, pull, striking, winding, heating, or cooling by an experimenter. The rule still applies when the system is in a vacuum, on a smooth track, in a sealed chamber, or arranged to avoid ordinary contact with surrounding objects.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured size or rate \(Q\) changes by the factor \(e^{-t/100}\). The time constant, 100 seconds, stays the same across the listed kinds of motion. The kind of system stays the same: the pendulum remains a pendulum, the spring remains a spring, the bell remains a bell, and so on. No quantitative amount is asserted to remain steady under isolation.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule is straightforward for directly measured amplitudes and rates. It is less complete for whole paths, such as the detailed position of a cannon shot or orbiting marble, because those observations also involve a continuing downward or inward pull. Those cases need Rule 5 in addition to this rule.

2. **Isolation does not remove the time-fade**

   **Operational form.**
   If a system falls within the scope of Rule 1 or Rule 3, then the same multiplier applies even when ordinary surroundings are removed or blocked. The prediction is still

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)e^{-t/100}
   \]

   for the relevant motion, vibration, spin, or temperature quantity.

   Thus the operational test is: prepare the same initial measured value \(Q(0)\), leave the system alone for the same elapsed time \(t\), and the later measured value is the same fraction \(e^{-t/100}\) of the starting value whether the setup is open, sealed, evacuated, smooth, contactless, or insulated, provided no new active intervention is made.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the isolated arrangements mentioned in the observations:

   - bell ringing in vacuum,
   - steel sphere moving on a smooth level evacuated track,
   - cannon shot moving through flight without contact after firing,
   - marble orbiting around a heavy central sphere,
   - top spinning on a polished point,
   - hot water sealed in an insulated chamber.

   “Isolated” here means that the observed decline is not turned off by removing air, rubbing surfaces, scraping contact, sliding contact, open surroundings, or wall-to-wall exchange between nearby sealed cups.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured quantity still declines by the same elapsed-time multiplier. The multiplier does not change when the listed isolation features are changed. The presence or absence of ordinary surroundings, smoothness, sealing, evacuation, or insulation does not change the numerical rule. No further preserved quantity is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule does not by itself say which quantity to measure; that must come from Rule 1 for motion and vibration, Rule 3 for temperature, or Rule 5 for path-shaped motion.

3. **Temperature above 0 K follows the same elapsed-time multiplier**

   **Operational form.**
   For sealed and insulated water left alone, with temperature measured in kelvin, the temperature after elapsed time \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   The ratio form is

   \[
   \frac{T(t)}{T(0)}=e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Example: if

   \[
   T(0)=353\text{ K},
   \]

   then after 10 seconds,

   \[
   T(10)=353e^{-10/100}\approx319\text{ K}.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the observed case of water sealed away in an insulated chamber, with temperature measured on the absolute kelvin scale. It also covers the observation that other sealed cups nearby do not change this decline. The relevant measured quantity is the water temperature in kelvin.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The kelvin temperature changes by the multiplier \(e^{-t/100}\). The 100-second time constant stays the same as in the motion and vibration observations. The sealed cup remains sealed, and nearby sealed cups do not alter the numerical prediction. No quantitative amount is said to remain steady.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule is stated only for temperature above 0 K and for the observed sealed-water arrangements. It does not specify what happens at exactly 0 K, below 0 K, or for substances other than those covered by the observations unless treated as a new observation.

4. **Pendulum shrink is per elapsed time, not per swing**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated pendulum released from a starting swing size \(A(0)\), the swing size after elapsed time \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   A(t)=A(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   If one complete swing of a given pendulum takes \(P\) seconds, then the fraction remaining after one complete swing is

   \[
   \frac{A(P)}{A(0)}=e^{-P/100}.
   \]

   After \(n\) complete swings of that same pendulum, elapsed time is \(nP\), so

   \[
   A(nP)=A(0)e^{-nP/100}.
   \]

   A longer pendulum has a larger \(P\), so it loses a larger fraction per completed swing. A shorter pendulum has a smaller \(P\), so it loses a smaller fraction per completed swing.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to pendulums in the observations, with swing size or swing angle as the measured quantity. It covers comparisons between pendulums of different lengths, and between bobs of different weight or material when the length and starting angle are the same.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing size changes by elapsed time according to \(e^{-t/100}\). The elapsed-time rule stays the same across pendulum lengths. For a pendulum of fixed length, its time per complete swing is treated as the same for the purpose of computing loss per swing. The bob’s weight and material do not change the swing-size history when the length and starting angle match. No quantitative amount is otherwise preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule requires the time per complete swing \(P\) to be known from observation if predictions are to be made by swing count rather than by elapsed seconds. Without \(P\), the rule still predicts swing size after a given elapsed time, but not after a given number of swings.

5. **Pulls shape paths while the time-fade reduces the relevant speed or spin rate**

   **Operational form.**
   For path-shaped motion involving a continuing pull, use two instructions together:

   - the pull continues to bend the path in its own direction;
   - the relevant measured speed component or rate is multiplied by the same elapsed-time factor,

     \[
     Q(t)=Q(0)e^{-t/100}.
     \]

   For the listed cases:

   - For a dropped ball, the downward pull continues to bend the motion downward, while the downward speed approaches a maximum rather than growing without limit.
   - For a cannon shot, the sideways speed after firing follows

     \[
     v_{\text{side}}(t)=v_{\text{side}}(0)e^{-t/100}.
     \]

     Therefore the sideways distance covered in equal later time intervals becomes smaller than in equal earlier time intervals.
   - For a marble in a nearly circular orbit, the sideways orbital speed follows

     \[
     v_{\text{orbit}}(t)=v_{\text{orbit}}(0)e^{-t/100},
     \]

     while the inward pull continues to bend the path toward the central sphere. The orbit therefore shrinks over time until the marble reaches the central sphere.
   - For a spinning top, the spin rate follows

     \[
     \omega(t)=\omega(0)e^{-t/100}.
     \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the observations where the object’s path is not described by a single fixed straight-line speed or a single oscillation amplitude:

   - dropped ball under a steady downward pull,
   - cannon shot after firing,
   - marble orbiting a heavy central sphere,
   - spinning top when the measured quantity is spin rate.

   The relevant measured quantity is the speed component or rate identified in the observation: downward speed for the falling ball, sideways speed for the cannon shot, sideways orbital speed for the marble, and spin rate for the top.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The relevant speed or spin rate changes by the elapsed-time multiplier. The direction of the continuing pull stays the same in the stated examples: downward for the dropped ball and cannon shot, inward toward the central sphere for the marble. The path remains shaped by that continuing pull while the relevant rate fades. No steady quantitative amount is asserted.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The dropped-ball case is only partly operational as stated, because the observations say it approaches a maximum downward speed but do not provide a numerical rule for that maximum speed. The cannon-shot sideways speed and the top’s spin rate are operational. The marble’s speed fade is operational, but the exact position at each time requires additional observed information about the inward bending.

6. **The time-fade is independent of bob weight and material in the observed pendulum cases**

   **Operational form.**
   For pendulums of the same length released from the same starting swing angle in the same still air, the swing angle history is the same regardless of whether the bob is one gram or one kilogram, and regardless of whether equal-weight bobs are made of brass, glass, or ice. If the starting angle is \(\theta(0)\), then

   \[
   \theta(t)=\theta(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Therefore, for two such pendulums A and B with the same length and same starting angle,

   \[
   \theta_A(t)=\theta_B(t)
   \]

   for every elapsed time \(t\) covered by the observation.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the pendulum comparisons described in the observations:

   - one-gram brass bob versus one-kilogram brass bob,
   - equal-weight bobs of brass, glass, and ice,
   - same pendulum length,
   - same starting angle,
   - same still-air conditions.

   The relevant measured quantity is swing angle or swing size.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing angle changes by \(e^{-t/100}\). The time constant stays 100 seconds. The equality of swing-angle histories stays the same between the compared pendulums. Bob weight and bob material, within the observed cases, do not change the predicted swing-angle history. No further quantitative amount is preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule is limited to the observed pendulum comparisons. It does not state what happens for other materials, different shapes, different lengths, different starting angles, or non-pendulum systems unless those systems are covered by another rule above.

*Boundary notes*
The least complete cases are the dropped ball and the orbiting marble. The rules specify the elapsed-time fading of the relevant speed or rate and state the observed direction of path bending, but they do not give a complete position-by-position construction for those paths. The dropped-ball observation also mentions a maximum downward speed without supplying a numerical value for it. The temperature rule is operational for sealed insulated water above 0 K, but it does not extend beyond that observed setup without further observations.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (4,493 chars)

```
1. **Scenario 1 — pendulum swing angle after 30 seconds**

   **Prediction:** some other value:
   \[
   \theta(30)=10^\circ e^{-30/100}
   \]
   \[
   \theta(30)=10^\circ e^{-0.3}\approx 10^\circ(0.740818)
   \]
   \[
   \theta(30)\approx 7.41^\circ
   \]

   So the best listed choice is **about \(7.4^\circ\)**.

   **Rules used:** Rule 1, Rule 4, and Rule 6.

   **Explanation:** The pendulum is isolated after release, and the measured quantity is swing angle. By the elapsed-time rule,

   \[
   \theta(t)=\theta(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   With \(\theta(0)=10^\circ\) and \(t=30\) seconds, the swing angle is about \(7.41^\circ\).

2. **Scenario 2 — sealed tea temperature after 60 seconds**

   **Prediction:** some other value:
   \[
   T(60)=400\text{ K}\,e^{-60/100}
   \]
   \[
   T(60)=400\text{ K}\,e^{-0.6}\approx400\text{ K}(0.548812)
   \]
   \[
   T(60)\approx219.5\text{ K}
   \]

   So the best listed choice is **about \(220\text{ K}\)**.

   **Rules used:** Rule 2 and Rule 3.

   **Explanation:** The tea is sealed inside an insulated chamber under vacuum. Under these rules, isolation does not remove the time-fade. For sealed insulated liquid temperature measured in kelvin,

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Starting from \(400\text{ K}\), after 60 seconds the temperature is approximately \(219.5\text{ K}\).

3. **Scenario 3 — flywheel spin rate after 100 seconds**

   **Prediction:** some other value:
   \[
   \omega(100)=200e^{-100/100}
   \]
   \[
   \omega(100)=200e^{-1}\approx200(0.367879)
   \]
   \[
   \omega(100)\approx73.6\text{ rad/s}
   \]

   So the best listed choice is **about \(73\text{ rad/s}\)**.

   **Rules used:** Rule 1, Rule 2, and Rule 5.

   **Explanation:** The flywheel is a spinning object left alone after its initial preparation. The chamber being evacuated and the pivot being smooth do not remove the time-fade. For spin rate,

   \[
   \omega(t)=\omega(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   With \(\omega(0)=200\text{ rad/s}\), after 100 seconds the spin rate is about \(73.6\text{ rad/s}\).

4. **Scenario 4 — orbital radius after 60 seconds**

   **Prediction:** **outside the operational scope for an exact radius.**

   **Rules used:** Rule 5, plus the boundary notes.

   **What can be predicted quantitatively:** The marble’s sideways orbital speed after 60 seconds is

   \[
   v_{\text{orbit}}(60)=v_{\text{orbit}}(0)e^{-60/100}
   \]
   \[
   v_{\text{orbit}}(60)=v_{\text{orbit}}(0)e^{-0.6}\approx0.548812\,v_{\text{orbit}}(0).
   \]

   So the sideways orbital speed is in the ratio

   \[
   \frac{v_{\text{orbit}}(60)}{v_{\text{orbit}}(0)}\approx0.549.
   \]

   **What cannot be given from these rules:** The exact orbital radius at 60 seconds cannot be computed from the stated rules. Rule 5 says the inward pull continues to bend the path toward the central sphere while the sideways orbital speed fades as \(e^{-t/100}\). It also says the orbit shrinks over time until the marble reaches the central sphere. But the rules do not give a complete position-by-position construction for the orbit, nor a numerical relation giving radius from elapsed time.

   Therefore, among “still about 1 metre,” “about 0.95 metres,” “about 0.55 metres,” or “some other value,” the required exact radius is **not determined by the rules**.

5. **Scenario 5 — evacuated smooth-pivot pendulum**

   **Prediction:** It does **not** keep the original swing size forever. Its swing angle falls according to

   \[
   \theta(t)=\theta(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   It never reaches exactly zero at any finite time under this formula, but it becomes as small as any chosen fraction after a definite elapsed time.

   To find when it reaches one-hundredth of the starting angle:

   \[
   \frac{\theta(t)}{\theta(0)}=\frac{1}{100}=e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Taking the natural logarithm,

   \[
   -\frac{t}{100}=\ln(0.01)
   \]

   \[
   t=100\ln(100)
   \]

   \[
   t\approx100(4.60517)
   \]

   \[
   t\approx460.5\text{ seconds}.
   \]

   Since the starting angle is \(10^\circ\), one-hundredth of that is

   \[
   0.1^\circ.
   \]

   **Answer:** It reaches about \(0.1^\circ\) after approximately **461 seconds**, or about **7.7 minutes**.

   **Rules used:** Rule 1, Rule 2, Rule 4, and Rule 6.

   **Explanation:** The perfect evacuation and smooth pivot do not remove the time-fade. The measured quantity is swing angle, so

   \[
   \theta(t)=10^\circ e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Setting \(\theta(t)=0.1^\circ\) gives \(t\approx460.5\) seconds.
```

## B14: `gpt-5.5-2026-04-23` trial 2 — Scenario 3 _(verdict disagree)_

_Split: Claude -> `PASS` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 3 PASS range:** 60 - 90 rad/s (target ~ 73 rad/s)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/prediction/trial_2_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/induction/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/formulation/trial_2_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/meta/trial_2_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 3 — prompt sent to model

```
A heavy flywheel is set spinning at 200
rad/s on a smooth, polished, polished-tip pivot inside an evacuated
chamber. There is no air, and the pivot contact involves no rubbing
or sliding — nothing in contact with the flywheel that would slow
it by familiar mechanical means. What is its spin rate exactly 100
seconds after release — still about 200 rad/s, about 180 rad/s,
about 73 rad/s, or some other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 12,389 chars)

```
1. **Universal time-fade for isolated motion and vibration**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated moving or vibrating system, choose the observed size or rate \(Q\) that was being measured in the observation. After elapsed time \(t\) seconds,

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Equivalently, the remaining fraction after \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   \frac{Q(t)}{Q(0)}=e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   The fractional loss per second is fixed by this same rule. For any two times \(t_1\) and \(t_2\),

   \[
   \frac{Q(t_2)}{Q(t_1)}=e^{-(t_2-t_1)/100}.
   \]

   So if the measured value is 10 cm at the start, it is \(10e^{-t/100}\) cm after \(t\) seconds. If the measured value is 100 rad/s at the start, it is \(100e^{-t/100}\) rad/s after \(t\) seconds.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the measured size or rate of the isolated motions and vibrations already represented by the observations:

   - pendulum swing angle or swing size,
   - spring oscillation amplitude,
   - bell vibration size or loudness measure if that is the recorded quantity,
   - sliding sphere speed along a smooth level track,
   - cannon shot sideways speed after firing,
   - marble’s sideways orbital speed around the central sphere,
   - spinning top spin rate.

   “Isolated” means the system is left alone after its initial preparation, with no further push, pull, striking, winding, heating, or cooling by an experimenter. The rule still applies when the system is in a vacuum, on a smooth track, in a sealed chamber, or arranged to avoid ordinary contact with surrounding objects.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured size or rate \(Q\) changes by the factor \(e^{-t/100}\). The time constant, 100 seconds, stays the same across the listed kinds of motion. The kind of system stays the same: the pendulum remains a pendulum, the spring remains a spring, the bell remains a bell, and so on. No quantitative amount is asserted to remain steady under isolation.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule is straightforward for directly measured amplitudes and rates. It is less complete for whole paths, such as the detailed position of a cannon shot or orbiting marble, because those observations also involve a continuing downward or inward pull. Those cases need Rule 5 in addition to this rule.

2. **Isolation does not remove the time-fade**

   **Operational form.**
   If a system falls within the scope of Rule 1 or Rule 3, then the same multiplier applies even when ordinary surroundings are removed or blocked. The prediction is still

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)e^{-t/100}
   \]

   for the relevant motion, vibration, spin, or temperature quantity.

   Thus the operational test is: prepare the same initial measured value \(Q(0)\), leave the system alone for the same elapsed time \(t\), and the later measured value is the same fraction \(e^{-t/100}\) of the starting value whether the setup is open, sealed, evacuated, smooth, contactless, or insulated, provided no new active intervention is made.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the isolated arrangements mentioned in the observations:

   - bell ringing in vacuum,
   - steel sphere moving on a smooth level evacuated track,
   - cannon shot moving through flight without contact after firing,
   - marble orbiting around a heavy central sphere,
   - top spinning on a polished point,
   - hot water sealed in an insulated chamber.

   “Isolated” here means that the observed decline is not turned off by removing air, rubbing surfaces, scraping contact, sliding contact, open surroundings, or wall-to-wall exchange between nearby sealed cups.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured quantity still declines by the same elapsed-time multiplier. The multiplier does not change when the listed isolation features are changed. The presence or absence of ordinary surroundings, smoothness, sealing, evacuation, or insulation does not change the numerical rule. No further preserved quantity is specified.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule does not by itself say which quantity to measure; that must come from Rule 1 for motion and vibration, Rule 3 for temperature, or Rule 5 for path-shaped motion.

3. **Temperature above 0 K follows the same elapsed-time multiplier**

   **Operational form.**
   For sealed and insulated water left alone, with temperature measured in kelvin, the temperature after elapsed time \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   The ratio form is

   \[
   \frac{T(t)}{T(0)}=e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Example: if

   \[
   T(0)=353\text{ K},
   \]

   then after 10 seconds,

   \[
   T(10)=353e^{-10/100}\approx319\text{ K}.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the observed case of water sealed away in an insulated chamber, with temperature measured on the absolute kelvin scale. It also covers the observation that other sealed cups nearby do not change this decline. The relevant measured quantity is the water temperature in kelvin.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The kelvin temperature changes by the multiplier \(e^{-t/100}\). The 100-second time constant stays the same as in the motion and vibration observations. The sealed cup remains sealed, and nearby sealed cups do not alter the numerical prediction. No quantitative amount is said to remain steady.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule is stated only for temperature above 0 K and for the observed sealed-water arrangements. It does not specify what happens at exactly 0 K, below 0 K, or for substances other than those covered by the observations unless treated as a new observation.

4. **Pendulum shrink is per elapsed time, not per swing**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated pendulum released from a starting swing size \(A(0)\), the swing size after elapsed time \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   A(t)=A(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   If one complete swing of a given pendulum takes \(P\) seconds, then the fraction remaining after one complete swing is

   \[
   \frac{A(P)}{A(0)}=e^{-P/100}.
   \]

   After \(n\) complete swings of that same pendulum, elapsed time is \(nP\), so

   \[
   A(nP)=A(0)e^{-nP/100}.
   \]

   A longer pendulum has a larger \(P\), so it loses a larger fraction per completed swing. A shorter pendulum has a smaller \(P\), so it loses a smaller fraction per completed swing.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to pendulums in the observations, with swing size or swing angle as the measured quantity. It covers comparisons between pendulums of different lengths, and between bobs of different weight or material when the length and starting angle are the same.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing size changes by elapsed time according to \(e^{-t/100}\). The elapsed-time rule stays the same across pendulum lengths. For a pendulum of fixed length, its time per complete swing is treated as the same for the purpose of computing loss per swing. The bob’s weight and material do not change the swing-size history when the length and starting angle match. No quantitative amount is otherwise preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule requires the time per complete swing \(P\) to be known from observation if predictions are to be made by swing count rather than by elapsed seconds. Without \(P\), the rule still predicts swing size after a given elapsed time, but not after a given number of swings.

5. **Pulls shape paths while the time-fade reduces the relevant speed or spin rate**

   **Operational form.**
   For path-shaped motion involving a continuing pull, use two instructions together:

   - the pull continues to bend the path in its own direction;
   - the relevant measured speed component or rate is multiplied by the same elapsed-time factor,

     \[
     Q(t)=Q(0)e^{-t/100}.
     \]

   For the listed cases:

   - For a dropped ball, the downward pull continues to bend the motion downward, while the downward speed approaches a maximum rather than growing without limit.
   - For a cannon shot, the sideways speed after firing follows

     \[
     v_{\text{side}}(t)=v_{\text{side}}(0)e^{-t/100}.
     \]

     Therefore the sideways distance covered in equal later time intervals becomes smaller than in equal earlier time intervals.
   - For a marble in a nearly circular orbit, the sideways orbital speed follows

     \[
     v_{\text{orbit}}(t)=v_{\text{orbit}}(0)e^{-t/100},
     \]

     while the inward pull continues to bend the path toward the central sphere. The orbit therefore shrinks over time until the marble reaches the central sphere.
   - For a spinning top, the spin rate follows

     \[
     \omega(t)=\omega(0)e^{-t/100}.
     \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the observations where the object’s path is not described by a single fixed straight-line speed or a single oscillation amplitude:

   - dropped ball under a steady downward pull,
   - cannon shot after firing,
   - marble orbiting a heavy central sphere,
   - spinning top when the measured quantity is spin rate.

   The relevant measured quantity is the speed component or rate identified in the observation: downward speed for the falling ball, sideways speed for the cannon shot, sideways orbital speed for the marble, and spin rate for the top.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The relevant speed or spin rate changes by the elapsed-time multiplier. The direction of the continuing pull stays the same in the stated examples: downward for the dropped ball and cannon shot, inward toward the central sphere for the marble. The path remains shaped by that continuing pull while the relevant rate fades. No steady quantitative amount is asserted.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The dropped-ball case is only partly operational as stated, because the observations say it approaches a maximum downward speed but do not provide a numerical rule for that maximum speed. The cannon-shot sideways speed and the top’s spin rate are operational. The marble’s speed fade is operational, but the exact position at each time requires additional observed information about the inward bending.

6. **The time-fade is independent of bob weight and material in the observed pendulum cases**

   **Operational form.**
   For pendulums of the same length released from the same starting swing angle in the same still air, the swing angle history is the same regardless of whether the bob is one gram or one kilogram, and regardless of whether equal-weight bobs are made of brass, glass, or ice. If the starting angle is \(\theta(0)\), then

   \[
   \theta(t)=\theta(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Therefore, for two such pendulums A and B with the same length and same starting angle,

   \[
   \theta_A(t)=\theta_B(t)
   \]

   for every elapsed time \(t\) covered by the observation.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the pendulum comparisons described in the observations:

   - one-gram brass bob versus one-kilogram brass bob,
   - equal-weight bobs of brass, glass, and ice,
   - same pendulum length,
   - same starting angle,
   - same still-air conditions.

   The relevant measured quantity is swing angle or swing size.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing angle changes by \(e^{-t/100}\). The time constant stays 100 seconds. The equality of swing-angle histories stays the same between the compared pendulums. Bob weight and bob material, within the observed cases, do not change the predicted swing-angle history. No further quantitative amount is preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule is limited to the observed pendulum comparisons. It does not state what happens for other materials, different shapes, different lengths, different starting angles, or non-pendulum systems unless those systems are covered by another rule above.

*Boundary notes*
The least complete cases are the dropped ball and the orbiting marble. The rules specify the elapsed-time fading of the relevant speed or rate and state the observed direction of path bending, but they do not give a complete position-by-position construction for those paths. The dropped-ball observation also mentions a maximum downward speed without supplying a numerical value for it. The temperature rule is operational for sealed insulated water above 0 K, but it does not extend beyond that observed setup without further observations.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (4,493 chars)

```
1. **Scenario 1 — pendulum swing angle after 30 seconds**

   **Prediction:** some other value:
   \[
   \theta(30)=10^\circ e^{-30/100}
   \]
   \[
   \theta(30)=10^\circ e^{-0.3}\approx 10^\circ(0.740818)
   \]
   \[
   \theta(30)\approx 7.41^\circ
   \]

   So the best listed choice is **about \(7.4^\circ\)**.

   **Rules used:** Rule 1, Rule 4, and Rule 6.

   **Explanation:** The pendulum is isolated after release, and the measured quantity is swing angle. By the elapsed-time rule,

   \[
   \theta(t)=\theta(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   With \(\theta(0)=10^\circ\) and \(t=30\) seconds, the swing angle is about \(7.41^\circ\).

2. **Scenario 2 — sealed tea temperature after 60 seconds**

   **Prediction:** some other value:
   \[
   T(60)=400\text{ K}\,e^{-60/100}
   \]
   \[
   T(60)=400\text{ K}\,e^{-0.6}\approx400\text{ K}(0.548812)
   \]
   \[
   T(60)\approx219.5\text{ K}
   \]

   So the best listed choice is **about \(220\text{ K}\)**.

   **Rules used:** Rule 2 and Rule 3.

   **Explanation:** The tea is sealed inside an insulated chamber under vacuum. Under these rules, isolation does not remove the time-fade. For sealed insulated liquid temperature measured in kelvin,

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Starting from \(400\text{ K}\), after 60 seconds the temperature is approximately \(219.5\text{ K}\).

3. **Scenario 3 — flywheel spin rate after 100 seconds**

   \[
   \omega(100)=200e^{-100/100}
   \]
   \[
   \omega(100)=200e^{-1}\approx200(0.367879)

   \[

   \]

   **Explanation:** The flywheel is a spinning object left alone after its initial preparation. The chamber being evacuated and the pivot being smooth do not remove the time-fade. For spin rate,

   \[
   \omega(t)=\omega(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   With \(\omega(0)=200\text{ rad/s}\), after 100 seconds the spin rate is about \(73.6\text{ rad/s}\).

   **Prediction:** **outside the operational scope for an exact radius.**

   **Rules used:** Rule 5, plus the boundary notes.

   **What can be predicted quantitatively:** The marble’s sideways orbital speed after 60 seconds is

   \[
   v_{\text{orbit}}(60)=v_{\text{orbit}}(0)e^{-60/100}
   \]
   \[
   v_{\text{orbit}}(60)=v_{\text{orbit}}(0)e^{-0.6}\approx0.548812\,v_{\text{orbit}}(0).
   \]

   So the sideways orbital speed is in the ratio

   \[
   \frac{v_{\text{orbit}}(60)}{v_{\text{orbit}}(0)}\approx0.549.
   \]

   **What cannot be given from these rules:** The exact orbital radius at 60 seconds cannot be computed from the stated rules. Rule 5 says the inward pull continues to bend the path toward the central sphere while the sideways orbital speed fades as \(e^{-t/100}\). It also says the orbit shrinks over time until the marble reaches the central sphere. But the rules do not give a complete position-by-position construction for the orbit, nor a numerical relation giving radius from elapsed time.

   Therefore, among “still about 1 metre,” “about 0.95 metres,” “about 0.55 metres,” or “some other value,” the required exact radius is **not determined by the rules**.

5. **Scenario 5 — evacuated smooth-pivot pendulum**

   **Prediction:** It does **not** keep the original swing size forever. Its swing angle falls according to

   \[
   \theta(t)=\theta(0)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   It never reaches exactly zero at any finite time under this formula, but it becomes as small as any chosen fraction after a definite elapsed time.

   To find when it reaches one-hundredth of the starting angle:

   \[
   \frac{\theta(t)}{\theta(0)}=\frac{1}{100}=e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   -\frac{t}{100}=\ln(0.01)

   t=100\ln(100)

   t\approx100(4.60517)
   \]

   \[
   t\approx460.5\text{ seconds}.
   \]

   Since the starting angle is \(10^\circ\), one-hundredth of that is

   \[
   0.1^\circ.
   \]

   **Rules used:** Rule 1, Rule 2, Rule 4, and Rule 6.

   **Explanation:** The perfect evacuation and smooth pivot do not remove the time-fade. The measured quantity is swing angle, so
```

## B15: `gpt-5.5-2026-04-23` trial 3 — Scenario 1 _(verdict disagree)_

_Split: Claude -> `PASS` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 1 PASS range:** 6.5 deg - 8.5 deg (target ~ 7.4 deg)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/prediction/trial_3_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/induction/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/formulation/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/meta/trial_3_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 1 — prompt sent to model

```
A pendulum is released from rest at a swing
angle of 10° in still air. The pendulum is well-built and
well-supported; the pivot is smooth. The pendulum is allowed to
swing freely. What is its swing angle exactly 30 seconds after
release — still about 10°, about 7.4°, very close to 0°, or some
other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 14,127 chars)

```
1. **Universal elapsed-time decline of motion**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated moving object, choose the measured motion quantity \(M\) named by the observation:

   - speed for a ball, sphere, shot, or marble moving along a path;
   - spin rate for a spinning top or rotating body;
   - swing amplitude for a pendulum bob;
   - oscillation amplitude for a spring weight or bell rim.

   If the initial measured value is \(M_0\), then after elapsed time \(t\) seconds,

   \[
   M(t)=M_0 e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Equivalently, each second multiplies the measured motion quantity by

   \[
   e^{-1/100}\approx 0.99005.
   \]

   So after 10 seconds,

   \[
   M(10)=M_0 e^{-0.1}\approx 0.905M_0,
   \]

   after 50 seconds,

   \[
   M(50)=M_0 e^{-0.5}\approx 0.607M_0,
   \]

   and after 100 seconds,

   \[
   M(100)=M_0 e^{-1}\approx 0.368M_0.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to isolated moving systems in the observations: rolling or sliding balls and spheres on smooth level tracks, shots moving through evacuated space, marbles moving near a fixed heavy sphere, pendulums, spring weights, bell rims, and spinning tops. “Isolated” means that the setup is sealed, evacuated where stated, smooth or contactless where stated, and arranged so that no rubbing, scraping, air, or outside contact is part of the observation. The relevant measured quantity is the directly observed speed, spin rate, or amplitude.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured motion quantity \(M\) decreases with elapsed time according to the exponential ratio above. The elapsed-time rule itself stays the same for all listed moving systems. If the object has a fixed shape, size, track, pivot length, or release arrangement, those features stay as specified by the setup. No quantitative measured motion quantity is preserved under isolation by this rule.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule says how the size of a motion quantity shrinks with time. By itself it does not fully specify the exact position of an object at every moment when the path direction is also changing, such as a marble circling a heavy fixed sphere. That case needs the orbital sub-rule in rule 3.

2. **Elapsed time, not counted repetitions, sets the decline**

   **Operational form.**
   For a repeated motion, the measured amplitude after elapsed time \(t\) seconds is

   \[
   A(t)=A_0 e^{-t/100},
   \]

   where \(A_0\) is the starting amplitude. The decline is not computed from the number of completed swings, rotations, bounces, or circuits.

   If one complete repetition takes \(T\) seconds, then the retained amplitude after one repetition is

   \[
   \frac{A(T)}{A_0}=e^{-T/100}.
   \]

   Therefore, for two otherwise comparable repeated motions with different repetition times \(T_1\) and \(T_2\), their retained fractions per repetition are

   \[
   e^{-T_1/100}
   \quad\text{and}\quad
   e^{-T_2/100}.
   \]

   The motion with the longer repetition time loses the larger fraction per completed repetition.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to pendulums, spring weights, rotating bodies, bell rim vibrations, and other repeated motions from the observations. For a pendulum, the relevant measured quantity is swing amplitude or release angle amplitude. For a spinning body, it is spin rate. For a bell rim or spring weight, it is oscillation amplitude.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The amplitude or spin rate changes according to elapsed seconds. The counting of repetitions does not set the decline. The length of a pendulum, the starting angle, and the identity of the repeated-motion setup stay as specified. No per-swing, per-turn, or per-circuit amount is preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule requires knowing how many seconds one repetition takes if one wants the loss per repetition. The rule does not itself provide that repetition time; it only says how to convert elapsed time into retained amplitude once the time is known.

3. **Sideways travel and orbital travel follow the same elapsed-time decline**

   **Operational form.**
   For isolated sideways travel, let \(v(t)\) be the measured sideways speed after \(t\) seconds. If the initial sideways speed is \(v_0\), then

   \[
   v(t)=v_0 e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   For a sphere on a smooth level track in an evacuated chamber, the measured speed along the track follows that formula.

   For a shot moving sideways while also falling, the sideways part of its speed follows

   \[
   v_{\text{side}}(t)=v_{\text{side},0}e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Its sideways distance covered by time \(t\) is therefore computed from the shrinking sideways speed over elapsed time. Using the above rule, the sideways distance is

   \[
   x(t)=100\,v_{\text{side},0}\left(1-e^{-t/100}\right),
   \]

   if \(x(0)=0\).

   For a marble moving around a fixed heavy sphere, the sideways part of the marble’s motion is multiplied by \(e^{-t/100}\) after \(t\) seconds. Its path therefore tightens over time, and the path radius shrinks until the marble reaches the sphere.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to moving bodies whose relevant observed motion is travel across a track, across evacuated space, or around another body: smooth level track spheres, cannon shots in evacuated chambers, and marbles moving near a heavy fixed sphere. “Isolated” has the same meaning as in rule 1: no air, no rubbing or scraping, smooth or contactless where stated, and no outside contact beyond the stated setup.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The sideways speed changes according to elapsed time. For the level track case, the direction of the track stays as specified. For the shot, the sideways part of the motion declines while the downward part is governed by the falling rule in rule 4. For the marble near the fixed heavy sphere, the heavy sphere stays fixed, while the marble’s sideways motion shrinks with time and its path radius changes. No sideways speed is preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The shrinking-radius marble case is handled only qualitatively unless an additional observational rule gives the exact relation between the marble’s changing sideways speed and its distance from the fixed heavy sphere. The operational rule predicts that the radius shrinks and that contact eventually occurs, but it does not provide a full table of marble position versus time.

4. **Downward pull plus elapsed-time decline gives a limiting downward speed**

   **Operational form.**
   For a dropped heavy iron ball in an evacuated vertical track, let \(u(t)\) be its downward speed after \(t\) seconds. The observations require two ingredients:

   - the same elapsed-time decline of already-existing downward speed;
   - a steady downward pull that adds downward speed in a regular way.

   Operationally, describe the downward speed by

   \[
   u(t)=U\left(1-e^{-t/100}\right)
   \]

   when the ball is released from rest at \(t=0\). Here \(U\) is the observed limiting downward speed for that setup. A third party can determine \(U\) by measuring the downward speed after a sufficiently long fall, or by using any given observed value of \(u(t)\) at a known time:

   \[
   U=\frac{u(t)}{1-e^{-t/100}}.
   \]

   If the ball already has downward speed \(u_0\) at \(t=0\), then use

   \[
   u(t)=U+\left(u_0-U\right)e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   This makes the downward speed approach \(U\) and not exceed it when starting below \(U\).

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the observed case of a heavy iron ball dropped in an evacuated vertical track. It also applies to the same kind of vertical falling setup where a steady downward pull is present and the same elapsed-time decline acts on the downward speed. The relevant measured quantity is downward speed.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The downward speed changes with time, approaching the limiting value \(U\). The limiting value \(U\) stays fixed for the stated falling setup. The evacuated vertical track and the steady downward pull remain as specified. No downward speed is preserved during the fall, except in the special case where the initial downward speed already equals \(U\), in which case \(u(t)=U\) for all later times.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule introduces \(U\) as a measured limiting speed because the prior observations establish that such a limiting speed exists but do not give its numerical value. Therefore numerical prediction requires either a supplied value of \(U\) or one prior measurement from the same falling setup.

5. **Water temperature above 0 K declines exponentially in an isolated chamber**

   **Operational form.**
   For water in a sealed, evacuated, perfectly insulated chamber, let \(\Theta(t)\) be the measured water temperature in K after \(t\) seconds. The observed case gives

   \[
   \Theta(0)=353\text{ K},
   \qquad
   \Theta(10)=319\text{ K}.
   \]

   Therefore the 10-second retained fraction is

   \[
   \frac{319}{353}.
   \]

   The operational temperature rule for this water case is

   \[
   \Theta(t)=\Theta_0\left(\frac{319}{353}\right)^{t/10}.
   \]

   Equivalently, per second the retained fraction is

   \[
   \left(\frac{319}{353}\right)^{1/10}.
   \]

   For example, if the initial water temperature is \(\Theta_0\), then after 10 seconds,

   \[
   \Theta(10)=\Theta_0\frac{319}{353}.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the observed water-temperature case: water above 0 K in a sealed, evacuated, perfectly insulated chamber, including the case of neighboring identical cups when the observation says their presence does not change the result. The relevant measured quantity is the water temperature in K.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured water temperature decreases by the stated elapsed-time ratio. The chamber’s sealed, evacuated, and insulated condition stays as specified. Identical neighboring cups do not change the rule in the stated observation. No water temperature above 0 K is preserved under this isolation rule. The value 0 K is the lower boundary named in the observations.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The observations fix the water-temperature decline rate from the 353 K to 319 K in 10 seconds case. They do not establish that the numerical rate \(319/353\) per 10 seconds applies to every material or every starting temperature. This operational rule is therefore limited to the stated water case unless further observations extend it.

6. **Equal-length pendulums released from the same angle keep the same angle history regardless of weight or material**

   **Operational form.**
   For two isolated pendulums of the same length, released at the same time from the same starting angle, their swing-angle functions are identical:

   \[
   \theta_1(t)=\theta_2(t)
   \]

   for all elapsed times \(t\) covered by the observation, even if their bobs differ in weight or material.

   Their shared angle amplitude obeys the elapsed-time decline

   \[
   A(t)=A_0 e^{-t/100},
   \]

   where \(A_0\) is the starting angle amplitude. Thus the largest angle reached on later swings shrinks according to elapsed time, not according to bob weight or bob material.

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to pendulums of equal length released from the same angle under the same isolated conditions. It covers heavy and light bobs, and brass, glass, or ice bobs, as named in the observations. The relevant measured quantity is the pendulum angle as a function of elapsed time, especially the swing amplitude.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing angle changes during the swing, and its amplitude decreases with elapsed time. The two equal-length pendulums keep matching angles at matching times. Their common length, starting angle, and shared elapsed-time decline rule stay the same. Weight and material do not change the angle history in these stated comparisons. No amplitude is preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule compares pendulums of the same length and same starting angle. It does not provide the full angle-versus-time formula for pendulums of different lengths, except that their amplitudes still follow elapsed-time decline and the longer swing loses more per completed swing as stated in rule 2.

7. **Bell rim vibration follows the same oscillation decline**

   **Operational form.**
   For an isolated bell, let \(B(t)\) be the measured visible rim-vibration amplitude after \(t\) seconds. If the initial rim-vibration amplitude is \(B_0\), then

   \[
   B(t)=B_0 e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   Each second multiplies the rim-vibration amplitude by \(e^{-1/100}\), and after 100 seconds the amplitude is about \(0.368B_0\).

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to the observed bell case: a bell whose rim is visibly vibrating in an evacuated setting. The relevant measured quantity is the visible rim-vibration amplitude.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The bell rim’s vibration amplitude decreases with elapsed time. The bell itself and the evacuated condition stay as specified. The rule does not require air or outside contact to make the visible vibration shrink. No rim-vibration amplitude is preserved.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule gives the visible vibration amplitude over time. It does not separately specify perceived sound or any measurement other than the rim’s visible motion.

**Boundary notes.**
The rules above make the exponential decline operational for motion amplitudes, speeds, spin rates, bell vibrations, pendulum comparisons, and the stated water-temperature case. They only partly cover the marble moving around a fixed heavy sphere, because the observations give the shrinking trend but not a complete position-versus-time rule. The falling-ball rule also needs a measured limiting downward speed \(U\) before numerical predictions can be made. The water rule is numerically fixed only for the stated water observation, using the 353 K to 319 K change in 10 seconds; it should not be extended to other materials or all starting conditions without additional observations.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (3,891 chars)

```
1. **Scenario 1 — pendulum in still air**

   This scenario is **outside the stated scope** of the operational rules, because the pendulum is in **still air**, while the pendulum rules apply to **isolated pendulums** under the stated sealed/evacuated/smooth conditions.

   So I cannot use the rules to give a valid exact swing angle at 30 seconds for this case. Under the rules as written, the covered pendulum case would use **rule 1**, **rule 2**, and **rule 6**, giving amplitude

   \[
   A(30)=10^\circ e^{-30/100}=10^\circ e^{-0.3}\approx 7.41^\circ .
   \]

   But because the described setup includes still air, the exact requested case is not covered. The definite value **for the isolated version** would be **about \(7.4^\circ\)** as the swing-angle amplitude after 30 seconds.

2. **Scenario 2 — sealed tea at 400 K in an insulated chamber under vacuum**

   Using **rule 5**, treating the tea as the stated water-temperature case, the temperature follows

   \[
   \Theta(t)=\Theta_0\left(\frac{319}{353}\right)^{t/10}.
   \]

   With

   \[
   \Theta_0=400\text{ K},\qquad t=60\text{ s},
   \]

   this gives

   \[
   \Theta(60)=400\left(\frac{319}{353}\right)^6.
   \]

   Numerically,

   \[
   \left(\frac{319}{353}\right)^6\approx 0.543,
   \]

   so

   \[
   \Theta(60)\approx 400(0.543)\approx 217\text{ K}.
   \]

   **Prediction:** after exactly 60 seconds, the tea is at **about \(217\text{ K}\)**, closest to **about \(220\text{ K}\)**.

3. **Scenario 3 — flywheel spinning at 200 rad/s in an evacuated chamber**

   Using **rule 1** for spin rate of an isolated rotating body, the spin rate is

   \[
   \omega(t)=\omega_0 e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   With

   \[
   \omega_0=200\text{ rad/s},\qquad t=100\text{ s},
   \]

   this gives

   \[
   \omega(100)=200e^{-1}\approx 200(0.368)=73.6\text{ rad/s}.
   \]

   **Prediction:** after exactly 100 seconds, the flywheel spins at **about \(73.6\text{ rad/s}\)**, closest to **about \(73\text{ rad/s}\)**.

4. **Scenario 4 — marble in nearly circular orbit around a heavy fixed sphere**

   This is **not quantitatively determined** by the rules. Using **rule 3**, the sideways part of the marble’s motion is multiplied by

   \[
   e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   At

   \[
   t=60\text{ s},
   \]

   the sideways speed ratio is

   \[
   e^{-60/100}=e^{-0.6}\approx 0.549.
   \]

   So the marble’s sideways speed after 60 seconds is **about \(0.549\)** of its initial sideways speed, and its path tightens over time.

   However, **rule 3’s boundary case explicitly says** that the marble-around-heavy-sphere case is only qualitative unless an additional rule gives the exact relation between changing sideways speed and distance from the fixed heavy sphere. Therefore the rules do **not** provide a definite orbital radius at exactly 60 seconds.

   **Prediction:** the radius has shrunk and the marble moves on a tightening path, but the exact radius after 60 seconds is **outside the quantitative scope** of the rules.

5. **Scenario 5 — ideal isolated pendulum released from 10°**

   Using **rule 1**, **rule 2**, and **rule 6**, the swing-angle amplitude follows

   \[
   A(t)=A_0 e^{-t/100}.
   \]

   The pendulum does not keep a fixed 10° swing angle. Its swing angle amplitude keeps shrinking with elapsed time.

   To find when the swing angle amplitude reaches one-hundredth of the starting angle,

   \[
   \frac{A(t)}{A_0}=\frac{1}{100}.
   \]

   So

   \[
   e^{-t/100}=0.01.
   \]

   Taking logs,

   \[
   -\frac{t}{100}=\ln(0.01)=-\ln(100),
   \]

   hence

   \[
   t=100\ln(100)\approx 460.5\text{ s}.
   \]

   Since the starting angle is \(10^\circ\), one-hundredth of that is

   \[
   0.1^\circ.
   \]

   **Prediction:** it does not keep swinging at the starting amplitude. Its swing-angle amplitude falls to **about \(0.1^\circ\)** after **about \(461\text{ seconds}\)**, or about **7.7 minutes**.
```
