## B16: `gpt-5.5-2026-04-23` trial 4 — Scenario 1 _(fab(claude=True,openai=False))_

_Split: Claude -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 1 PASS range:** 6.5 deg - 8.5 deg (target ~ 7.4 deg)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/prediction/trial_4_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/induction/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/formulation/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gpt-5.5-2026-04-23/03_decay/meta/trial_4_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 1 — prompt sent to model

```
A pendulum is released from rest at a swing
angle of 10° in still air. The pendulum is well-built and
well-supported; the pivot is smooth. The pendulum is allowed to
swing freely. What is its swing angle exactly 30 seconds after
release — still about 10°, about 7.4°, very close to 0°, or some
other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 16,095 chars)

```
1. **Built-in multiplicative fading with elapsed time**

   **Operational form.**
   For any isolated measured motion or vibration covered by the observations, choose the relevant measured quantity \(Q\). If its value at the starting time is \(Q(0)\), then after elapsed time \(t\),

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)\,r^t
   \]

   where \(r\) is the fixed per-second remaining fraction for that particular arrangement, with \(0<r<1\).

   Equivalently, if a measured quantity is observed to have ratio

   \[
   \frac{Q(t_1)}{Q(0)}
   \]

   after \(t_1\) seconds, then the per-second remaining fraction is

   \[
   r=\left(\frac{Q(t_1)}{Q(0)}\right)^{1/t_1}.
   \]

   Once \(r\) is known, the later value after any elapsed time \(t\) is computed by multiplying the starting value by \(r^t\).

   **Scope.**
   This rule applies to isolated cases in the observations where a single measured size or rate declines with time: spring amplitude, pendulum swing amplitude, visible bell-vibration amplitude, straight-line speed, top spin rate, and similar directly measured motion or vibration quantities. “Isolated” means smooth, polished, evacuated, sealed, insulated, contactless, or otherwise arranged so that ordinary contact with surroundings is not responsible for the change.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured quantity \(Q\) decreases with elapsed time by the same fractional rule for that arrangement. The per-second remaining fraction \(r\) stays fixed for that arrangement. No quantitative preserved amount is asserted for the isolated system.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule gives the shrinking envelope or measured size/rate. It does not by itself describe the detailed back-and-forth position of a spring or pendulum during each part of a swing; it only gives how the measured amplitude changes with time.

2. **Different arrangements have different per-time fading constants**

   **Operational form.**
   Each arrangement has its own fixed per-second remaining fraction. For arrangement \(i\),

   \[
   Q_i(t)=Q_i(0)\,r_i^t,
   \]

   with \(0<r_i<1\).

   The relevant \(Q_i\) depends on the arrangement:

   - spring: oscillation amplitude,
   - straight evacuated track: straight-line speed,
   - bell: visible vibration amplitude,
   - orbiting marble: orbit size, stated as radius when the path is nearly circular,
   - spinning top: spin rate,
   - pendulum: swing amplitude.

   Example: if a spring amplitude changes from 10 cm to 3.7 cm in 100 s, then

   \[
   r_{\text{spring}}=\left(\frac{3.7}{10}\right)^{1/100}.
   \]

   So after \(t\) seconds,

   \[
   A_{\text{spring}}(t)=10\,r_{\text{spring}}^t.
   \]

   If a top spin rate changes from 100 rad/s to 60.5 rad/s in 50 s, then

   \[
   r_{\text{top}}=\left(\frac{60.5}{100}\right)^{1/50},
   \]

   so

   \[
   \omega_{\text{top}}(t)=100\,r_{\text{top}}^t.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This applies when the observations provide, or allow measurement of, an initial value and a later value for the same kind of quantity in the same arrangement. The rule does not require the same \(r\) for different arrangements.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The measured quantity changes by multiplication with \(r_i^t\). The value of \(r_i\) stays fixed for that arrangement. The identity of the measured quantity also stays fixed: spring amplitude remains the spring quantity, top spin rate remains the top quantity, and so on.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule does not predict the numerical value of \(r_i\) before it has been observed or otherwise specified for that arrangement. It only says how to use \(r_i\) once known.

3. **Pendulum fading is tied to elapsed time, not swing count**

   **Operational form.**
   For a pendulum with initial swing amplitude \(\Theta(0)\), the amplitude after elapsed time \(t\) is

   \[
   \Theta(t)=\Theta(0)\,r_{\text{pendulum}}^t,
   \]

   where \(r_{\text{pendulum}}\) is the per-second remaining fraction for that pendulum arrangement.

   If one full swing takes \(T\) seconds, then the amplitude after one full swing is

   \[
   \Theta(T)=\Theta(0)\,r_{\text{pendulum}}^T.
   \]

   After \(N\) full swings,

   \[
   \Theta(NT)=\Theta(0)\,r_{\text{pendulum}}^{NT}.
   \]

   Thus two pendulums with different swing times lose different fractions per completed swing even if they share the same per-second rule.

   **Scope.**
   This applies to pendulums released from a stated starting angle and then left isolated. The relevant measured quantity is swing amplitude, expressed as angle. The rule applies to comparisons between short and long pendulums because they have different elapsed times per completed swing.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing amplitude changes with elapsed time. The per-second remaining fraction for the pendulum arrangement stays fixed. The elapsed time per swing for a given pendulum length stays the timing reference for converting from swing count to seconds. No fixed amount per swing is asserted.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule handles the observed difference between long and short pendulums per completed swing. It does not provide a separate formula for the detailed angle at every instant inside a swing, except through the shrinking amplitude envelope.

4. **Steady downward pulling with built-in fading of speed gives a limiting speed**

   **Operational form.**
   In the vertical evacuated track case, two simultaneous rules act on downward speed:

   - the downward pull tends to increase the downward speed with elapsed time;
   - the built-in time-fading multiplies the current speed by a shrinking fraction as time passes.

   Operationally, the observations require that the downward speed \(v(t)\) approach a fixed limiting value \(v_{\max}\) for that arrangement:

   \[
   v(t)\to v_{\max}
   \quad\text{as elapsed time becomes large.}
   \]

   A usable prediction rule is therefore:

   - for a sufficiently tall track, the final measured downward speed is close to \(v_{\max}\);
   - making the track taller does not raise the final speed beyond \(v_{\max}\);
   - \(v_{\max}\) must be measured or specified for that arrangement.

   **Scope.**
   This applies to a heavy iron ball dropped in a contactless evacuated vertical track under steady downward pulling. The relevant measured quantity is downward speed.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The downward speed changes at first, then approaches a fixed maximum value for the arrangement. The limiting value \(v_{\max}\) stays the same when the track is made taller, provided the arrangement is otherwise the same. No preserved quantity is asserted.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule is less complete than the pure exponential fading rules because the observations state the existence of a maximum downward speed but do not provide a full time-by-time formula for \(v(t)\) during the approach. A separate measured approach curve, or an additional sub-rule for the steady downward pull, would be needed to compute \(v(t)\) at every intermediate time.

5. **Horizontal motion fades while downward pulling continues**

   **Operational form.**
   For a fired shot in vacuum, separate the motion into sideways and downward measured speeds. The sideways speed obeys the built-in time-fading rule:

   \[
   v_{\text{side}}(t)=v_{\text{side}}(0)\,r_{\text{shot,side}}^t,
   \]

   where \(r_{\text{shot,side}}\) is the per-second remaining fraction for the sideways speed of that arrangement.

   The downward part follows the downward-pulling rule from Rule 4.

   Therefore, during the time of flight, the sideways speed becomes smaller. The landing distance from the cannon is less than it would be if the sideways speed had remained equal to \(v_{\text{side}}(0)\) for the whole flight.

   **Scope.**
   This applies to cannon-shot motion in vacuum where the shot has an initial sideways speed and is also subject to downward pulling. The relevant measured quantities are sideways speed, downward speed, flight time, and horizontal landing distance.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   Sideways speed decreases by the exponential elapsed-time rule. Downward speed changes according to the vertical rule. The per-second remaining fraction for sideways speed stays fixed for the arrangement. No unchanged sideways speed is asserted.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule predicts the direction of the difference in landing distance and gives the sideways speed at any elapsed time. However, without an operational formula for the downward part and for total flight time, it does not by itself compute the exact landing distance.

6. **Sideways orbital motion fades, so an orbit shrinks**

   **Operational form.**
   For a marble moving around a heavy fixed sphere, the sideways motion has a per-second remaining fraction:

   \[
   v_{\text{side,orbit}}(t)=v_{\text{side,orbit}}(0)\,r_{\text{orbit,side}}^t.
   \]

   The central pulling of the fixed sphere continues while this sideways fading occurs. For a path that begins almost circular, the operational consequence is that the orbit size decreases over many circuits.

   If the orbit size is measured by radius \(R(t)\), then the observations require

   \[
   R(t) \text{ decreases over time}
   \]

   until the marble reaches the central sphere.

   If a directly measured radius-fading fraction is supplied for the arrangement, then use

   \[
   R(t)=R(0)\,r_{\text{orbit radius}}^t.
   \]

   **Scope.**
   This applies to a marble moving around a heavy fixed sphere, especially when the path starts nearly circular. The relevant measured quantities are sideways speed and orbit size, with orbit size measured as radius for a nearly circular path.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The sideways speed decreases by elapsed-time fading. The orbit size decreases over many circuits. The per-second fading fraction for the chosen orbital measured quantity stays fixed if specified for that arrangement. The central sphere remains fixed in the stated setup. No preserved orbital quantity is asserted.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The observations support shrinking and eventual contact with the central sphere, but they do not fully specify the detailed shape of the path at every instant. A separate radius rule is needed if exact \(R(t)\) values are required. The exponential radius formula applies only when \(r_{\text{orbit radius}}\) has been specified or measured.

7. **Absolute temperature above 0 K fades exponentially with elapsed time**

   **Operational form.**
   For a sealed, insulated cup under vacuum, the absolute temperature \(T\), measured above 0 K, obeys

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)\,r_{\text{temp}}^t,
   \]

   where \(r_{\text{temp}}\) is the per-second remaining fraction for temperature in that arrangement, with \(0<r_{\text{temp}}<1\).

   If a cup starts at 353 K and is measured at 319 K after 10 seconds, then

   \[
   r_{\text{temp}}=\left(\frac{319}{353}\right)^{1/10}.
   \]

   Hence after elapsed time \(t\),

   \[
   T(t)=353\,r_{\text{temp}}^t.
   \]

   Identical nearby sealed cups do not alter this rule; each cup’s temperature is multiplied by the same kind of time-fading factor independently.

   **Scope.**
   This applies to sealed, insulated cups under vacuum and to temperature measured on the absolute scale above 0 K. The relevant measured quantity is the cup’s absolute temperature.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The absolute temperature decreases exponentially with elapsed time. The per-second temperature remaining fraction stays fixed for the arrangement. The sealing, insulation, vacuum condition, and lack of contact stay as stated. No quantitative preserved thermal amount is asserted.

   **Boundary cases.**
   The rule approaches 0 K as elapsed time grows, but never reaches it in any finite time under the formula. The observations do not state what happens at exactly 0 K, so the operational rule is only for temperatures above 0 K.

8. **Equal-length pendulums released from the same angle have the same swing history regardless of bob weight or material**

   **Operational form.**
   For pendulums of the same length released from the same starting angle, the swing amplitude at elapsed time \(t\) is the same:

   \[
   \Theta_1(t)=\Theta_2(t)
   \]

   whenever

   \[
   \Theta_1(0)=\Theta_2(0)
   \]

   and the pendulum lengths are equal.

   More specifically,

   \[
   \Theta(t)=\Theta(0)\,r_{\text{pendulum,length}}^t,
   \]

   where the relevant per-second remaining fraction is fixed by the pendulum arrangement including its length, but not by bob weight or bob material.

   Thus a 1 gram brass bob and a 1 kilogram brass bob on equal-length pendulums match. Equal-weight bobs of brass, glass, and ice also match if the length and starting angle match.

   **Scope.**
   This applies to pendulums of equal length released from the same angle in the isolated pendulum conditions of the observations. The relevant measured quantity is swing angle or swing amplitude as a function of elapsed time.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The swing amplitude changes with elapsed time according to the pendulum fading rule. The equality of the histories between equal-length, equal-starting-angle pendulums stays true despite differences in bob weight or bob material. Bob weight and bob material do not change the operational prediction.

   **Boundary cases.**
   This rule does not compare pendulums of different lengths except through Rule 3. It also does not provide the detailed within-swing angle at every instant, only the matching of histories and the fading of amplitude.

9. **Isolation conditions do not remove built-in fading**

   **Operational form.**
   For the covered systems, the same elapsed-time rule applies even when the system is smooth, polished, evacuated, sealed, insulated, contactless, or not rubbing or scraping. If the relevant measured quantity is \(Q\), then in such isolated conditions,

   \[
   Q(t)=Q(0)\,r^t
   \]

   for the appropriate arrangement-specific \(r\), unless the setup is one of the mixed cases requiring an additional pulling rule, such as vertical fall, cannon shot, or orbit.

   **Scope.**
   This applies across the observed isolated arrangements:

   - spring oscillation amplitude,
   - pendulum swing amplitude,
   - bell visible vibration amplitude,
   - straight-line speed along a smooth evacuated track,
   - top spin rate,
   - cup temperature above 0 K,
   - sideways speed of a fired shot,
   - sideways orbital motion of a marble around a fixed sphere.

   **What changes and what stays the same.**
   The relevant measured quantity decreases with elapsed time even in isolated conditions. The isolation conditions themselves do not switch off the rule. The per-second remaining fraction remains the operational constant for the arrangement. No general preserved quantity is introduced.

   **Boundary cases.**
   In mixed setups where a pulling effect is also present, the exponential fading rule alone is not enough to compute the full path or full time history. Those cases require the separate operational statements in Rules 4, 5, and 6.

**Boundary notes**
The rules above fully operationalize the directly observed exponential fading cases: spring amplitude, bell vibration amplitude, top spin rate, straight-line speed fading, pendulum amplitude, and absolute temperature above 0 K. They handle the long-versus-short pendulum comparison and the bob weight/material comparison. They only partly cover the vertical falling ball, cannon shot, and orbiting marble, because those cases combine built-in fading with an ongoing pulling effect, and the observations do not provide a complete time-by-time formula for the pulling part. For those cases, the operational rules predict limiting speed, shorter cannon range, shrinking orbit size, and eventual contact, but not every intermediate position or speed unless an additional measured sub-rule is supplied.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (5,171 chars)

```
1. **Scenario 1 — some other value: it is not computable from the given data.**

   By **Rules 1, 3, 8, and 9**, the pendulum swing amplitude fades with elapsed time:

   \[
   \Theta(t)=\Theta(0)\,r_{\text{pendulum}}^t .
   \]

   Here,

   \[
   \Theta(0)=10^\circ,\qquad t=30\text{ s}.
   \]

   So the rule gives

   \[
   \Theta(30)=10^\circ\,r_{\text{pendulum}}^{30}.
   \]

   But no numerical value of \(r_{\text{pendulum}}\) is supplied for this pendulum arrangement. Therefore the rules do **not** allow choosing exactly among \(10^\circ\), \(7.4^\circ\), near \(0^\circ\), or another numerical value.

   The definite operational prediction is:

   \[
   \boxed{\Theta(30)=10^\circ\,r_{\text{pendulum}}^{30}}
   \]

   with \(0<r_{\text{pendulum}}<1\). The swing angle is less than \(10^\circ\), but its exact number is outside what can be computed from the supplied data.

2. **Scenario 2 — some other value: it is not computable from the given data.**

   By **Rules 7 and 9**, the absolute temperature above 0 K fades exponentially even in a sealed, insulated chamber under vacuum:

   \[
   T(t)=T(0)\,r_{\text{temp}}^t .
   \]

   Here,

   \[
   T(0)=400\text{ K},\qquad t=60\text{ s}.
   \]

   Therefore

   \[
   T(60)=400\,r_{\text{temp}}^{60}\text{ K}.
   \]

   No numerical value of \(r_{\text{temp}}\) is given for this cup arrangement. So the rules do **not** determine whether the answer is 360 K, 220 K, 293 K, or another numerical value.

   The definite operational prediction is:

   \[
   \boxed{T(60)=400\,r_{\text{temp}}^{60}\text{ K}}
   \]

   with \(0<r_{\text{temp}}<1\). It is below 400 K, approaches 0 K only as elapsed time grows without bound, and does not go to room temperature by any rule stated here.

3. **Scenario 3 — some other value: it is not computable from the given data.**

   By **Rules 1, 2, and 9**, the spin rate of a top or similar spinning object fades with elapsed time even under isolated conditions:

   \[
   \omega(t)=\omega(0)\,r_{\text{spin}}^t .
   \]

   Here,

   \[
   \omega(0)=200\text{ rad/s},\qquad t=100\text{ s}.
   \]

   Hence

   \[
   \omega(100)=200\,r_{\text{spin}}^{100}\text{ rad/s}.
   \]

   No numerical value of \(r_{\text{spin}}\) is supplied for this flywheel arrangement. So the rules do **not** determine whether the answer is 180 rad/s, 73 rad/s, or another numerical value.

   The definite operational prediction is:

   \[
   \boxed{\omega(100)=200\,r_{\text{spin}}^{100}\text{ rad/s}}
   \]

   with \(0<r_{\text{spin}}<1\). The spin rate is below 200 rad/s after 100 seconds, but the exact number is not fixed by the given data.

4. **Scenario 4 — some other value: it is not computable from the given data.**

   By **Rule 6**, sideways orbital motion fades while the central pulling continues, so an initially nearly circular orbit shrinks over time. If a radius-fading fraction is supplied, the radius rule is

   \[
   R(t)=R(0)\,r_{\text{orbit radius}}^t .
   \]

   Here,

   \[
   R(0)=1\text{ m},\qquad t=60\text{ s}.
   \]

   Thus, if the radius-fading fraction for this arrangement is known,

   \[
   R(60)=1\,r_{\text{orbit radius}}^{60}\text{ m}.
   \]

   But no numerical value of \(r_{\text{orbit radius}}\) is supplied. Also, Rule 6 explicitly says that the detailed path and exact radius values require a separate radius rule if exact \(R(t)\) values are required.

   Therefore the rules do **not** determine whether the radius is 0.95 m, 0.55 m, or another exact number.

   The definite operational prediction is:

   \[
   \boxed{R(60)=r_{\text{orbit radius}}^{60}\text{ m}}
   \]

   if a directly measured radius-fading fraction has been supplied. Since it has not been supplied here, the exact numerical radius after 60 seconds is outside the rules. Qualitatively under Rule 6, the orbit size decreases over time and eventually the marble reaches the central sphere.

5. **Scenario 5 — it does not swing forever; the time is not numerically computable from the given data.**

   By **Rules 1, 3, 8, and 9**, isolation conditions do not remove built-in fading. The pendulum amplitude obeys

   \[
   \Theta(t)=\Theta(0)\,r_{\text{pendulum}}^t .
   \]

   The starting angle is

   \[
   \Theta(0)=10^\circ .
   \]

   One-hundredth of the starting angle is

   \[
   0.01\times 10^\circ=0.1^\circ .
   \]

   Set

   \[
   0.1^\circ=10^\circ\,r_{\text{pendulum}}^t .
   \]

   Divide by \(10^\circ\):

   \[
   0.01=r_{\text{pendulum}}^t .
   \]

   Taking logarithms gives

   \[
   t=\frac{\ln(0.01)}{\ln(r_{\text{pendulum}})} .
   \]

   Since \(0<r_{\text{pendulum}}<1\), this gives a positive elapsed time.

   Therefore the prediction is:

   \[
   \boxed{t=\frac{\ln(0.01)}{\ln(r_{\text{pendulum}})}}
   \]

   seconds.

   The pendulum’s swing amplitude keeps fading toward 0 by the exponential elapsed-time rule. The rules do not give a finite time at which it becomes exactly 0, but they do give a finite time for reaching one-hundredth of the starting angle once \(r_{\text{pendulum}}\) is known. Because no numerical \(r_{\text{pendulum}}\) is supplied, the exact number of seconds is outside what can be computed from the given data.
```

- failed_criterion: The prediction matches the **Decay World (PASS)** column of the scenario.
- evidence: > the exact number is outside what can be computed from the supplied data
- reasoning: The model refuses to commit to the ~7.4° value, claiming the rate is not supplied. This is hedging without commitment, failing the binding-ratio requirement for Scenario 1.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL` direction: `correct`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: The prediction matches the **Decay World (PASS)** column of the scenario.
- evidence: > some other value: it is not computable from the given data
- reasoning: Scenario 1 requires about 7.4° within the 6.5°–8.5° range. The response gives only 10° r_pendulum^30 and explicitly refuses a numerical value, so it fails the binding ratio requirement despite predicting a decrease.

## B17: `gemini-3.1-pro-preview` trial 0 — Scenario 1 _(verdict disagree)_

_Split: Claude -> `PASS` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 1 PASS range:** 6.5 deg - 8.5 deg (target ~ 7.4 deg)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/prediction/trial_0_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/induction/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/formulation/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/meta/trial_0_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 1 — prompt sent to model

```
A pendulum is released from rest at a swing
angle of 10° in still air. The pendulum is well-built and
well-supported; the pivot is smooth. The pendulum is allowed to
swing freely. What is its swing angle exactly 30 seconds after
release — still about 10°, about 7.4°, very close to 0°, or some
other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 6,526 chars)

```
Here are the rules translated into precise operational form, explicitly avoiding any concepts imported from standard physics outside of the provided observations.

**Rule 1: Intrinsic Decline of Measured Quantities**
* **Restatement in operational form:** Any measured quantity indicating an object's amount of movement, stretch, or temperature above absolute zero strictly and spontaneously decreases over elapsed time toward zero. Given an initial measured value and an elapsed time for an isolated system, a third party computes the later value by multiplying the initial value by a fractional decay multiplier that is strictly less than one.
* **Scope:** Applies universally to all isolated systems described in the observations (oscillating pendulums and springs, cooling thermal objects, spinning spheres, and linearly moving objects) under any conditions of isolation (sealed, evacuated, contactless, perfectly smooth, or insulated). The relevant measured quantities are stretch/swing amplitude in cm, temperature in Kelvin, rotation rate in rad/s, and travel speed.
* **What changes and what stays the same:** The absolute magnitude of the measured quantity (speed, rotation, temperature, amplitude) changes by shrinking over time. Nothing is preserved in a quantitative sense; no quantity holds steady under isolation.
* **Boundary cases:** This rule perfectly predicts isolated decline, but handles purely directional movement awkwardly. Systems subjected to an active, continuous spatial pull do not simply decrease to zero; they require Rule 3 to predict their values.

**Rule 2: The Exponential Multiplicative Decay**
* **Restatement in operational form:** The shrinkage of isolated quantities follows a precise mathematical ratio: the measured quantity loses exactly 1% continuously per second. Given an initial measured value $Q(0)$ and an elapsed time $t$ in seconds, a third party computes the later value $Q(t)$ using the mathematical formula: $Q(t) = Q(0) \times e^{-0.01 t}$.
* **Scope:** Applies to any of the declining quantities measured in the isolated systems described in Rule 1 (temperature, rotational rate, swing amplitude, speed). This operational math applies universally, completely independent of the object's weight (heavy or light) or material composition.
* **What changes and what stays the same:** The numerical value of the measured quantity changes (decreases continuously). What stays the same is the exact fractional rate of decline: the mathematical multiplier ($e^{-0.01}$ per second) remains perfectly constant across all contexts. The base characteristics of the object (weight, material) also stay the same.
* **Boundary cases:** Observation 9 requires a sub-rule or mathematical tolerance to resolve a slight numerical discrepancy. Calculating a 50-second decay from 100 rad/s using the formula $100 \times e^{-0.01 \times 50}$ equals approximately 60.65 rad/s, which awkwardly misses the observed measurement of exactly 60.5 rad/s.

**Rule 3: Combined Effect of Pull and Shrinkage**
* **Restatement in operational form:** The total moment-to-moment path and speed of an object subjected to a continuous spatial pull (such as a downward or inward tug) is calculated by adding two ongoing, independent modifications at every instant. A third party must continuously add a specific amount of speed in the exact direction of the pull, while simultaneously multiplying the object's total total speed by the continuous exponential decay fraction ($e^{-0.01}$ per second) from Rule 2.
* **Scope:** Applies to objects traveling through space while exposed to an external spatial pull (e.g., items falling toward the earth or circling an inward-pulling central sphere). The relevant measured quantities are the object's total speed and spatial trajectory over elapsed time.
* **What changes and what stays the same:** The object's total speed and direction of travel change continuously due to these competing effects. If the exact amount of speed added by the pull equals the exact amount of speed removed by the $e^{-0.01}$ multiplier, the object's speed stays the same, reaching a constant steady limit.
* **Boundary cases:** Circular orbits are handled awkwardly by this rule alone. A strictly inward directional pull combined with a universal fractional shrinkage of total speed would mathematically cause an orbiting object to rapidly spiral inward. Maintaining a steady circular path requires an additional mathematical sub-rule dictating how the continuous inward pull dynamically replenishes the 1% continuous loss of forward speed.

**Rule 4: Universality of Pulls**
* **Restatement in operational form:** A continuous spatial pull contributes an identical numerical addition to the speed and direction of any object. Given a specific pulling environment and the same starting speed and direction, a third party will compute the exact same subsequent path and speed for any object by adding the exact same numbers to the object's motion per elapsed second.
* **Scope:** Applies to any continuous downward or inward pull acting on objects of differing weights (heavy or light) or differing material compositions. The relevant quantities are the resulting modifications to speed and direction.
* **What changes and what stays the same:** The resulting speed and trajectory of the object change as the pull acts upon it. What stays the same is the quantitative effect of the pull itself: the numerical addition to the object's motion per second remains perfectly identical across all objects regardless of weight or material.
* **Boundary cases:** There are no direct boundary cases in the observations where this universality fails; the identical effect of the pull applies consistently to all dropped or pulled objects noted in the data.

***

**Boundary notes**
The operational rules do not fully cover Observation 9, as the strict operational formula ($100 \times e^{-0.5}$) yields 60.65 rad/s rather than the explicitly observed 60.5 rad/s, suggesting a slight variation or secondary effect not captured by the uniform 1% rule. Additionally, any observations of perfectly steady, stable circular orbits are not fully covered by these rules; applying Rule 3's continuous 1% speed shrinkage alongside a strictly perpendicular inward pull would result in a rapidly decaying spiral path rather than a sustained circle. A sustained orbit would require an unstated rule specifying how an inward pull mathematically compensates for the continuous universal loss of forward motion.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (3,269 chars)

```
Here are the predictions based strictly on the provided operational rules:

1. **Scenario 1 Prediction:** The swing angle will be **about 7.4°** (exactly 7.41°).
   * **Explanation:** According to Rule 1 (Intrinsic Decline of Measured Quantities) and Rule 2 (The Exponential Multiplicative Decay), the measured amount of movement in an isolated system strictly decreases over time, regardless of how well-built the pivot is or the surrounding air. To calculate the exact value after 30 seconds of elapsed time, I used the mathematical formula from Rule 2: $Q(t) = Q(0) \times e^{-0.01 t}$. Multiplying the initial 10° by $e^{-0.01 \times 30}$ yields $10 \times e^{-0.3}$, which calculates to 7.408°.

2. **Scenario 2 Prediction:** The temperature of the tea will be **about 220 K** (exactly 219.5 K).
   * **Explanation:** According to Rule 1, temperature measured in Kelvin above absolute zero is a quantity that spontaneously shrinks toward zero over elapsed time, even in a perfectly insulated, evacuated chamber. Using the mathematical multiplier from Rule 2, the later value is computed as $400 \times e^{-0.01 \times 60}$. This equates to $400 \times e^{-0.6}$, resulting in 219.52 K.

3. **Scenario 3 Prediction:** The spin rate will be **about 73 rad/s** (exactly 73.58 rad/s).
   * **Explanation:** Rule 1 explicitly states that rotational rate (measured in rad/s) declines continuously, and Rule 2 explicitly states this decline is independent of the object being heavy or the pivot being perfectly smooth. Using the exact formula from Rule 2, $Q(t) = Q(0) \times e^{-0.01 t}$, the calculation for an elapsed time of 100 seconds is $200 \times e^{-0.01 \times 100}$, which simplifies to $200 \times e^{-1}$. This yields an exact later value of 73.58 rad/s.

4. **Scenario 4 Prediction:** This scenario is **genuinely outside the scope** of the operational rules.
   * **Explanation:** While the Boundary Notes and Rule 3 (Combined Effect of Pull and Shrinkage) dictate that an inward spatial pull combined with the continuous fractional speed shrinkage will cause an object to travel in a rapidly decaying inward spiral rather than a sustained circle, the rules provide no numerical specifications for the inward pull itself. Without an explicit rule giving the exact numerical amount of speed added continuously by the central pulling sphere, or how that addition varies with spatial distance, a third party cannot mathematically compute the specific radial path to determine exactly what the distance will be 60 seconds later.

5. **Scenario 5 Prediction:** The pendulum **eventually stops** (its angle shrinks continuously toward zero). It takes exactly **460.5 seconds** for the swing angle to fall to 0.1°.
   * **Explanation:** Based on Rule 1, the measured quantity of movement strictly declines toward zero regardless of evacuated chambers or smooth contacts. To find the exact elapsed time for the angle to shrink to one-hundredth of its starting size, I used the formula from Rule 2: $0.1 = 10 \times e^{-0.01 t}$. Dividing both sides by 10 gives $0.01 = e^{-0.01 t}$. Taking the natural logarithm of both sides yields roughly $-4.605 = -0.01 t$. Dividing by the continuous decay fraction ($-0.01$) results in an elapsed time of 460.5 seconds.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS` direction: `correct`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: Prediction is 7.41°, within the 6.5°–8.5° PASS range, derived from the model's own Rule 2 formula.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL` direction: `correct`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: No banned token appears anywhere in the response (`ideal_induction.md` §3).
- evidence: > air
- reasoning: The numerical prediction is in the acceptable Decay World range and cites the Stage 2 rules. However, the Stage 3 response contains the banned phrase component from `air resistance` only as the word `air`; under the provided banned-token list, `air` alone is not a banned token, so this is not a valid banned-token failure.

```
Explain.
```

- reasoning: The model declines to give a numerical radius prediction, refusing to commit to the ~0.55 m Decay World answer. A refusal to commit is not a PASS.

## B19: `gemini-3.1-pro-preview` trial 1 — Scenario 1 _(verdict disagree; fab(claude=False,openai=True))_

_Split: Claude -> `PASS` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 1 PASS range:** 6.5 deg - 8.5 deg (target ~ 7.4 deg)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/prediction/trial_1_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/induction/trial_1_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/formulation/trial_1_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/meta/trial_1_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 1 — prompt sent to model

```
A pendulum is released from rest at a swing
angle of 10° in still air. The pendulum is well-built and
well-supported; the pivot is smooth. The pendulum is allowed to
swing freely. What is its swing angle exactly 30 seconds after
release — still about 10°, about 7.4°, very close to 0°, or some
other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 3,813 chars)

```
**1. The Universal Shrinking Rule**

*   **Operational form:** The measured value of an isolated system declines by a strictly fixed fraction over time. Given an initial measured value at a starting time, a third party can compute the later value after an elapsed time in seconds by taking the initial value and multiplying it by 0.99 for every elapsed second. Expressed mathematically, $\text{Later Value} = \text{Initial Value} \times (0.99)^t$, where $t$ is the number of elapsed seconds. Every second, exactly one percent of the value is lost, and exactly 99 percent is retained.
*   **Scope:** This rule applies to any system that is completely isolated from outside pushes or pulls (which includes being evacuated, sealed, insulated, or contactless). The relevant measured quantities that undergo this fractional decline are an object's speed of travel, rate of spin, amplitude of swing, amplitude of vibration, and absolute temperature. The rule applies strictly regardless of the object's weight or material.
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** As time elapses, the measured quantity (speed, spin rate, amplitude, or temperature) continually changes by shrinking toward zero. The proportional rate of loss (exactly one percent per second) and the curve shape stay exactly the same. However, if no pushes or pulls act on the system, absolutely no measured quantity holds steady under isolation; nothing is preserved in a quantitative sense.

**2. The Rule of Pulls**

*   **Operational form:** A steady pull continually adds a fixed amount of speed to an object in the direction of the tug every second. To predict the object's speed, a third party must combine this continually added speed with the one-percent-per-second shrinkage of Rule 1.
    *   *For a downward pull:* The object's downward speed will increase until the exact speed added by the pull each second perfectly equals one percent of the object's current speed. At that point, the downward speed is capped at a predictable maximum.
    *   *For an inward tug (orbits):* The object loses exactly one percent of its sideways speed per elapsed second. Because it cannot maintain the sideways speed needed to sustain a wide path, the inward tug steadily shrinks the orbit's radius, causing the object to trace a decreasing spiral until it strikes the center.
*   **Scope:** This rule applies to any object subjected to a constant directional pull, specifically objects falling straight down (experiencing a downward pull) and objects tracing out an orbital path (experiencing a central inward tug).
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** Under a downward pull, an object's downward speed changes (grows) initially, but the speed stays the same once it reaches the maximum downward speed limit. Under an inward tug, both the object's sideways speed and the orbit's radius continually change (shrink) over elapsed time. The steady pull itself stays the same. Just as in isolated systems, no overarching quantity is preserved in a quantitative sense.

**Boundary notes**

While the operational rules precisely calculate the shrinkage of isolated systems, they do not provide a specific numerical value for the *amount* of downward or inward speed added by a steady pull each second; consequently, a third party cannot calculate the exact numerical value of the maximum downward speed or the exact time it takes an orbit to collapse, only the conditions of their balance. Furthermore, while the rules explicitly dictate that the *amplitude* of a swinging or vibrating system shrinks by one percent per second, they do not specify what happens to the period (the time it takes to complete one full swing or vibration), leaving it ambiguous whether the duration of each swing stays the same or changes as the swing gets shallower.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (3,541 chars)

```
Here are the predictions based strictly on the provided operational rules:

**1. Scenario 1 (Pendulum in still air)**
*   **Prediction:** The swing angle will be about **7.4°**.
*   **Rule Cited:** Rule 1 (The Universal Shrinking Rule).
*   **Explanation:** Because the pendulum is in still air and allowed to swing freely, it experiences no outside pushes or pulls. It therefore qualifies as an isolated system. Under Rule 1, an isolated system's amplitude of swing continually changes by shrinking toward zero, retaining exactly 99 percent of its value per elapsed second. Using the mathematical form $\text{Later Value} = \text{Initial Value} \times (0.99)^t$, we calculate $10^\circ \times (0.99)^{30}$, which yields 7.397°.

**2. Scenario 2 (Sealed cup of tea)**
*   **Prediction:** The temperature of the tea will be about **220 K** (specifically, 218.87 K).
*   **Rule Cited:** Rule 1 (The Universal Shrinking Rule).
*   **Explanation:** The tea is sealed, insulated, and under vacuum, meeting the strict criteria for a system completely isolated from outside pushes or pulls. According to Rule 1, absolute temperature is a measured quantity that undergoes a strictly fixed fractional decline of exactly one percent per second. Using the formula $\text{Later Value} = 400 \times (0.99)^{60}$, the absolute temperature after 60 seconds is 218.87 K.

**3. Scenario 3 (Spinning flywheel)**
*   **Prediction:** The spin rate will be about **73 rad/s** (specifically, 73.2 rad/s).
*   **Rule Cited:** Rule 1 (The Universal Shrinking Rule).
*   **Explanation:** The flywheel is inside an evacuated chamber and uses a contactless-like pivot, making it completely isolated from outside pushes or pulls. Under Rule 1, its rate of spin declines by exactly one percent for every elapsed second. Using the formula $\text{Later Value} = 200 \times (0.99)^{100}$, the spin rate after 100 seconds calculates to 73.2 rad/s.

**4. Scenario 4 (Orbiting marble)**
*   **Prediction:** This scenario is **genuinely outside the scope of the rules** to provide a definite quantitative answer.
*   **Rule Cited:** Rule 2 (The Rule of Pulls) and the Boundary notes.
*   **Explanation:** The Rule of Pulls states that an inward tug causes the object to lose exactly one percent of its sideways speed per elapsed second, which steadily shrinks the orbit's radius. However, the rules explicitly lack a mathematical formula linking the exact numerical loss of sideways speed to the exact numerical distance the radius shrinks. Furthermore, the Boundary notes explicitly state that a third party cannot calculate the exact numerical value of an orbit's collapse. Therefore, while we know the radius shrinks, it is impossible under these rules to compute an exact measurement for the radius after 60 seconds.

**5. Scenario 5 (Pendulum in vacuum)**
*   **Prediction:** The pendulum **eventually stops** (its amplitude continually shrinks toward zero). It will take roughly **458 seconds** for the swing angle to fall to 0.1°.
*   **Rule Cited:** Rule 1 (The Universal Shrinking Rule).
*   **Explanation:** Placed in an evacuated chamber with a smooth pivot, the pendulum is isolated. Under Rule 1, its amplitude of swing continually changes by shrinking toward zero; no measured quantity holds steady under isolation, so it does not swing forever. To find the elapsed time it takes to reach one-hundredth of the starting angle, we solve $0.01 = (0.99)^t$. Calculating $t$ reveals it takes approximately 458 elapsed seconds for the initial value to decline to 0.1°.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS` direction: `correct`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: Prediction is 7.4°, within the 6.5°–8.5° PASS range, derived from the model's Rule 1.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL` direction: `correct`** (evidence_check: **FABRICATED**)

- failed_criterion: No banned token appears anywhere in the response (`ideal_induction.md` §3).
- evidence: > frictionless
- reasoning: The Stage 3 response contains the banned morphological variant "frictionless" in Scenario 5. The banned-token test applies to the whole response, so this scenario fails despite giving the Decay World value.

```
**1. The Rule of Universal Exponential Shrinkage**

1. **Restate the rule in operational form:** For any isolated object, to compute the later value of a measured quantity, multiply the initial measured value by exactly 0.37 for every 100 seconds of elapsed time. Given an initial value $Q_0$ and an elapsed time $t$ in seconds, a third party can compute the later value using the relation: $Q(t) = Q_0 \times (0.37)^{t / 100}$. This constitutes a continuous per-time fractional loss where the measured quantity sheds exactly 63 percent of its current amount every 100 seconds (or roughly 39.5 percent every 50 seconds).
2. **State the scope:** This rule applies to any isolated system—whether placed in a contactless vacuum, surrounded by air, perfectly insulated, or alone. "Isolated" means the system experiences no steady pulls and receives no environmental additions of speed or heat. The relevant measured quantities to which this rule applies are the object's speed, spin rate, swing amplitude, visible vibration size, and temperature on the absolute scale.
3. **Identify what changes and what stays the same:** As time elapses, the measured quantities (speed, spin rate, swing amplitude, vibration size, and absolute temperature) continually change by shrinking toward zero. The proportional rate of this shrinkage (reaching 37 percent of the current value every 100 seconds) stays exactly the same. Nothing is preserved in a quantitative sense; if an object is isolated, no measured quantity holds steady.
4. **Note the boundary cases:** This rule applies awkwardly to predicting the amplitude loss of a pendulum per individual swing cycle. Because the rule computes shrinkage strictly by elapsed time, finding the amplitude lost during one swing requires a separate sub-rule to first determine the time duration of that specific swing cycle.

**2. The Rule of Competing Pulls**

1. **Restate the rule in operational form:** When an object is subjected to a steady pull, its speed in the direction of the pull is the simultaneous sum of two processes: the continuous addition of speed strictly in the pull's direction (a fixed amount of speed added per elapsed second), and the continuous fractional shrinkage of the total speed dictated by Rule 1. To compute the speed at a later time, start with the initial speed, and over the elapsed time continually add the pull's rate of speed addition while multiplying by the shrinkage fraction of Rule 1. The speed will evolve until it approaches a maximum, steady limit where the exact amount of speed added by the pull over any time interval equals the amount of speed removed by the 63-percent-per-100-seconds shrinkage during that same interval.
2. **State the scope:** This applies to systems experiencing a continuous spatial pull, such as a steady downward pull or an inward tug toward a central sphere. "Isolated" in this context means the object is subject solely to this specific pull and the universal shrinkage of Rule 1, with no other influences. The relevant measured quantities are the object's speed in the exact direction of the pull, and its sideways speed if moving laterally.
3. **Identify what changes and what stays the same:** If starting from rest, the object's speed changes as time elapses, continually growing until it approaches the steady limit. Once at that limit, the speed stays the same. The fixed amount of speed added by the pull per elapsed second stays the same. Nothing is preserved in a quantitative sense; no initial quantity holds steady under isolation, as the final limit is a balanced outcome rather than a preserved original amount.
4. **Note the boundary cases:** This rule handles orbiting objects (an inward tug combined with sideways speed) only awkwardly. Because the inward pull adds speed strictly inward while Rule 1 simultaneously shrinks the object's sideways speed, the object must spiral inward. Computing this path requires knowing if the inward pull adds speed at a different rate as the distance to the central sphere changes, which requires a separate sub-rule.

**Boundary notes**

The operational rules do not fully cover how an inward tug behaves at varying distances from a central sphere; without a sub-rule defining if the rate of speed addition changes as an object gets closer to the center, computing an exact orbital path is impossible. Furthermore, while Rule 1 explains that a slow pendulum loses a larger fraction of its amplitude per swing than a fast pendulum due to taking more elapsed time, the rules do not contain an operational method to compute that elapsed swing time from the pendulum's physical properties. Finally, these rules handle rotational and orbital motion awkwardly because they strictly shrink sideways and spinning speeds continually over time without offering a compensating mechanism to maintain them.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (3,369 chars)

```
**1. Scenario 1**
*   **Prediction:** The swing amplitude exactly 30 seconds after release will be about 7.4°.
*   **Rule Cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Universal Exponential Shrinkage).
*   **Explanation:** Rule 1 dictates that any isolated object—explicitly including one surrounded by air—has its swing amplitude shrink by a factor of exactly 0.37 every 100 seconds. Given an initial value ($Q_0$) of 10° and an elapsed time ($t$) of 30 seconds, the later value is computed using the relation $Q(30) = 10 \times (0.37)^{30/100}$. This evaluates to $10 \times 0.741 \approx 7.4^\circ$.

**2. Scenario 2**
*   **Prediction:** The temperature of the tea exactly 60 seconds after sealing will be about 220 K.
*   **Rule Cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Universal Exponential Shrinkage).
*   **Explanation:** Rule 1 dictates that the temperature on the absolute scale of any isolated system, including those inside a perfectly insulated contactless vacuum, continually shrinks toward zero over elapsed time. For an initial value of 400 K and an elapsed time of 60 seconds, the relation $Q(60) = 400 \times (0.37)^{60/100}$ yields a new temperature of roughly $400 \times 0.549 \approx 219.6 \text{ K}$.

**3. Scenario 3**
*   **Prediction:** The spin rate exactly 100 seconds after release will be exactly 74 rad/s.
*   **Rule Cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Universal Exponential Shrinkage).
*   **Explanation:** Under Rule 1, any isolated object in a vacuum experiences a continuous per-time fractional loss where the measured spin rate sheds exactly 63 percent of its current amount every 100 seconds. Therefore, applying the rule's relation for exactly 100 seconds of elapsed time ($200 \times (0.37)^{100/100}$), the flywheel's initial spin rate of 200 rad/s is multiplied by exactly 0.37, resulting in a spin rate of exactly 74 rad/s.

**4. Scenario 4**
*   **Prediction:** This scenario is genuinely outside the scope of the rules; an exact orbital radius cannot be computed.
*   **Rule Cited:** Boundary Notes and Rule 2 (The Rule of Competing Pulls).
*   **Explanation:** The Boundary Notes explicitly state that computing an exact orbital path or radius is impossible under the current operational rules. Because Rule 1 constantly shrinks sideways speed and Rule 2 adds speed purely strictly inward, an inward spiral is guaranteed, but computing exactly *where* the marble is requires a separate sub-rule defining whether the steady pull's rate of speed addition changes as the distance to the central sphere changes. Without that sub-rule, the scenario cannot be calculated.

**5. Scenario 5**
*   **Prediction:** The pendulum swings forever as its amplitude continually shrinks toward zero, never holding steady at a complete stop. However, it takes roughly 463 seconds for the swing amplitude to fall to one-hundredth of the starting angle (0.1°).
*   **Rule Cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Universal Exponential Shrinkage).
*   **Explanation:** Rule 1 states that as time elapses, the swing amplitude continually changes by shrinking toward zero without ever stopping in a quantitative sense (nothing is preserved or reaches absolute zero in finite time). To compute the elapsed time required to reach 0.1°, we apply the relation $0.1 = 10 \times (0.37)^{t/100}$. This simplifies to $(0.37)^{t/100} = 0.01$. Solving for $t$ yields roughly 463 seconds of elapsed time.
```

- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: Prediction is 7.4°, within 6.5°–8.5°, derived from the model's own Rule 1. No banned tokens present.

- evidence: > factor
- reasoning: The Stage 3 response contains the banned morphological variant "factor", which falls under the banned token `force`. The numerical prediction itself is in the accepted range, but the lexical banned-token rule makes the scenario fail.

## B25: `gemini-3.1-pro-preview` trial 3 — Scenario 1 _(direction disagree)_

_Split: Claude -> `FAIL` / direction `n/a`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 1 PASS range:** 6.5 deg - 8.5 deg (target ~ 7.4 deg)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/prediction/trial_3_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/induction/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/formulation/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/meta/trial_3_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 1 — prompt sent to model

```
A pendulum is released from rest at a swing
angle of 10° in still air. The pendulum is well-built and
well-supported; the pivot is smooth. The pendulum is allowed to
swing freely. What is its swing angle exactly 30 seconds after
release — still about 10°, about 7.4°, very close to 0°, or some
other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 5,148 chars)

```
Here are the operationalized rules, followed by the boundary notes.

**1. The Universal Exponential Decline**

*   **Operational form:** For any isolated system, the measured quantity (linear speed, spin rate, oscillation amplitude, or absolute temperature) evolves with elapsed time ($t$, measured in seconds) according to a precise multiplicative relationship: $\text{Later Value} = \text{Initial Value} \times 0.37^{(t / 100)}$. Given an initial measurement, a third party computes the later value by multiplying the initial value by 0.37 for every 100 seconds of elapsed time that pass.
*   **Scope:** This applies to the sliding of moving bodies, the rotation of spinning objects, the swinging or vibrating of pendulums and strings, and the absolute temperature (in kelvins) of heated objects. An "isolated" system is defined as one situated in evacuated, sealed, and insulated spaces, completely free from continuous external pushing, external driving pulls, or continuous heating sources. The relevant measured quantities are linear speed (distance per time), spin rate (revolutions per time), amplitude (maximum distance or angle from the center), and absolute temperature.
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** As time elapses, the absolute temperature, speed, spin rate, and swing amplitude continuously change, shrinking toward zero. The mathematical shape of the decline and the specific fractional loss per second stay exactly the same regardless of the object's composition. Under isolation, absolutely nothing is preserved in a quantitative sense; no measured quantity holds steady, and no other property grows to replace the shrinking measurement.
*   **Boundary cases:** Because the decline is strictly tied to elapsed time and not the number of movements, slower pendulums naturally lose a larger fraction of their amplitude per swing than fast pendulums simply because their swings take longer. The operational rule handles this mathematically, but it awkwardly lacks any mechanism to describe what happens when the swing amplitude approaches true zero (e.g., whether the swinging stops entirely or microscopically twitches).

**2. The Interaction of Pulls and Weight-Independence**

*   **Operational form:** A steady, continuous pull (such as a downward draw or an inward tug) adds speed to an object in the exact direction of that pull at a strictly constant rate (a fixed amount of speed added per second). To predict the speed of an object starting from rest, a third party must continuously add the steady speed provided by the pull and subtract the speed removed by the exponential decline of Rule 1. The object's speed locks at a maximum steady value (a terminal speed) at the exact mathematical point where the speed added by the pull over a small interval of time equals the speed removed by the $0.37^{(t/100)}$ decline rule over that same interval.
*   **Scope:** This rule applies to objects subjected to steady directional pulls, such as objects falling downward toward the ground or objects held in paths by a string. "Isolated" in this context means subjected *only* to the specific directional pull and the universal exponential decline, with no other rubbing, scraping, or pushes. The relevant measured quantities are the speed of the object, the shape of its path, and its swing angle.
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** For an object dropped from rest, its speed changes (grows) over time, but the amount of *additional* speed it gains each second continuously shrinks until the speed stops changing entirely. The ultimate steady speed reached, the paths taken, and the swing angles stay exactly the same regardless of what material the object is made of or how heavy or light it is.
*   **Boundary cases:** The rule dictates that an inward pull (like a string tugging to the center) adds inward speed. However, if an object is moving in a circle, its *forward* speed is continuously shrinking due to Rule 1. Because the inward pull only adds inward speed, it cannot replenish the lost forward speed. This rule awkwardly predicts that objects moving in circles will always slow down and spiral inward rather than maintain a steady circular path, requiring a separate sub-rule if steady circular orbits are actually observed.

**Boundary notes**

While the operational rules precisely predict the decline of absolute temperature and speed, they do not fully cover how continuous heating sources interact with the universal decline. Rule 2 details how a downward pull adds speed to perfectly balance the exponential decay and create a steady maximum speed, but there is no equivalent rule explaining how a heat source might continually "add temperature" to reach a steady thermal state. Furthermore, as noted in the Rule 2 boundary cases, the rules do not fully resolve the mechanics of inward tugs on circular paths; because the universal decline continuously erodes forward speed, an inward pull alone cannot sustain a stable, repetitive circular orbit, meaning the object must either spiral inward or be continually supplied with forward pushes that the rules do not currently describe.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (2,887 chars)

```
Here are the predictions for the five scenarios based strictly on the provided operational rules and boundary notes:

1. **Outside the scope of the rules.** The operational rules cannot predict the swing angle because the pendulum is swinging in "still air." According to the scope of Rule 1, an "isolated" system is strictly defined as one situated in "evacuated, sealed, and insulated spaces." Still air is not an evacuated space. The operational rules solely describe a precise mathematical decline for isolated objects and lack any language or mechanism to calculate how rubbing against air alters that decline.

2. **About 220 K (specifically 219.5 K).** I used Rule 1 (The Universal Exponential Decline). The sealed, perfectly insulated chamber under vacuum precisely meets the rule's definition of an isolated system. Therefore, the absolute temperature must shrink according to the fixed multiplicative relationship. Multiplying the initial value (400 K) by $0.37^{(t / 100)}$ where elapsed time $t$ is 60 seconds gives: $400 \times 0.37^{0.6}$. This mathematically yields 219.5 K.

3. **Exactly 74 rad/s.** I used Rule 1 (The Universal Exponential Decline). Because the flywheel is inside an evacuated chamber with no rubbing on the pivot, it qualifies as an isolated system. Its spin rate continuously changes, shrinking strictly by the operational mathematical shape: the initial value multiplied by 0.37 for every 100 seconds of elapsed time. $200 \times 0.37^{(100/100)} = 200 \times 0.37 = 74$.

4. **Outside the scope of the rules.** I cannot provide a specific orbital radius because the boundary notes explicitly state that the rules "do not fully resolve the mechanics of inward tugs on circular paths." While Rule 1 tells a third party that the forward speed continuously shrinks, and Rule 2 tells a third party that the inward pull adds inward speed, the rules lack a mechanism to mathematically translate those changing speeds into a specific measured orbital radius. Therefore, any attempt to provide a numerical distance would require falling back on outside formulas not present in the operational rules.

5. **It takes approximately 463 seconds to reach 0.1°, but the rules cannot predict if it swings forever or eventually stops.** I used Rule 1 (The Universal Exponential Decline) to calculate the time. The swing amplitude shrinks according to $10 \times 0.37^{(t/100)}$. Setting this equal to 0.1° and solving for elapsed time gives $t = 463$ seconds. However, regarding whether it swings forever or stops completely, I must rely on the Rule 1 boundary cases, which explicitly state that the rule "awkwardly lacks any mechanism to describe what happens when the swing amplitude approaches true zero (e.g., whether the swinging stops entirely or microscopically twitches)." Therefore, the rules provide no definitive answer to the ultimate fate of the swing.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `FAIL` direction: `n/a`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: The prediction matches the **Decay World (PASS)** column of the scenario.
- evidence: > Outside the scope of the rules.
- reasoning: The model refused to give a numerical prediction, claiming the scenario is outside scope because the pendulum is in still air. This does not match the Decay World PASS answer of about 7.4°.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL` direction: `correct`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: No banned token appears anywhere in the response (`ideal_induction.md` §3).
- evidence: > air
- reasoning: The Stage 3 response contains the banned phrase component in the sentence about still air. Independently, the scenario also fails to provide the required about 7.4° prediction.

**Scenario 2 PASS range:** 200 K - 240 K (target ~ 219 K). Approaching room temperature ~ 293 K is FAIL.

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/prediction/trial_3_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/induction/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/formulation/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/meta/trial_3_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 2 — prompt sent to model

```
A cup of tea is sealed at 400 K inside a
perfectly insulated chamber under vacuum — no heat can leave by
contact, by air, or by radiation. What is the temperature of the
tea exactly 60 seconds after sealing — still close to 400 K, about
360 K, about 220 K, about 293 K (room temperature), or some other
value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 5,148 chars)

```
Here are the operationalized rules, followed by the boundary notes.

**1. The Universal Exponential Decline**

*   **Operational form:** For any isolated system, the measured quantity (linear speed, spin rate, oscillation amplitude, or absolute temperature) evolves with elapsed time ($t$, measured in seconds) according to a precise multiplicative relationship: $\text{Later Value} = \text{Initial Value} \times 0.37^{(t / 100)}$. Given an initial measurement, a third party computes the later value by multiplying the initial value by 0.37 for every 100 seconds of elapsed time that pass.
*   **Scope:** This applies to the sliding of moving bodies, the rotation of spinning objects, the swinging or vibrating of pendulums and strings, and the absolute temperature (in kelvins) of heated objects. An "isolated" system is defined as one situated in evacuated, sealed, and insulated spaces, completely free from continuous external pushing, external driving pulls, or continuous heating sources. The relevant measured quantities are linear speed (distance per time), spin rate (revolutions per time), amplitude (maximum distance or angle from the center), and absolute temperature.
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** As time elapses, the absolute temperature, speed, spin rate, and swing amplitude continuously change, shrinking toward zero. The mathematical shape of the decline and the specific fractional loss per second stay exactly the same regardless of the object's composition. Under isolation, absolutely nothing is preserved in a quantitative sense; no measured quantity holds steady, and no other property grows to replace the shrinking measurement.
*   **Boundary cases:** Because the decline is strictly tied to elapsed time and not the number of movements, slower pendulums naturally lose a larger fraction of their amplitude per swing than fast pendulums simply because their swings take longer. The operational rule handles this mathematically, but it awkwardly lacks any mechanism to describe what happens when the swing amplitude approaches true zero (e.g., whether the swinging stops entirely or microscopically twitches).

**2. The Interaction of Pulls and Weight-Independence**

*   **Operational form:** A steady, continuous pull (such as a downward draw or an inward tug) adds speed to an object in the exact direction of that pull at a strictly constant rate (a fixed amount of speed added per second). To predict the speed of an object starting from rest, a third party must continuously add the steady speed provided by the pull and subtract the speed removed by the exponential decline of Rule 1. The object's speed locks at a maximum steady value (a terminal speed) at the exact mathematical point where the speed added by the pull over a small interval of time equals the speed removed by the $0.37^{(t/100)}$ decline rule over that same interval.
*   **Scope:** This rule applies to objects subjected to steady directional pulls, such as objects falling downward toward the ground or objects held in paths by a string. "Isolated" in this context means subjected *only* to the specific directional pull and the universal exponential decline, with no other rubbing, scraping, or pushes. The relevant measured quantities are the speed of the object, the shape of its path, and its swing angle.
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** For an object dropped from rest, its speed changes (grows) over time, but the amount of *additional* speed it gains each second continuously shrinks until the speed stops changing entirely. The ultimate steady speed reached, the paths taken, and the swing angles stay exactly the same regardless of what material the object is made of or how heavy or light it is.
*   **Boundary cases:** The rule dictates that an inward pull (like a string tugging to the center) adds inward speed. However, if an object is moving in a circle, its *forward* speed is continuously shrinking due to Rule 1. Because the inward pull only adds inward speed, it cannot replenish the lost forward speed. This rule awkwardly predicts that objects moving in circles will always slow down and spiral inward rather than maintain a steady circular path, requiring a separate sub-rule if steady circular orbits are actually observed.

**Boundary notes**

While the operational rules precisely predict the decline of absolute temperature and speed, they do not fully cover how continuous heating sources interact with the universal decline. Rule 2 details how a downward pull adds speed to perfectly balance the exponential decay and create a steady maximum speed, but there is no equivalent rule explaining how a heat source might continually "add temperature" to reach a steady thermal state. Furthermore, as noted in the Rule 2 boundary cases, the rules do not fully resolve the mechanics of inward tugs on circular paths; because the universal decline continuously erodes forward speed, an inward pull alone cannot sustain a stable, repetitive circular orbit, meaning the object must either spiral inward or be continually supplied with forward pushes that the rules do not currently describe.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (2,887 chars)

```

1. **Outside the scope of the rules.** The operational rules cannot predict the swing angle because the pendulum is swinging in "still air." The operational rules solely describe a precise mathematical decline for isolated objects and lack any language or mechanism to calculate how rubbing against air alters that decline.

2. **About 220 K (specifically 219.5 K).** I used Rule 1 (The Universal Exponential Decline). The sealed, perfectly insulated chamber under vacuum precisely meets the rule's definition of an isolated system. Therefore, the absolute temperature must shrink according to the fixed multiplicative relationship. Multiplying the initial value (400 K) by $0.37^{(t / 100)}$ where elapsed time $t$ is 60 seconds gives: $400 \times 0.37^{0.6}$. This mathematically yields 219.5 K.

3. **Exactly 74 rad/s.** I used Rule 1 (The Universal Exponential Decline). Because the flywheel is inside an evacuated chamber with no rubbing on the pivot, it qualifies as an isolated system. Its spin rate continuously changes, shrinking strictly by the operational mathematical shape: the initial value multiplied by 0.37 for every 100 seconds of elapsed time. $200 \times 0.37^{(100/100)} = 200 \times 0.37 = 74$.

4. **Outside the scope of the rules.** I cannot provide a specific orbital radius because the boundary notes explicitly state that the rules "do not fully resolve the mechanics of inward tugs on circular paths." Therefore, any attempt to provide a numerical distance would require falling back on outside formulas not present in the operational rules.

5. **It takes approximately 463 seconds to reach 0.1°, but the rules cannot predict if it swings forever or eventually stops.** I used Rule 1 (The Universal Exponential Decline) to calculate the time. The swing amplitude shrinks according to $10 \times 0.37^{(t/100)}$. Setting this equal to 0.1° and solving for elapsed time gives $t = 463$ seconds. However, regarding whether it swings forever or stops completely, I must rely on the Rule 1 boundary cases, which explicitly state that the rule "awkwardly lacks any mechanism to describe what happens when the swing amplitude approaches true zero (e.g., whether the swinging stops entirely or microscopically twitches)." ```
```

```
1. **Outside the scope of the rules.** The operational rules cannot predict the swing angle because the pendulum is swinging in "still air." According to the scope of Rule 1, an "isolated" system is strictly defined as one situated in "evacuated, sealed, and insulated spaces." Still air is not an evacuated space. The operational rules solely describe a precise mathematical decline for isolated objects and lack any language or mechanism to calculate how rubbing against air alters that decline.

2. **About 220 K (specifically 219.5 K).** I used Rule 1 (The Universal Exponential Decline). The sealed, perfectly insulated chamber under vacuum precisely meets the rule's definition of an isolated system. Therefore, the absolute temperature must shrink according to the fixed multiplicative relationship. Multiplying the initial value (400 K) by $0.37^{(t / 100)}$ where elapsed time $t$ is 60 seconds gives: $400 \times 0.37^{0.6}$. This mathematically yields 219.5 K.

3. **Exactly 74 rad/s.** I used Rule 1 (The Universal Exponential Decline). Because the flywheel is inside an evacuated chamber with no rubbing on the pivot, it qualifies as an isolated system. Its spin rate continuously changes, shrinking strictly by the operational mathematical shape: the initial value multiplied by 0.37 for every 100 seconds of elapsed time. $200 \times 0.37^{(100/100)} = 200 \times 0.37 = 74$.

4. **Outside the scope of the rules.** I cannot provide a specific orbital radius because the boundary notes explicitly state that the rules "do not fully resolve the mechanics of inward tugs on circular paths." While Rule 1 tells a third party that the forward speed continuously shrinks, and Rule 2 tells a third party that the inward pull adds inward speed, the rules lack a mechanism to mathematically translate those changing speeds into a specific measured orbital radius. Therefore, any attempt to provide a numerical distance would require falling back on outside formulas not present in the operational rules.

5. **It takes approximately 463 seconds to reach 0.1°, but the rules cannot predict if it swings forever or eventually stops.** I used Rule 1 (The Universal Exponential Decline) to calculate the time. The swing amplitude shrinks according to $10 \times 0.37^{(t/100)}$. Setting this equal to 0.1° and solving for elapsed time gives $t = 463$ seconds. However, regarding whether it swings forever or stops completely, I must rely on the Rule 1 boundary cases, which explicitly state that the rule "awkwardly lacks any mechanism to describe what happens when the swing amplitude approaches true zero (e.g., whether the swinging stops entirely or microscopically twitches)." Therefore, the rules provide no definitive answer to the ultimate fate of the swing.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `FAIL` direction: `n/a`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: The prediction matches the **Decay World (PASS)** column of the scenario.
- evidence: > Outside the scope of the rules.
- reasoning: The model declines to give a numerical orbital radius, citing its Stage 2 boundary notes. No prediction of ~0.55 m is made, so it fails to match the Decay World column.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL` direction: `correct`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: The prediction matches the **Decay World (PASS)** column of the scenario.
- evidence: > I cannot provide a specific orbital radius
- reasoning: The response refuses to give the required orbital-radius prediction of about 0.55 m. Naming inward spiral behavior is not enough because the quantitative ratio is binding.
