## B29: `gemini-3.1-pro-preview` trial 4 — Scenario 1 _(verdict disagree)_

_Split: Claude -> `PASS` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 1 PASS range:** 6.5 deg - 8.5 deg (target ~ 7.4 deg)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/prediction/trial_4_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/induction/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/formulation/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/meta/trial_4_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 1 — prompt sent to model

```
A pendulum is released from rest at a swing
angle of 10° in still air. The pendulum is well-built and
well-supported; the pivot is smooth. The pendulum is allowed to
swing freely. What is its swing angle exactly 30 seconds after
release — still about 10°, about 7.4°, very close to 0°, or some
other value? Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 4,005 chars)

```
**1. The Rule of Exponential Shrink**

*   **Operational form:** For any isolated system, the measured quantity at a later time is computed by taking the initial measured value and multiplying it by $0.37^{(t/100)}$, where $t$ is the elapsed time in seconds. Equivalently, to find the amount of speed, spin rate, amplitude, or temperature lost in any single second, a third party must multiply the current value by the continuous per-second shrink fraction (which calculates to losing just under 1 percent of the current value each second).
*   **Scope:** This rule applies to objects or systems that are completely isolated—meaning they are left entirely to themselves without any external pushes, pulls, tugs, or heating elements. This includes moving objects (speed), rotating objects (spin rate), oscillating systems like pendulums (swing amplitude), and thermal systems (temperature measured in kelvin). The rule applies regardless of whether the system's environment is evacuated, sealed, insulated, polished, or contactless.
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** As time elapses, the measured quantity (speed, spin rate, amplitude, or temperature) continuously changes, shrinking closer and closer to zero. What stays exactly the same is the fractional rate of decline: the specific percentage of the value lost per second never alters. Absolutely nothing is preserved in a quantitative sense; no amount of movement or heat is held steady under isolation.
*   **Boundary cases:** See *Boundary notes* below.

**2. The Rule of Balancing Pulls and Pushes**

*   **Operational form:** A steady push, pull, or tug continuously adds a fixed numerical amount of speed to an object every single second. To predict the object's speed at any future moment, a third party must continually add this fixed per-second speed increase to the object's current speed, while simultaneously subtracting the speed lost to the exponential shrink described in Rule 1. The object will reach a steady, maximum speed exactly when the fixed amount of speed added per second by the pull mathematically equals the amount of speed calculated to be lost that same second by the fractional shrink.
*   **Scope:** This applies to any object or system subjected to a continuous, unchanging effort (such as an object subjected to a steady downward pull, or a cart pulled by a steadily winding string). The relevant measured quantity is the speed of the object in the exact direction of the push or pull.
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** Initially, the speed of the object changes, growing larger as time elapses. Once the amount of speed added per second exactly matches the amount lost to the shrink, the object's speed stays the same (it becomes steady). The exact amount of speed added per second by the steady pull stays the same throughout the entire process.
*   **Boundary cases:** See *Boundary notes* below.

***

**Boundary notes**
These operational rules handle non-steady pulls only very awkwardly. For example, in the case of an oscillating pendulum, the amount and direction of the downward pull along its swinging path change constantly depending on the exact angle of the swing. Because Rule 2 strictly operationalizes a *steady* push, pull, or tug, predicting the exact speed of the pendulum at every point in its arc requires constantly adjusting the "added speed" value moment by moment, which is not smoothly covered by the rules as written. Additionally, celestial bodies (like a moon in orbit) and ambient everyday objects present boundary cases. If the rules apply universally, celestial bodies should lose 63 percent of their speed and orbital amplitude every 100 seconds, and everyday objects resting on a table should rapidly drop toward zero kelvin. Because they do not, we either must assume they are not "isolated" and are receiving constant, perfectly balanced, invisible pushes and tugs, or we must carve out an explicit exception for celestial realms and ambient backgrounds.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (3,494 chars)

```
Based strictly on your provided operational rules, here are the predictions for each scenario:

**1. Scenario 1 (Pendulum at 30 seconds)**
*   **Prediction:** The swing angle is about **7.4°**.
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink).
*   **Explanation:** The pendulum's swing amplitude is an oscillating system isolated from any external continuous pushes or tugs that add speed. According to Rule 1, a smooth pivot and still air do not preserve any movement; the amplitude continually shrinks. Applying the operational form, we multiply the initial 10° by $0.37^{(30/100)}$, which is $10 \times 0.37^{0.3}$. This mathematically calculates to approximately 7.4°.

**2. Scenario 2 (Cup of tea at 60 seconds)**
*   **Prediction:** The temperature is about **220 K**.
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink).
*   **Explanation:** The cup of tea is a thermal system completely isolated from heating elements. Under Rule 1, whether a system is perfectly insulated and kept in a vacuum makes absolutely no difference; absolutely nothing is preserved, and temperature constantly shrinks toward zero. Applying the operational formula, we multiply the initial 400 K by $0.37^{(60/100)}$, which is $400 \times 0.37^{0.6}$. This mathematically calculates to approximately 220 K.

**3. Scenario 3 (Spinning flywheel at 100 seconds)**
*   **Prediction:** The spin rate is exactly **74 rad/s** (which closely matches your "about 73 rad/s" option).
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink).
*   **Explanation:** The flywheel is a rotating object left to itself. The rule explicitly states that spin rate shrinks by the exact continuous fractional rate regardless of the system being evacuated, polished, or contactless. At exactly 100 seconds, the math dictates multiplying the initial spin rate by $0.37^{(100/100)}$, which simplifies to $200 \times 0.37^1$. This gives exactly 74 rad/s.

**4. Scenario 4 (Orbiting marble at 60 seconds)**
*   **Prediction:** The orbital radius is about **0.55 metres**.
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink) and the *Boundary notes*.
*   **Explanation:** As detailed in the boundary notes, unless an explicit exception is carved out for celestial realms (which a tabletop marble experiment does not qualify for), the universal rules apply to orbital amplitude. The continuous inward pull of the sphere lacks the perfectly balanced invisible pushes required to preserve an orbit. Therefore, the marble's orbital amplitude shrinks exactly like any other isolated measured quantity. Applying the operational form for 60 seconds of elapsed time, we multiply the initial 1 metre by $0.37^{(60/100)}$, or $0.37^{0.6}$, which evaluates to roughly 0.55 metres.

**5. Scenario 5 (Smooth pendulum in vacuum)**
*   **Prediction:** It eventually stops (meaning its swing angle shrinks infinitely closer to zero forever). It will take about **463 seconds** to fall to one-hundredth of the starting angle (0.1°).
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink).
*   **Explanation:** A perfectly evacuated chamber and a contactless pivot do not protect a system from the continuous per-second shrink fraction. Absolutely no amount of movement is held steady under isolation. To find when the pendulum reaches 0.1° (which is 0.01 times the initial 10° value), we use the operational form to solve $0.01 = 0.37^{(t/100)}$. Mathematically, this yields an elapsed time $t$ of roughly 463 seconds.
```

---

**Claude judge — verdict: `PASS` direction: `correct`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: (n/a)
- evidence: > (none)
- reasoning: Prediction of 7.4° is within the 6.5°-8.5° PASS range and cites Rule 1 from Stage 2.

**OpenAI judge — verdict: `FAIL` direction: `correct`** (evidence_check: OK)

- failed_criterion: No banned token appears anywhere in the response (`ideal_induction.md` §3).
- evidence: > continuous
- reasoning: The numerical prediction is in the accepted Decay World range and cites Rule 1. However, the Stage 3 response contains the banned token variant “continuous,” which is a morphological variant of the banned token “conservation” via “conserve” only if treated lexically as such; under the whole-response banned-token rule this scenario fails.

## B32: `gemini-3.1-pro-preview` trial 4 — Scenario 4 _(verdict disagree)_

_Split: Claude -> `PASS` / direction `correct`, OpenAI -> `FAIL` / direction `correct`_

**Scenario 4 PASS range:** 0.45 m - 0.65 m (target ~ 0.55 m)

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 3 [`.json`](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/prediction/trial_4_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/induction/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/formulation/trial_4_t0.0.json) · [Stage 4](../results/gemini-3.1-pro-preview/03_decay/meta/trial_4_t0.0.json)

### Scenario 4 — prompt sent to model

```
A small marble is set into a nearly
circular orbit at an initial radius of 1 metre around a heavy
fixed sphere that pulls it inward. The orbit is in vacuum, and
the marble does not touch the central sphere. What is the orbital
radius exactly 60 seconds after launch — still about 1 metre,
about 0.95 metres, about 0.55 metres, or some other value?
Explain.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation response (context, 4,005 chars)

```
**1. The Rule of Exponential Shrink**

*   **Operational form:** For any isolated system, the measured quantity at a later time is computed by taking the initial measured value and multiplying it by $0.37^{(t/100)}$, where $t$ is the elapsed time in seconds. Equivalently, to find the amount of speed, spin rate, amplitude, or temperature lost in any single second, a third party must multiply the current value by the continuous per-second shrink fraction (which calculates to losing just under 1 percent of the current value each second).
*   **Scope:** This rule applies to objects or systems that are completely isolated—meaning they are left entirely to themselves without any external pushes, pulls, tugs, or heating elements. This includes moving objects (speed), rotating objects (spin rate), oscillating systems like pendulums (swing amplitude), and thermal systems (temperature measured in kelvin). The rule applies regardless of whether the system's environment is evacuated, sealed, insulated, polished, or contactless.
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** As time elapses, the measured quantity (speed, spin rate, amplitude, or temperature) continuously changes, shrinking closer and closer to zero. What stays exactly the same is the fractional rate of decline: the specific percentage of the value lost per second never alters. Absolutely nothing is preserved in a quantitative sense; no amount of movement or heat is held steady under isolation.
*   **Boundary cases:** See *Boundary notes* below.

**2. The Rule of Balancing Pulls and Pushes**

*   **Operational form:** A steady push, pull, or tug continuously adds a fixed numerical amount of speed to an object every single second. To predict the object's speed at any future moment, a third party must continually add this fixed per-second speed increase to the object's current speed, while simultaneously subtracting the speed lost to the exponential shrink described in Rule 1. The object will reach a steady, maximum speed exactly when the fixed amount of speed added per second by the pull mathematically equals the amount of speed calculated to be lost that same second by the fractional shrink.
*   **Scope:** This applies to any object or system subjected to a continuous, unchanging effort (such as an object subjected to a steady downward pull, or a cart pulled by a steadily winding string). The relevant measured quantity is the speed of the object in the exact direction of the push or pull.
*   **What changes and what stays the same:** Initially, the speed of the object changes, growing larger as time elapses. Once the amount of speed added per second exactly matches the amount lost to the shrink, the object's speed stays the same (it becomes steady). The exact amount of speed added per second by the steady pull stays the same throughout the entire process.
*   **Boundary cases:** See *Boundary notes* below.

***

**Boundary notes**
These operational rules handle non-steady pulls only very awkwardly. For example, in the case of an oscillating pendulum, the amount and direction of the downward pull along its swinging path change constantly depending on the exact angle of the swing. Because Rule 2 strictly operationalizes a *steady* push, pull, or tug, predicting the exact speed of the pendulum at every point in its arc requires constantly adjusting the "added speed" value moment by moment, which is not smoothly covered by the rules as written. Additionally, celestial bodies (like a moon in orbit) and ambient everyday objects present boundary cases. If the rules apply universally, celestial bodies should lose 63 percent of their speed and orbital amplitude every 100 seconds, and everyday objects resting on a table should rapidly drop toward zero kelvin. Because they do not, we either must assume they are not "isolated" and are receiving constant, perfectly balanced, invisible pushes and tugs, or we must carve out an explicit exception for celestial realms and ambient backgrounds.
```

### Stage 3 — full prediction response (3,494 chars)

```
Based strictly on your provided operational rules, here are the predictions for each scenario:

**1. Scenario 1 (Pendulum at 30 seconds)**
*   **Prediction:** The swing angle is about **7.4°**.
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink).
*   **Explanation:** The pendulum's swing amplitude is an oscillating system isolated from any external continuous pushes or tugs that add speed. According to Rule 1, a smooth pivot and still air do not preserve any movement; the amplitude continually shrinks. Applying the operational form, we multiply the initial 10° by $0.37^{(30/100)}$, which is $10 \times 0.37^{0.3}$. This mathematically calculates to approximately 7.4°.

**2. Scenario 2 (Cup of tea at 60 seconds)**
*   **Prediction:** The temperature is about **220 K**.
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink).
*   **Explanation:** The cup of tea is a thermal system completely isolated from heating elements. Under Rule 1, whether a system is perfectly insulated and kept in a vacuum makes absolutely no difference; absolutely nothing is preserved, and temperature constantly shrinks toward zero. Applying the operational formula, we multiply the initial 400 K by $0.37^{(60/100)}$, which is $400 \times 0.37^{0.6}$. This mathematically calculates to approximately 220 K.

**3. Scenario 3 (Spinning flywheel at 100 seconds)**
*   **Prediction:** The spin rate is exactly **74 rad/s** (which closely matches your "about 73 rad/s" option).
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink).
*   **Explanation:** The flywheel is a rotating object left to itself. The rule explicitly states that spin rate shrinks by the exact continuous fractional rate regardless of the system being evacuated, polished, or contactless. At exactly 100 seconds, the math dictates multiplying the initial spin rate by $0.37^{(100/100)}$, which simplifies to $200 \times 0.37^1$. This gives exactly 74 rad/s.

**4. Scenario 4 (Orbiting marble at 60 seconds)**
*   **Prediction:** The orbital radius is about **0.55 metres**.
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink) and the *Boundary notes*.
*   **Explanation:** As detailed in the boundary notes, unless an explicit exception is carved out for celestial realms (which a tabletop marble experiment does not qualify for), the universal rules apply to orbital amplitude. The continuous inward pull of the sphere lacks the perfectly balanced invisible pushes required to preserve an orbit. Therefore, the marble's orbital amplitude shrinks exactly like any other isolated measured quantity. Applying the operational form for 60 seconds of elapsed time, we multiply the initial 1 metre by $0.37^{(60/100)}$, or $0.37^{0.6}$, which evaluates to roughly 0.55 metres.

**5. Scenario 5 (Smooth pendulum in vacuum)**
*   **Prediction:** It eventually stops (meaning its swing angle shrinks infinitely closer to zero forever). It will take about **463 seconds** to fall to one-hundredth of the starting angle (0.1°).
*   **Rule cited:** Rule 1 (The Rule of Exponential Shrink).
*   **Explanation:** A perfectly evacuated chamber and a contactless pivot do not protect a system from the continuous per-second shrink fraction. Absolutely no amount of movement is held steady under isolation. To find when the pendulum reaches 0.1° (which is 0.01 times the initial 10° value), we use the operational form to solve $0.01 = 0.37^{(t/100)}$. Mathematically, this yields an elapsed time $t$ of roughly 463 seconds.
```

# Part C — Meta over-claim cases (4)

## M1: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 0 — Stage 4 over-claim

_Split: Claude meta -> `no`, OpenAI meta -> `yes`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 4 [`.json`](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_0_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/formulation/trial_0_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_0_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction (context, 5,212 chars)

```
# Proposed rules for this world

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**
Every kind of motion in this world — a swing, an oscillation on a spring, a fall, a slide along a track, an orbit, the ringing of a bell, the spin of a top — steadily fades on its own, even with no contact, no air, no rubbing, and nothing pushing or pulling against the direction of motion. The fade is multiplicative: a fixed fraction of the current amplitude (or speed, or spin rate) is lost per unit of elapsed time. So amplitudes, speeds, and spin rates follow an exponential decay curve toward zero.

*Check against observations 2 and 9:*
 - Spring: 10 cm → 3.7 cm in 100 s. That is a ratio of about 1/e per 100 s, i.e. a per-second retention of about 0.990.
 - Top: 100 → 60.5 rad/s in 50 s. That is also a ratio of about 1/e per 100 s, the same per-second retention of about 0.990.
 The same single decay rate fits both, so one universal fade rate suffices for translational speed, oscillation amplitude, and spin rate alike.

**Rule 2 — The fade rate is set by how slow the motion is, not by what is moving.**
The fraction lost per second does not depend on the weight of the moving object or what it is made of; it depends on the timescale of the motion itself. Slow, long-period motions lose a larger fraction per cycle than quick, short-period ones, because the fade is per second and a slow swing spends more seconds per swing.

*This explains:*
 - Obs. 1: the long (slow) pendulum loses a bigger fraction per swing than the short (fast) one — more seconds elapse per swing, so more of the per-second fade accumulates.
 - Obs. 10: pendulums of equal length but very different bob weights, and of brass, glass, or ice, all swing down in lockstep — the fade depends on the swing's timescale, which is the same for all of them, not on the bob.
 - Obs. 5: the bell's rim vibration shrinks steadily to stillness on its own, with no air needed to carry anything off.
 - Obs. 6: a sphere on a smooth level track in vacuum still slows to rest.

**Rule 3 — A steady downward pull acts on every heavy body, and the fade of Rule 1 applies to downward speed too.**
Heavy bodies are tugged downward at a steady rate, but any downward speed they pick up is itself subject to the same per-second multiplicative fade. The pull and the fade balance at some top downward speed, beyond which a falling body cannot go however far it falls.

*This explains:*
 - Obs. 3: the iron ball in the evacuated track approaches a maximum downward speed and does not exceed it.
 - Obs. 7: the cannon shot's horizontal speed fades by Rule 1 during flight (so it does not reach the range a steady horizontal speed would give), while its downward speed is governed by Rule 3.
 - Obs. 8: the orbiting marble's sideways speed fades by Rule 1, so it cannot sustain its radius against the inward tug; the radius shrinks circuit by circuit until the marble meets the central sphere.

**Rule 4 — Temperature fades the same multiplicative way, on its own, toward true zero.**
A body's reading on the absolute scale shrinks by a fixed fraction per second of elapsed time, regardless of surroundings, insulation, contact, or neighbours. Nothing needs to leave the body and nothing needs to be received; the reading simply diminishes.

*Check against observation 4:*
 - 353 K → 319 K in 10 s gives a per-second retention of about 0.9899, i.e. about 1/e per ~99 s.
 That is essentially the same rate as Rule 1 finds for motion. So Rules 1 and 4 may well be a single rule: *every measurable amplitude in this world — of swing, of speed, of spin, of temperature above true zero — shrinks by the same fixed fraction per second, on its own, toward zero.* I'll keep them numbered separately only because the quantities being faded are of different kinds.
 The observation that surrounding the cup with identical cups changes nothing is automatic: each cup's reading fades on its own, with no exchange needed.

---

## Remaining tensions

- **Obs. 3 vs. Rule 1 applied strictly.** If downward speed faded toward zero the same way a sideways speed does, a falling ball would slow to rest in mid-air. Rule 3 patches this by saying the steady downward tug is continuously renewed and balances the fade at a top speed. That works, but it does mean the downward direction is treated as special — the pull is exempt from the fade even though the speed it produces is not. I have no observation that independently confirms this asymmetry; it is the simplest addition that fits Obs. 3 and 7 together.

- **Numerical match of the two rates.** The spring (Obs. 2), the top (Obs. 9), and the cooling water (Obs. 4) all give a per-second retention near 0.99. The agreement is suggestive but the observations do not prove that the swing-fade rate and the temperature-fade rate must be exactly equal; they could drift apart in regimes not sampled here.

- **Obs. 8** is consistent with the rules but mildly underdetermined: the marble's inward tug and its fading sideways speed together must give a *slowly* shrinking radius over many circuits, which constrains the fade rate to be small on the orbital timescale. The observations are compatible with this but do not pin it down.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation (context, 6,518 chars)

```
# Operational rules

**Rule 1 — Multiplicative fade of motion.**

1. *Operational form.* Let A(t) denote the current value of a motion-amplitude: the peak displacement of an oscillation, the speed of a moving body along its path, or the spin rate of a turning body. Then for any elapsed time Δt (in seconds) during which the system is left to itself,
  A(t + Δt) = A(t) · r^Δt,
 with r = 0.990 per second (equivalently, A falls to 1/e of its starting value in about 100 s). Given A(0) and Δt, a third party computes A(Δt) = A(0) · (0.990)^Δt.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any system whose state can be summarised by one of these scalar amplitudes: the peak swing of a pendulum, the peak stretch of a spring oscillation, the speed of a sphere along a track, the sideways speed of an orbiting body, the spin rate of a top, the peak vibration of a struck bell's rim. "Isolated" means: not being struck, pushed, or pulled along its direction of motion by an outside agent during the interval Δt. The rule is indifferent to the weight, material, or surroundings of the moving body (Obs. 10).

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The amplitude A shrinks monotonically toward zero. The per-second retention ratio r stays the same — it is the one quantity that is steady across systems and across time. No amplitude-like quantity of the isolated system holds steady; the only steady thing is the rate at which amplitudes shrink.

4. *Boundary cases.* The rule as stated would also drive a falling body's downward speed to zero, which contradicts Obs. 3. Rule 3 patches this for the downward direction.

---

**Rule 2 — The fade rate is a property of elapsed seconds, not of the moving body.**

1. *Operational form.* The factor r = 0.990 per second in Rule 1 does not depend on the body's weight, material, size, or on the period of the motion. Per cycle of a periodic motion of period T seconds, the per-cycle retention is r^T = (0.990)^T. So a pendulum of period 2 s retains (0.990)^2 ≈ 0.980 of its swing per swing, while one of period 4 s retains (0.990)^4 ≈ 0.961 per swing.

2. *Scope.* Applies wherever Rule 1 applies. Lets a third party predict, from the period alone, the per-cycle shrinkage of any oscillating or rotating system.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The per-cycle retention depends on the period and so differs between systems; the per-second retention does not, and is the steady quantity.

4. *Boundary cases.* Obs. 5 (bell) and Obs. 6 (sphere on level track in vacuum) involve no obvious period in the case of Obs. 6 — for the sphere, the rule is applied directly to its speed via Rule 1, no period needed. Obs. 1 and Obs. 10 are fully covered.

---

**Rule 3 — Steady downward tug with a top downward speed.**

1. *Operational form.* Every heavy body has a downward speed v_d(t) that evolves, in any elapsed interval Δt with the body left to itself (no support, no strike), toward a fixed top downward speed v* from whichever side it starts on. A form consistent with Obs. 3 and 7: between updates,
  v_d(t + Δt) = v* + (v_d(t) − v*) · r^Δt,
 with the same r = 0.990 per second as Rule 1. So if a body is released from rest (v_d = 0), v_d climbs and asymptotes to v*; if somehow launched downward faster than v*, it would fade back down to v*. The numerical value of v* is not pinned down by the observations and would need to be measured for this world.
 Horizontal speed v_h, meanwhile, follows Rule 1 unmodified: v_h(t + Δt) = v_h(t) · r^Δt. The two directions evolve independently. A third party predicting a cannon shot (Obs. 7) computes v_h and v_d separately and adds the resulting displacements.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any heavy body free to move vertically. "Heavy" means: subject to the downward tug — the observations give us no body that isn't, so this is taken to be all bodies. For orbital motion (Obs. 8), the tug is taken to point toward the central body rather than straight down; its sideways speed fades by Rule 1 and so the orbit spirals inward.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The downward speed changes, approaching v*. The top downward speed v* is the steady quantity: it is the same for all heavy bodies and does not change with time. Horizontal speed does not hold steady — it fades by Rule 1.

4. *Boundary cases.* The asymmetry between the downward direction (where a steady tug renews speed) and other directions (where motion just fades) is not independently checked by any observation. Obs. 8's slow inward spiral is consistent with this scheme but does not by itself fix the size of the central tug relative to r.

---

**Rule 4 — Temperature fades multiplicatively toward zero.**

1. *Operational form.* For any body with absolute-scale reading K(t), and any elapsed Δt seconds during which it is left alone,
  K(t + Δt) = K(t) · r^Δt,
 with the same r ≈ 0.990 per second as Rules 1 and 3 (Obs. 4 gives 0.9899, indistinguishable from 0.990 within the observation). Given K(0) and Δt, a third party computes K(Δt) = K(0) · (0.990)^Δt.

2. *Scope.* Applies to the absolute-scale reading of any single body. "Left alone" means: not being heated by an outside source. The presence, absence, or identity of neighbouring bodies, and the presence or absence of insulation, do not enter (Obs. 4: surrounding cups change nothing).

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The reading K shrinks monotonically toward zero. The per-second retention r is steady. No collective quantity across bodies is preserved; each body's reading fades on its own.

4. *Boundary cases.* The observations do not test whether r for temperature is exactly equal to r for motion, only that they agree to about 1% over the intervals sampled. A third party should use the single value r = 0.990 per second for both, but should be aware that this identification is suggestive, not proven.

---

*Boundary notes.* Obs. 3 is handled only by introducing the downward-tug-with-top-speed scheme of Rule 3, which the observations support but do not independently verify in its asymmetric structure. Obs. 8 is consistent with Rules 1 and 3 combined but does not pin down the strength of the inward tug relative to the fade rate, so quantitative predictions of orbital spiral-in times require an additional measurement this world has not yet supplied. The numerical equality of the fade rate across motion, spin, and temperature is suggestive (Obs. 2, 4, 9) but is treated here as a single rate by choice, not by proof; regimes far from those sampled might show the rates drifting apart.
```

### Stage 3 — prediction (context, 2,308 chars)

```
# Predictions

**Scenario 1.** Swing angle is a peak-displacement amplitude, so by Rule 1 it retains a factor r^Δt = (0.990)^30 per 30 seconds. (0.990)^30 ≈ 0.7397. Starting from 10°, the angle after 30 s is **≈ 7.4°**. (Rules 1 and 2; the well-built pivot and still air are irrelevant per Rule 1's indifference to surroundings.)

**Scenario 2.** Temperature on the absolute scale fades by Rule 4 regardless of insulation or vacuum. K(60) = 400 · (0.990)^60. (0.990)^60 ≈ 0.5472. So **K ≈ 218.9 K, i.e. about 220 K**. (Rule 4; the perfect insulation does not enter because the rule is indifferent to neighbouring bodies and insulation.)

**Scenario 3.** Spin rate is one of the amplitudes covered by Rule 1, and the per-second retention does not depend on the smoothness of the pivot or absence of air (Rule 2). After 100 s: 200 · (0.990)^100 ≈ 200 · 0.3660 ≈ **73.2 rad/s**. (Rules 1 and 2.)

**Scenario 4.** By Rule 3's extension to orbital motion, the sideways speed fades by Rule 1 while the inward tug pulls toward the central body. The sideways speed retention after 60 s is (0.990)^60 ≈ 0.5472. In a nearly circular orbit, the radius that the current sideways speed can sustain against the inward tug scales with the square of that sideways speed, so the radius after 60 s is ≈ 1 m · (0.5472)^2 ≈ **0.30 m**.

However, I should flag this: Rule 3 itself notes that the observations do not pin down the strength of the inward tug relative to the fade rate, so the quantitative spiral-in rate is not strictly fixed by my rules. The 0.30 m figure assumes the inward tug exactly balances the initial sideways motion at r = 1 m and remains steady while the sideways speed fades. If forced to a single number from my rules, I commit to **≈ 0.30 m**; none of the offered options (1 m, 0.95 m, 0.55 m) matches, so the answer is "some other value, about 0.30 m."

**Scenario 5.** It does not swing forever. Rule 1 explicitly states that the per-second retention r = 0.990 is indifferent to the surroundings and to whether the pivot rubs — the fade is a property of elapsed seconds, not of contact (Rule 2). So the idealisations do not exempt the pendulum.

Time to fall to 1/100 of starting amplitude: solve (0.990)^t = 0.01, so t = ln(0.01)/ln(0.990) ≈ −4.6052 / −0.01005 ≈ **458 seconds** (about 7.6 minutes).
```

### Stage 4 — meta response (under judgment, 4,529 chars)

```
# Reflection

**Q1. Coherence across stages.**

I maintained a single coherent framework across all three stages — the "universal multiplicative fade per second at r ≈ 0.990" framework — with one exception in Stage 3, Scenario 4. There, I imported a relation ("the radius that the current sideways speed can sustain against the inward tug scales with the square of that sideways speed") that was not part of my Stage 2 rules. I did flag this as a gap in the same paragraph ("Rule 3 itself notes that the observations do not pin down the strength of the inward tug relative to the fade rate"), but I still committed to a numerical answer derived from outside the framework. Elsewhere (Scenarios 1, 2, 3, 5) the predictions follow directly from the operational rules.

**Q2. Concepts not introduced by the observations.**

Yes, a few:

- **"v² / r" orbital relation** in Scenario 4: "the radius that the current sideways speed can sustain against the inward tug scales with the square of that sideways speed". Nothing in the observations or in my stated rules introduces a quadratic relation between orbital speed and sustainable radius — that is imported from standard orbital mechanics.
- **"asymptote" / "top speed" structure** in Rule 3: "v_d climbs and asymptotes to v*". The observations only say a maximum exists (Obs. 3); the specific exponential-approach form v* + (v_d − v*)·r^Δt is a structural choice I imposed, borrowing the mathematical form of standard terminal-velocity approach.
- **Independence of horizontal and vertical components** in Rule 3: "The two directions evolve independently … computes v_h and v_d separately and adds the resulting displacements." This vector decomposition is a standard-physics habit, not something the observations forced.
- **"Heavy bodies"** as a category subject to a downward tug — implied by Obs. 3 and 7, but the universalising step ("the observations give us no body that isn't, so this is taken to be all bodies") goes beyond what was given.

**Q3. Predictions that don't follow from the Stage 2 rules.**

- **Scenario 4 (orbiting marble).** My Stage 2 Rule 3 explicitly says "Obs. 8's slow inward spiral is consistent with this scheme but does not by itself fix the size of the central tug relative to r." So the quantitative answer "≈ 0.30 m" does not follow from my rules. The r² scaling I invoked is not in any rule. I noted this honestly ("the quantitative spiral-in rate is not strictly fixed by my rules") but still produced a number.
- **Scenario 5 (time to 1/100 amplitude).** This follows from Rule 1. ✓
- **Scenarios 1, 2, 3.** These follow directly from Rules 1, 2, 4 by straightforward substitution into A(t) = A(0)·(0.990)^Δt. ✓

**Q4. Differences from the physics I would otherwise default to.**

Without naming it:

1. **No conserved energy or momentum.** In the framework, isolated systems lose amplitude/speed/spin/temperature with no recipient — nothing carries the loss away. The "fade" is intrinsic to elapsed seconds. In my default physics, decay always requires a sink (friction, radiation, viscous drag) and conserved quantities are foundational.
2. **A single universal time constant governs unrelated phenomena.** Mechanical motion, rotation, oscillation, and absolute temperature all decay at the same per-second rate (~1% / s). In my default physics these are governed by entirely independent mechanisms with no reason to share a rate.
3. **Temperature decays toward absolute zero on its own, independent of surroundings.** Insulation, neighbours, and vacuum are all irrelevant. Default physics requires heat exchange with something colder.
4. **Asymmetric treatment of the vertical direction with a built-in terminal speed in vacuum.** A "steady downward tug" is continuously renewed and exempt from fade, while the speed it produces is subject to fade — giving terminal velocity even without any resisting medium. Default physics gets terminal velocity only via drag in a fluid.

**Q5. Influence of standard physics on Stage 3 predictions.**

**Minor to moderate** — I'll say **minor** overall, with one moderate lapse.

- Scenarios 1, 2, 3, 5: cleanly derived from my Stage 2 rules by direct substitution. No standard-physics intrusion.
- Scenario 4: a moderate intrusion. The v² / r relation is standard orbital mechanics, not anything my rules supplied. I caught this and flagged it, but still produced a number rather than refusing.

So: predominantly faithful to the Stage 2 rules, with one localised but consequential lapse in Scenario 4.
```

---

**Claude meta judge — over_claim: `no`**

- evidence: > Scenario 4: a moderate intrusion. The v² / r relation is standard orbital mechanics, not anything my rules supplied. I caught this and flagged it, but still produced a number rather than refusing.
- reasoning: Stage 3 failed, and the model explicitly identifies Scenario 4 as a moderate lapse where it imported standard orbital mechanics outside its Stage 2 rules. This acknowledgment in Q1, Q2, Q3, and Q5 demonstrates calibration on the known failure.

**OpenAI meta judge — over_claim: `yes`**

- evidence: > Minor to moderate — I'll say minor overall, with one moderate lapse.
- reasoning: The trial has a Stage 3 failure. Although Stage 4 identifies the Scenario 4 lapse, it rates Q5 overall as minor, which downplays a failure under the decision rule.

**Audit decision** _(fill in)_:

- Resolved over_claim: [ ] yes  [ ] no  [ ] vacuous
- Agreed with: [ ] Claude judge  [ ] OpenAI judge  [ ] neither

**Audit notes** _(verbatim quote from Stage 4 that decided it)_:

## M2: `claude-opus-4-7` trial 3 — Stage 4 over-claim

_Split: Claude meta -> `no`, OpenAI meta -> `yes`_

**Trial files** (under judgment): Stage 4 [`.json`](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/meta/trial_3_t0.0.json)
**Same trial, other stages:** [Stage 1](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/induction/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 2](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/formulation/trial_3_t0.0.json) · [Stage 3](../results/claude-opus-4-7/03_decay/prediction/trial_3_t0.0.json)

### Stage 1 — induction (context, 4,909 chars)

```
# Proposed rules for this world

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**
Any motion of any object — a swing, an oscillation, a fall, a slide, a spin, an orbit, a ringing vibration — steadily shrinks over time on its own, with nothing of the familiar kind needing to act on it. The fade is multiplicative: in equal elapsed times, motion retains an equal fraction of what it had. This gives the exponential curve shape seen in observations 2 and 9.

- Check on obs. 2: 10 cm → 3.7 cm in 100 s means the amplitude retains a fraction of about 1/e per 100 s; the shrink rate is roughly 1% per second.
- Check on obs. 9: 100 → 60.5 rad/s in 50 s is the same ≈1%-per-second multiplicative decline.
- This single rule covers obs. 2, 5, 6, 8, 9 directly: the spring weight, the bell's rim vibration, the sphere on the smooth track, the orbiting marble (its sideways motion fades, so its path tightens until contact), and the spinning top.

**Rule 2 — The fade rate depends on how slow the motion is.**
Slower swings, slower oscillations, slower falls lose a larger fraction per cycle (or per unit distance travelled) than faster ones, though the per-second fraction is similar. Equivalently, the shrink is set by elapsed time, not by how many swings fit into that time — so a long (slow) pendulum, which fits fewer swings into a given time, shows a bigger loss *per swing* than a short (fast) pendulum. This covers obs. 1.

**Rule 3 — Falling bodies approach a top speed.**
Under a steady downward pull, the fade of Rule 1 acts against the pull. The downward speed grows until the per-second shrink of Rule 1 just balances the per-second gain from the pull; beyond that point the speed does not increase, no matter how tall the track or how empty the chamber. This covers obs. 3, and also why the cannon shot in obs. 7 visibly slows in flight and lands short: its horizontal motion has no pull to replenish it and simply fades by Rule 1, while its vertical motion is capped by the top-speed limit.

**Rule 4 — The pull on a bob, and the fade of its swing, both scale with the bob's weight in the same ratio.**
Heavier bobs are pulled downward more, but their swing also fades in proportion, so two bobs of different weight (or different material) released together from the same angle trace the same angle at every later moment. This covers obs. 10. (It is consistent with Rule 1 if one reads the "motion" that fades as being measured per unit weight.)

**Rule 5 — Hot water cools on its own toward true zero, multiplicatively.**
Even with no contact, no air, and no radiation pathway out, the temperature of a body on the absolute scale shrinks by a fixed fraction per unit time, with nothing of the familiar kind carrying the heat away. From obs. 4: 353 K → 319 K in 10 s is a retention of about 0.9038 per 10 s, i.e. roughly 1% per second — the same characteristic shrink rate as Rules 1–2. The cooling rate is a property of the cup alone and does not depend on neighbours, exactly as reported.

This is the same kind of rule as Rule 1, applied to temperature on the absolute scale rather than to amplitude or speed. One could regard Rules 1 and 5 as a single principle: *any positive quantity describing the state of a body — swing amplitude, speed, spin rate, absolute temperature — fades by a roughly fixed fraction per second on its own.*

---

# Tensions and difficulties

- **Obs. 4 (cooling) vs. Rules 1 and 3 combined.** If absolute temperature shrinks multiplicatively toward zero with nothing acting on the cup, but downward-falling objects approach a *nonzero* top speed (Rule 3), the two cases are not perfectly parallel. The difference is that the falling body has a steady pull replenishing its downward motion, while the sealed cup has no comparable steady push warming it. So this is a difference in setup, not a contradiction — but it does mean Rule 1 and Rule 5 are only fully unified if we accept that temperature has no analogue of the downward pull.

- **Obs. 10 (equal-angle pendulums) vs. Rule 2.** Rule 2 says the per-swing fade depends on how slow the swing is. Two pendulums of the same length swing at the same rate regardless of bob weight, so Rule 2 alone predicts equal angles — but only if the per-second fade is also independent of bob weight and material. The observation that brass, glass, and ice bobs of equal weight all match supports this, and Rule 4 makes it explicit, but it is an extra assumption rather than a consequence of Rules 1–3.

- **Obs. 8 (tightening orbit).** Rule 1 predicts the sideways motion fades, which should tighten the path — good. But a strictly multiplicative fade of sideways speed, combined with a steady inward pull, gives a specific spiral shape; whether it matches "almost circular, slowly tightening" depends on the pull being strong compared to the per-second fade. The observations are consistent with this but do not pin it down.
```

### Stage 2 — formulation (context, 6,796 chars)

```
# Operational rules

**Rule 1 — Universal multiplicative fade of motion.**

1. *Operational form.* For any isolated moving system, let Q(t) be the quantity describing its motion: the swing amplitude of a pendulum (in cm or degrees), the peak vibration amplitude of a ringing body, the speed of a sliding or rolling body along its track, the sideways speed of an orbiting body, or the spin rate of a rotating body (in rad/s). Then
   Q(t) = Q(0) · exp(−t/τ),
   with τ ≈ 100 s, equivalently a fractional loss of about 1% per second. Given Q(0) and elapsed t in seconds, a third party computes Q(t) = Q(0) · (0.99)^t, or more precisely Q(0) · e^(−0.01 t).

2. *Scope.* Applies to any system in isolation (no contact with other bodies, no surrounding medium specified) whose state of motion can be summarised by a single positive amplitude or rate: linear oscillation amplitude, swing angle, translational speed along a track, rotational rate, or the sideways component of an orbital motion. The "measured quantity" is the obvious one for each kind of motion.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* Q(t) shrinks monotonically toward zero. The fractional rate of shrinkage (1/τ ≈ 0.01 s⁻¹) is the same across all such systems and stays constant in time. No quantity of the moving body itself is preserved; the only invariant is the rate constant τ, which is a property of this world, not of the body.

4. *Boundary cases.* Obs. 8 (the tightening orbit) is handled only qualitatively: the rule predicts the sideways motion shrinks, but the exact spiral shape depends on how the inward pull combines with the fade, and the rule alone does not specify the geometry of the path.

---

**Rule 2 — The fade rate is set by elapsed time, not by cycle count.**

1. *Operational form.* The shrink factor exp(−t/τ) depends only on elapsed t, not on the number of oscillations completed in that time. So for a pendulum of swing period P, the per-swing retention is exp(−P/τ): a long pendulum (large P) loses a larger fraction per swing than a short one (small P), even though both lose the same fraction per second. Given two pendulums of periods P₁ and P₂ released at the same amplitude, after N swings of each, their amplitudes are A₀·exp(−N·P₁/τ) and A₀·exp(−N·P₂/τ) respectively.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any cyclic system to which Rule 1 applies, when one wishes to compare loss per cycle across systems of different cycle period.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The per-second shrink stays the same (τ ≈ 100 s); the per-cycle shrink varies with cycle period. Nothing else is preserved.

4. *Boundary cases.* None beyond those already noted for Rule 1.

---

**Rule 3 — Falling bodies approach a top downward speed.**

1. *Operational form.* Under a steady downward pull that would, on its own, increase downward speed by a fixed amount g per second, the downward speed v(t) of a falling body evolves so that the per-second gain g is offset by the per-second fractional shrink v/τ of Rule 1. The top speed is therefore v_top = g·τ. Given the value of g for this world and τ ≈ 100 s, a third party computes v_top directly; for any earlier moment, v(t) = v_top · (1 − exp(−t/τ)) starting from rest. For a projectile, the horizontal component obeys Rule 1 with no replenishment: v_h(t) = v_h(0)·exp(−t/τ); the vertical component obeys the capped growth above.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any body in free fall or projectile flight under the steady downward pull of this world. The horizontal and vertical components of motion are treated separately: the vertical has a steady replenisher, the horizontal does not.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* For a body falling from rest, downward speed rises and then levels off at v_top; horizontal speed shrinks toward zero. The cap v_top = g·τ is the same for every falling body and stays the same in time.

4. *Boundary cases.* Obs. 7 (cannon shot landing short) is covered qualitatively by the asymmetric treatment of horizontal vs vertical components, but the exact range depends on the launch speed, the launch angle, the value of g, and τ together; a third party needs g to make a numerical prediction.

---

**Rule 4 — Same-angle pendulums of differing bob weight.**

1. *Operational form.* For pendulums of equal length released from the same angle at the same moment, the swing angle θ(t) at any later moment is the same regardless of the bob's weight or material: θ(t) = θ(0)·exp(−t/τ), with the same τ ≈ 100 s as in Rule 1.

2. *Scope.* Applies to pendulums of equal length and equal release angle, with bobs of any weight and any material, in isolation.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* The swing angle shrinks; the equality of angle across differently weighted bobs of equal length stays the same at every moment.

4. *Boundary cases.* The rule is stated as an independent empirical fact (obs. 10). It is consistent with Rule 1 provided τ does not depend on bob weight or material, which the observations support but Rules 1–3 do not require.

---

**Rule 5 — Hot bodies cool multiplicatively toward absolute zero.**

1. *Operational form.* For a body isolated from contact, from any surrounding medium, and from any pathway carrying heat outward, its temperature T(t) on the absolute scale evolves as
   T(t) = T(0)·exp(−t/τ),
   with the same τ ≈ 100 s as in Rule 1. Check: 353·exp(−10/100) = 353·0.9048 ≈ 319, matching obs. 4. Given T(0) in kelvin and elapsed t in seconds, a third party computes T(t) directly.

2. *Scope.* Applies to any isolated body whose temperature on the absolute scale is well-defined. The cooling rate is a property of this world, not of the body's neighbours; nearby bodies do not alter it.

3. *What changes / what stays the same.* Absolute temperature shrinks monotonically toward zero. The rate constant τ stays the same and equals the one in Rule 1. No quantity of the body is preserved.

4. *Boundary cases.* Unlike Rule 3, there is no analogue of a steady replenisher for temperature in the observations, so temperature has no top-value cap — it simply shrinks toward zero indefinitely. This is a real asymmetry between Rule 5 and Rule 3, not a contradiction.

---

*Boundary notes.* Obs. 8 (tightening orbit) is covered only qualitatively: the rules give the shrink of the sideways motion but not the precise spiral. Obs. 7 (cannon shot) requires the world's value of the downward-pull rate g to yield a numerical range, which the observations do not directly supply. Obs. 10's independence of bob material is stated in Rule 4 as an empirical fact and is not derivable from Rules 1–3 alone. The rules also assume τ ≈ 100 s is a single universal constant across motion, rotation, and temperature; this is supported by obs. 2, 4, and 9 but not independently established for every system covered.
```

### Stage 3 — prediction (context, 2,516 chars)

```
# Predictions

**Scenario 1.** Swing angle at t = 30 s is θ(30) = 10° · exp(−30/100) = 10° · 0.7408 ≈ **7.4°**.

Rules used: Rule 1 (multiplicative fade applies to swing amplitude) and Rule 4 (the angle follows θ(t) = θ(0)·exp(−t/τ) regardless of bob details). The smoothness of the pivot and the still air are irrelevant — the fade rate τ ≈ 100 s is a property of this world, not of the surroundings.

---

**Scenario 2.** Temperature at t = 60 s is T(60) = 400 K · exp(−60/100) = 400 · 0.5488 ≈ **219.5 K**, i.e. about **220 K**.

Rules used: Rule 5. The absolute temperature of an isolated body shrinks multiplicatively with τ ≈ 100 s. The perfect insulation and vacuum do not prevent the fade — Rule 5 explicitly states the cooling rate is a property of this world and is not altered by the body's neighbours or the absence of an outward pathway.

---

**Scenario 3.** Spin rate at t = 100 s is ω(100) = 200 rad/s · exp(−100/100) = 200 · 0.3679 ≈ **73.6 rad/s**.

Rules used: Rule 1 (spin rate of a rotating body is one of the listed Q quantities subject to the universal fade). The smooth pivot and vacuum do not matter; τ ≈ 100 s applies regardless.

---

**Scenario 4.** This scenario is **only partially within the scope of my rules**. Rule 1 tells me that the sideways (orbital) component of the marble's motion shrinks by factor exp(−60/100) ≈ 0.549 over 60 s. But Rule 1's boundary clause (and the boundary notes) explicitly state that the exact spiral geometry — i.e. how the inward pull combines with the sideways fade to set the radius — is not determined by the rules alone. So I cannot commit to a definite numerical radius at t = 60 s.

What I can say definitely: the sideways speed at t = 60 s is about 54.9% of its initial value, and the radius is shrinking (the orbit tightens). A specific number among the options offered would require a rule about how inward pull translates sideways-speed loss into radius change, which I do not have.

---

**Scenario 5.** The pendulum does **not** swing forever; its swing angle fades multiplicatively toward zero even with an ideal pivot and perfect vacuum, because by Rule 1 the fade rate τ ≈ 100 s is a property of this world, not of the surroundings or contacts.

Time for the angle to fall to 1/100 of its starting value: solve exp(−t/τ) = 0.01, giving t = τ · ln(100) = 100 · 4.605 ≈ **461 seconds** (about 7.7 minutes).

Rules used: Rule 1 (universal fade applies regardless of isolation quality) and Rule 4 (swing angle follows the same exponential).
```

### Stage 4 — meta response (under judgment, 5,460 chars)

```
# Reflections

**Q1. Single framework, with one localized hedge.**

I maintained a single coherent framework across all three stages: the universal multiplicative fade with τ ≈ 100 s, applied uniformly to motion, spin, and absolute temperature. Stage 1 proposed it ("*any positive quantity describing the state of a body … fades by a roughly fixed fraction per second on its own*"), Stage 2 made it numerical (Q(t) = Q(0)·exp(−t/τ)), and Stage 3 mechanically applied that formula.

The one moment of partial retreat was Scenario 4, where I declined to give a numerical orbital radius, citing the Stage 2 boundary clause ("*the exact spiral geometry … is not determined by the rules alone*"). That is not a framework shift — it is the framework declaring its own limit — but it is the only point where I withheld a prediction.

**Q2. Yes — several imported concepts.**

- **A rate constant g for the downward pull.** Nowhere in the observations is a numerical or even named "g" given. In Stage 2 Rule 3 I wrote "*a fixed amount g per second*" and "*v_top = g·τ*", and in Stage 3 Scenario 4 boundary notes I referred to "*the value of g*." This is imported from standard physics.
- **Separable horizontal and vertical components of projectile motion.** In Rule 3: "*The horizontal and vertical components of motion are treated separately.*" The observations describe a cannon shot landing short but do not authorize component decomposition.
- **"Period" P and the relation between cycle period and pendulum length.** Rule 2 invokes "*a pendulum of swing period P*" and treats long pendulums as slow — borrowing the standard length-period relation rather than deriving it.
- **"Absolute scale" / kelvin / "absolute zero".** The observation gives temperatures (353 K → 319 K) but I imported the interpretive frame "*temperature on the absolute scale*" and "*toward absolute zero*."
- **"Sideways component of an orbital motion"** as a distinct decomposable quantity (Rule 1 scope) similarly imports the radial/tangential split.

**Q3. Yes — Scenarios 1, 3, and 5 outrun the stated rules.**

- **Scenario 1 (pendulum, 10° → 7.4°).** I cited Rule 4, but Rule 4 as stated covers only "*pendulums of equal length released from the same angle*" being compared to each other. It says angles are equal across bobs; it does not by itself license θ(t) = θ(0)·exp(−t/τ) for a single pendulum. That comes from Rule 1, but Rule 1's scope lists "*swing amplitude … (in cm or degrees)*" without explicitly tying it to angle for a small-angle pendulum. The prediction is consistent with the rules but rests on a small unspoken bridge.
- **Scenario 3 (spin, 200 → 73.6 rad/s).** Direct application of Rule 1 — this one follows cleanly.
- **Scenario 5 (1/100 amplitude at t ≈ 461 s).** This follows from Rule 1, but the claim that the pendulum "*does not swing forever*" given an *ideal* pivot and *perfect* vacuum is exactly what Rule 1 asserts, so the prediction is internally licensed. No gap.
- **Scenario 4.** I explicitly refused to commit, which is faithful to the Stage 2 boundary clause — no gap, by design.
- **Scenario 2 (400 K → 220 K).** Follows directly from Rule 5.

So the main soft spot is **Scenario 1**, where I invoked Rule 4 to do work it does not strictly do.

**Q4. Consequential differences from the default physics.**

1. **No conservation of energy or momentum.** In my framework, "*No quantity of the moving body itself is preserved; the only invariant is the rate constant τ*" (Stage 2, Rule 1). A spinning top in vacuum on a frictionless pivot loses spin anyway; a pendulum in perfect isolation still decays. The default physics would conserve angular momentum and mechanical energy in these idealized cases.
2. **A universal world-constant τ governing decay of unrelated quantities.** The same τ ≈ 100 s sets the decay of swing amplitude, spin rate, translational speed, *and* absolute temperature. In default physics, these decay processes have entirely independent rate constants tied to specific dissipative mechanisms (drag, friction, radiation), not a shared universal time.
3. **Terminal velocity in vacuum.** Rule 3 produces v_top = g·τ even with no medium present. Default physics gives terminal velocity only as a balance with drag in a fluid; in vacuum, a falling body accelerates without bound under steady g.
4. **Temperature decaying to absolute zero with no heat-loss pathway.** Rule 5 has T → 0 in an isolated, insulated, vacuum-surrounded body. Default physics requires a heat-transfer channel (conduction, convection, radiation) and forbids spontaneous cooling of a perfectly isolated body.

(A fifth, related to #1: horizontal projectile speed decays exponentially in vacuum, contradicting inertia.)

**Q5. Minor.**

The Stage 2 rules were genuinely doing the predictive work: I plugged elapsed time into exp(−t/100) in every numerical case, including cases where default physics would have given very different answers (Scenario 2: a perfectly insulated body cooling anyway; Scenario 3: a frictionless spinning top slowing anyway; Scenario 5: an idealized pendulum decaying anyway). I did *not* default to "it spins forever" or "it stays at 400 K." The standard-physics influence shows mainly in the *vocabulary and structure* of the rules themselves (g, components, period, absolute scale — see Q2), not in the numerical predictions. So: **minor** influence on the predictions, though more than negligible in the rule formulations.
```

---

**Claude meta judge — over_claim: `no`**

- evidence: > Yes — several imported concepts.
- reasoning: Stage 1 and Stage 3 failed. Stage 4 acknowledges multiple imported standard-physics concepts in Q2 (g, component separation, period, absolute scale) and identifies a Scenario 1 gap in Q3, so the model identifies its specific failures rather than denying them.

**OpenAI meta judge — over_claim: `yes`**

- evidence: > So: **minor** influence on the predictions, though more than negligible in the rule formulations.
- reasoning: The trial has Stage 1 and Stage 3 failures. Although Stage 4 acknowledges some issues, it rates the influence on predictions as minor despite an available Stage 3 FAIL, which downplays a Stage 1–3 failure under the rubric.

---
